The Particle 'Aw' in Negation: Generalization Rule
Rule that 'aw' generalizes in negation like an indefinite noun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'aw' generalizes in negation to determine the scope of a negated disjunction in legal statements.

Core Idea
In negation, 'aw' generalizes over all alternatives, like an indefinite noun, negating each one.

The particle 'aw' (or) is a common linguistic tool in legal Arabic. Its effect depends on whether it appears in an affirmative or negative context. In affirmation, it indicates one of two possibilities; in negation, it takes on a generalizing force, akin to the indefinite noun (nakira) in negative sentences. This short lesson anchors that key principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Determine whether the sentence containing 'aw' is affirmative or negative. The ruling changes based on this.
In usul, the particle's meaning is context-dependent.
2
Recall the affirmation rule
In affirmation, 'aw' indicates that the ruling applies to only one of the mentioned alternatives (e.g., 'I will give Zaid or Amr a dirham' means only one gets it).
This is the default, non-generalizing meaning.
3
Apply the negation rule
In negation, 'aw' generalizes: negating 'A or B' negates both A and B. The justification is that negation of an ambiguous item (mubham) is not certain unless all individuals are excluded.
Analogy to the indefinite noun in negation (al-nakira).
4
Contrast with other particles
Note that 'aw' in negation behaves like 'waw' (and) in some contexts, but the Hanafi rule is specific to 'aw' by analogy to nakira.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (أو لأحد الأمرين أي لواحد من الأمرين فيعم في النفي دون الإثبات كالنكرة) فإنه في المعنى مثلها فأعطي حكمها، فإن نفي المبهم لا يكون إلا بنفي جميع الافراد عرفاً، وإن جاز
Issue: 'Aw' (or) is for one of two matters, i.e., for a single one of the matters. So it generalizes in negation but not in affirmation, like the indefinite noun. For it is in meaning similar to it, so it is given its ruling. Indeed, negation of an ambiguous [item] is not achieved except by negating all individuals conventionally, even if it is permissible...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This is a usul rule about the particle 'aw' (أو). In affirmation, 'aw' implies one of the alternatives, not all. However, in negation, it generalizes, meaning that negating 'either A or B' negates both A and B, similar to the indefinite noun in negation (e.g., 'No man came' negates all men). The justification is that negation of an ambiguous or indefinite thing cannot be certain unless all possible individuals are negated. This rule is important in understanding legal statements, especially in contracts and oaths.

Key Terms
أو (aw): A particle that signifies one of two or more matters; in affirmation it is for one, in negation it generalizes.
النكرة (al-nakira): An indefinite noun; in negation it implies universality (all individuals).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Analogy used to justify the generalization of 'aw' in negation?
A) The particle 'waw' (and) in negation
B) The indefinite noun (nakira) in negation
C) The definite noun (ma'rifa) in affirmation
D) The particle 'bal' (but) in exclusion

Key Takeaway
In negation, 'aw' takes the ruling of the indefinite noun, negating all alternatives, while in affirmation it indicates one only.

Negation of Qualified Act: The Hanafi Rule
Analyzing the effect of 'لا' and 'لكن' on ijaza of a fuduli marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that negation of a qualified act (nafy al-muqayyad) negates both the qualification and the act itself, and distinguish it from mere objection to a condition.

Core Idea
Negation of the qualified contract negates the entire contract, not just the condition.

In Hanafi usul, when someone says 'I do not authorize it for one hundred but for two hundred,' the negation ('la') attaches to the entire qualified act (the marriage with a dowry of one hundred). The act is void, and the 'but' does not save it—rather, it requires a new contract. This passage examines the linguistic principles behind this rule and responds to alternative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic structure
The statement uses 'la' (negation) and 'lakin' (correction/istidrak). Determine whether the negation applies to the entire qualified entity or only to the qualification (qayd).
The particle 'lam' here refers to the specific pact (al-'ahd).
2
Apply the Hanafi principle of nafy al-muqayyad
In Hanafi usul, negation of the muqayyad (qualified act) negates both the asl (act itself) and the qayd. The marriage with a dowry of one hundred is a single unit; rejecting ijaza voids the entire unit.
The muqayyad is the object of ijaza.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
Some argue that negation targets only the qayd (dowry amount), meaning the marriage itself remains but with a different condition. The passage rebuts this because the muqayyad is what depends on ijaza, and if ijaza is denied, the muqayyad falls.
The alternative would require a separate contract.
4
Analyze the role of 'lakin' (istidrak)
The 'lakin' clause adjusts the qayd but cannot revive a voided muqayyad. Therefore, the statement is a new proposal, not a modification of an existing contract.
Istidrak corrects a condition, not the essence.
5
Conclude the ruling
The first marriage is void, and the second part is a new authorization for a different contract. Hence, the master's statement does not authorize the original marriage.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية، والسلب لانتفاء أصل النكاح بنفيه : ثم ابتدائه بقدر آخر بعد الانفساخ بخلاف لا أجيزه بمائة لكن بمائتين، فإن الاستدراك في قدر المهر لا أصل النكاح وهذا مناف لكلام الإمام فخر الإسلام والبديع وغيرهما من الكتب المعتبرة، فلا بد من تصحيح النقل عمن يعارض نقله نقل هؤلاء الأخيار، ثم إن الفرق أيضاً غير واف، لأن اللام ههنا يكون حينئذ للعهد، إذ هو السابق في الاعتبار، فالمعنى لا أجيز هذا النكاح الذي بمائة لكن بمائتين نعم يرد على أصل الكلام أن عدم الاتساق ممنوع لجواز ورود النفي على المهر أي لا أجيز النكاح بمهر مائة لكن أجيز هذا النكاح بعينه بمهر مائتين ويؤيده أن مناط الحكم المقيد إنما يكون القيد فإن كان نفياً فالمقصود نفي القيد لا أصل الحكم وكذا في الإثبات فحينئذ المقصود بنفي الإجازة هو القيد، فمورد الإيجاب والنفي اختلفا، والجواب : أن المقصود بالإجازة وعدمها إنما هو ما كان موقوفاً على الإجازة والموقوف عليها النكاح الذي عقده الفضولي وهو النكاح المقيد بمهر مائة، فبانتفاء الإجازة قد بطل هذا الموقوف وإن كان المقصود بنفي الإجازة هو القيد فإنما يكون مقصوداً في ضمن نفي المقيد لا أن المقيد ثابت والقيد منتف، وهو تهافت ولا أنه ثابت في مقيد آخر إذ لا بد له من حجة وظاهر أن ههنا نكاحاً واحداً موقوفاً على إجازة السيد، وقد بطل، فلا يمكن إجازته بمائتين فهو استئناف ولو قيل : إن مقصوده عدم الرضا بهذا القيد والتقييد بمهر مائتين فهو الزام أمر لم يلتزمه الزوج إلا أن يقال المقصود الإجازة تعليقاً أي لكن أجيز هذا النكاح مقيد بمهر مائتين إن قبل الزوج وهذا لا يساعده اللفظ وبالجملة أن الموقوف كان هو المقيد، وقد ارتفع بانتفاء الإجازة، فلا يعود وليس هناك عقد آخر حتى تلحقه الإجازة، فلا بد أن يكون استئنافاً لإجازة نكاح آخر، هذا والله أعلم بأحكامه.
The third article on linguistic principles: Negation negates the origin of marriage. Then his beginning it again with a different amount after annulment, unlike 'I do not authorize it for one hundred but for two hundred,' for the correction is in the amount of the dowry, not the origin of marriage. This contradicts the statement of Imam Fakhr al-Islam and al-Badi' and other reliable books, so it is necessary to correct the transmission from those who contradict their transmission. Then the distinction is also insufficient, because the 'lam' here would be for the pact, since it is prior in consideration, so the meaning is 'I do not authorize this marriage which is for one hundred but for two hundred.' Yes, an objection may be raised that lack of consistency is not accepted, because it is possible that the negation applies to the dowry, i.e., 'I do not authorize the marriage with a dowry of one hundred but I authorize this very marriage with a dowry of two hundred.' This is supported by the fact that the locus of the restricted ruling is the restriction. If it is negation, the intent is to negate the restriction, not the origin of the ruling, similarly in affirmation. So the object of affirmation and negation differ. The answer: That the intent of authorization and its absence is only what was dependent on authorization, and what it depends on is the marriage contracted by the fuduli, which is the marriage restricted to a dowry of one hundred. With the negation of authorization, this dependent thing has become void. Even if the intent of negating authorization is the restriction, it is intended within the negation of the restricted, not that the restricted is established and the restriction is negated—this is contradictory. Nor is it established in another restricted matter, as it must have proof. It is apparent that here there is one marriage dependent on the master's authorization, and it has become void, so it cannot be authorized for two hundred; it is a new beginning. If it is said that his intent is dissatisfaction with this restriction and restriction to a dowry of two hundred, this is imposing a matter that the husband did not commit to, unless it is said that the intent is conditional authorization, i.e., 'but I authorize this marriage restricted to a dowry of two hundred if the husband accepts,' but the wording does not support this. In summary, what was dependent was the restricted, and it has been removed by the negation of authorization, so it does not return, and there is no other contract for authorization to attach to, so it must be a new beginning for authorizing another marriage. And Allah knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of how negation (nafi) interacts with a qualified act (muqayyad) in the context of a fuduli marriage needing ijaza (authorization). The author argues that when someone says 'I do not authorize it for one hundred but for two hundred,' the negation attaches to the entire qualified marriage (muqayyad bi mahr mi'a), so the act itself is void. The opposing view that the negation only targets the dowry qualification is rejected because the muqayyad is the object of ijaza; if ijaza is denied, the entire muqayyad falls. The analysis uses Hanafi usul principles: the particle 'la' for negation of the muqayyad, and the role of istidrak (correction) with 'lakin' showing that the correction is in the qayd, but that does not preserve the original. The author criticizes earlier Hanafi scholars who allowed a different interpretation, affirming the strict Hanafi position that such a statement constitutes a new ijaza, not a modification of the first.

Key Terms
فُضُولِيّ (fudūlī): A person who contracts on behalf of another without prior authorization; such contracts are suspended on the principal's ratification.
الاسْتِدْرَاك (al-istidrāk): Correction or amendment in speech, typically with 'lakin' indicating the correction of a preceding clause.
نَفْي الْمُقَيَّد (nafy al-muqayyad): Negation of the qualified entity, which in Hanafi usul negates both the qualification and the qualified itself unless context indicates otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fuduli marriage case, if the master says 'I do not authorize it for one hundred but for two hundred,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The marriage is authorized for two hundred.
B) The marriage is void; a new contract is needed.
C) The master's statement is contradictory and rejected.
D) The fuduli may adjust the dowry to two hundred.

Key Takeaway
Negation of the qualified act (nafy al-muqayyad) in Hanafi usul voids the entire act, not just the condition; a correction with 'lakin' cannot revive it.

Generality of 'Aw' in Negation and 'Waw' in Affirmation
Stating the rule that 'aw' gives generality in negation and 'waw' gives generality in affirmation unless there is a qarina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'aw' gives generality in negation and 'waw' gives generality in affirmation, and identify when a qarina diverts the default ruling.

Core Idea
'Aw' in negation = total; 'waw' in negation = together; exception requires qarina.

This passage lays out a fundamental Hanafi linguistic principle: the particle 'aw' (or) generalizes in negation, meaning it negates all individuals, while 'waw' (and) generalizes in affirmation but its negation typically negates the conjunction, not the individuals. We will explore the reasoning, the exceptions, and the practical application using the example of ila' (oath of abstinence).

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for 'aw' in negation
Recognize that 'aw' is like an indefinite noun (nakira) in negation: negation of an ambiguous entity requires negating all individuals in the custom of Arabic.
This is established by customary usage ('urf), not just logic.
2
Contrast 'waw' in affirmation vs negation
In affirmation, 'waw' indicates conjunction of all items. In negation, it negates the conjunction (i.e., the social gathering) unless a qarina indicates otherwise.
The default is that negation of 'waw' means 'not together'.
3
Understand the exception with qarina
If there is a contextual indicator (qarina) that the negation is meant to include all individuals, then 'waw' can also give generality in negation.
The majority of Hanafis follow the established usage, not the qiyas that would make 'waw' general.
4
Apply to the ila' example
A husband saying 'I will not approach either of you' (using 'aw') creates ila' from both wives. Saying 'I will not approach one of you' (using 'ihdakuma', definite) creates ila' from only one.
This shows the practical fiqh consequence of the usul rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (أو لأحد الأمرين أي لواحد من الأمرين فيعم في النفي دون الإثبات كالنكرة) فإنه في المعنى مثلها فأعطي حكمها، فإن نفي المبهم لا يكون إلا بنفي جميع الافراد عرفاً، وإن جاز عقلاً نفيه في ضمن النفي عن البعض، وما قيل إنه أي التعميم من قبيل الاستعارة فلا يظهر له وجه (إلا بدليل) صارف عن مقتضاها بخلاف الواو) فإنه يعم في الإثبات دون النفي، لأنه للجمع والنفي سلبه فيكون لسبب الاجتماع (إلا) بقرينة) صارفة عن مقتضاه، قال «مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»، القياس يقتضي أن تكون الواو أيضاً عامة في النفي، لأنها لمطلق الجمع، فإذا ورد عليه النفي اقتضى اشتراك المعطوف عليه والمعطوف فيه كما في الإثبات، ولهذا قيد صدر الشريعة الحكم بما إذا كان للاجتماع فيه تأثير ومقصوده بما إذا كان قرينة سلب الاجتماع، وإنما ذكره لأنه مضبوط دون غيره، لكن القوم ما قالوا بهذا، بل استمروا على أن الأصل فيه في النفي سلب الاجتماع إلا لصارف، فلعلهم وجدوا الاستعمال كذلك، وبهذا اندفع ما في التلويح، و«التحرير» أن سلب العموم قد يكون فيما لا يكون للاجتماع تأثير فافهم فقوله : لا أقرب ذي أو ذي مشيراً إلى زوجتيه (إيلاء منهما فأيتهما لم يقربها أربعة أشهر بانت، لأن أو في النفي يفيد التعميم (وفي) قوله : لا أقرب (إحداكما) يكون الإيلاء (من إحداهما لأن إحداكما معرفة فلا تعم في النفي
Issue: 'Aw' means 'one of two things,' so it gives generality in negation but not in affirmation, like the indefinite noun (nakira). For its meaning is similar to it, so it is given its ruling. The negation of an ambiguous entity is not achieved except by negating all individuals customarily, even if rationally its negation within the negation of some is possible. What is said that this generalization is metaphorical has no apparent basis. Except if there is a context that diverts from its import. Unlike 'waw', it gives generality in affirmation but not in negation, because it is for conjunction, and negation negates it, so it is for the purpose of social gathering. Except with a qarina that diverts from its requirement. The author of 'Matla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: Analogy would require that 'waw' also be general in negation, because it indicates absolute conjunction. So if negation occurs on it, it requires the co-participation of the conjoined and the conjoined-to in the negation, as in affirmation. For this reason, Sadr al-Shari'ah restricted the ruling to cases where social gathering has an effect. His intent is when there is a qarina negating social gathering. He mentioned it because it is well-defined unlike others. But the scholars did not adopt this; rather, they continued that the principle regarding it in negation is negation of social gathering except with a qarina. Perhaps they found usage that way. With this, what is in al-Talwih and al-Tahrir is refuted: that negation of generality may occur where social gathering has no effect. Understand. So his saying: 'I will not approach either so-and-so or so-and-so' pointing to his two wives is an oath of abstinence (ila') from both, so whichever he does not approach for four months becomes divorced, because 'aw' in negation gives generality. And in his saying: 'I will not approach one of you' is ila' from one of them, because 'one of you' is definite, so it does not give generality in negation.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule from Hanafi linguistic principles. It establishes that the particle 'aw' (or) functions like an indefinite noun (nakira) in that it generalizes only in the context of negation, not affirmation. For example, if one says 'I did not see Zaid or Amr,' it means he saw neither. But in affirmation, 'I saw Zaid or Amr' does not mean both. Conversely, 'waw' (and) generalizes in affirmation but not in negation. In negation, 'I did not see Zaid and Amr' typically means he did not see both together. The author notes that Sadr al-Shari'ah restricted this to cases where social gathering (ijtima') has effect, but the majority of Hanafi scholars follow the established usage that 'waw' in negation simply negate the conjunction unless context indicates otherwise. The example of ila' illustrates: 'I will not approach either of you' (using 'aw') creates an oath from both wives, while 'I will not approach one of you' (using 'ihdakuma') creates an oath from only one. This shows the practical fiqh application of the usul rule.

Key Terms
نَكِرَة (nakira): An indefinite noun; in negation, it gives generality (umum) because its ambiguity requires complete negation.
الْعُمُوم (al-ʿumūm): Generality; inclusion of all individuals under a ruling.
قَرِينَة (qarīna): Contextual indicator that diverts a particle from its default ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his two wives, 'I will not approach either (ذِي أو ذِي) of you,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) It is ila' from both wives; whichever he does not approach for 4 months divorces.
B) It is ila' from only one wife.
C) It is not a valid ila' because the oath is ambiguous.
D) The husband must choose which wife he meant.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that 'aw' generalizes in negation but 'waw' does not is a key linguistic principle derived from customary usage and applied in oaths and other legal contexts.

Interpreting 'أو' in Manumission Statements: The Hanafi Dispute
Resolving the meaning of 'أو' when saying 'this is free or this'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting 'أو' in legal utterances and explain the dispute between Zufar and the majority.

Core Idea
When a master says 'هذا حر أو هذا', the last-named is freed and the master chooses from the first two.

The interpretation of the particle 'أو' in legal utterances can determine the legal outcome. In a manumission statement like 'هذا حر أو هذا' (this is free or this), scholars differ on whether the ruling applies to the nearest mentioned item or requires clarification. This Usul al-Fiqh passage from Ibn al-Humam's commentary analyzes two grammatical possibilities and resolves the preferred ruling in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two grammatical structures
Recognize that 'هذا حر أو هذا' can be read as a conjunction of subjects under a single predicate (meaning 'both are free') or as a disjunction of two sentences (meaning 'one of them is free').
The first reading is literal conjunction; the second treats 'أو' as introducing an alternative sentence.
2
Connect each structure to a scholar's position
Zufar adopts the conjunction reading (first probability) and insists that no manumission occurs without clarification. The majority (jumhur) adopts the disjunction reading (second probability) and holds that the last-named is freed immediately while the master chooses among the earlier ones.
The majority view is also the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
Zufar argues that the literal conjunction requires no added estimation. The majority counters that the change in predicate (from singular to dual) is conventional (wad'i), not necessary, and that their interpretation avoids excessive estimations.
The majority also appeals to the conventional function of 'أو' as indicating choice.
4
Follow the tarjih (preponderance) argument
The author prefers the majority view because it requires fewer estimations and aligns with the standard usage of 'أو'. The text also compares 'أو' with 'و' to argue for unity of the predicate.
The preponderance is based on linguistic simplicity and established convention.
5
State the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Zufar's view, no slave is freed until the master clarifies his intention. Under the majority, the last-named slave is immediately freed, and the master must select one of the earlier slaves to free.
This difference has real implications for the status of the slaves involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد
فرع اختلف في : هذا حر أو هذا وهذا يحتمل أن يكون معطوفاً على مدخول أو فالمعنى هذا حر أو هذان ويحتمل أن يكون عطف الجملة على الجملة أو المفرد على مفهوم أحدهما المأخوذ أي أحدهما حر وهذا فقيل : وعليه زفر لا عتق إلا بالبيان كهذا أو هذان) رجوعاً إلى الاحتمال الأول وقيل وعليه الجمهور) وهو ظاهر الرواية (يعتق الأخير ويتخير من الأولين، لأنه كأحدهما) حر (وهذا) رجوعاً إلى الاحتمال الثاني وينبغي أن يكون النزاع فيما لا نية له، وإلا فيحال على النية (ورجح) القول الثاني ( بأن التغير ههنا (ضروري) بتراخي حكم الإنشاء عنه (وهي مندفعة بتوقف الأول على الثاني حتى يصير معه كلاماً واحداً مفيداً لحكم موقوف على خيار السيد (فقط) من دون توقفه على الثالث والضرورات تتقدر بقدرها فلا يتوقف عليه فافهم، وفيه شائبة من الخفاء، فإن كون التغير ضرورياً غير ظاهر، بل التغير وضعي، لأن وضع أو يقتضي أن ما بعده بعده مع ما قبله كلام مفيد لإيقاع الحكم في الواحد
وهذا
المبهم، وههنا يجوز أن يكون ما بعده هو والثالث معاً، وأيضاً لكلام في قدر الضرورة، فإنه لو كان معطوفاً على ما بعد أو لزم الضرورة في توقفه على الثالث فتدبر (والترجيح بلزوم تقدير التثنية خبراً (على) الاحتمال (الأول) في المعطوف مع كونه في المعطوف عليه مفرداً كما قال صدر الشريعة (ليس بشيء فلا نسلم اللزوم) ونقول : التقدير هكذا : هذا أو هذا حر حر، وفيه أن فيه كثرة التقديرات والقلة فيها أولى فتأمل (ولا) نسلم (بطلان اللازم) فإنه يجوز في الخبر بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه وهو شائع ذائع، بل إنما يجب اتحاد المادة فقط، وأنت خبير بأن حكم الواو وحكم أو واحد في تعلق المعطوف بعين ما يتعلق به المعطوف عليه إن أمكن وإلا يقدر المثل في عطف المفردات وفي صورة التخالف عطف الجملة على الجملة، وههنا من قبيل عطف المفردات، ولو تنزلنا قلنا : لا شك أولوية الاتحاد وهذا القدر كاف للترجيح فتأمل.
One / Branch: they differed concerning: 'This is free or this is free' (meaning a statement like 'this slave is free or this slave'). It is possible that it is conjoined to the particle 'or', so the meaning is 'this is free or these two'. And it is possible that it is the conjunction of a sentence to a sentence, or a single word to the understood concept of one of them, i.e., 'one of them is free'. So it was said: and upon this Zufar holds that no manumission occurs except with clarification, like 'this or these two', returning to the first possibility. And it was said: and upon this the majority, and it is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya): the latter is freed and he chooses from the first two, because it is as if one of them is free. And this returns to the second possibility. And the dispute should be concerning cases where there is no intention; otherwise it is referred to intention. And the second opinion is preponderated because the change here is necessary due to the delay of the ruling of the utterance, and it is removed by making the first dependent on the second so that together they become one complete speech giving a ruling dependent on the master's choice only, without relying on the third. And necessities are estimated by their measure, so it does not rely on it. Understand. And there is an element of obscurity in it, for the change being necessary is not apparent; rather the change is conventional (wad'i), because the conventional use of 'or' requires that what follows it is with what precedes it a complete speech for effecting a ruling in the ambiguous. And here it is possible that what follows it is both the second and the third together. And also there is discussion about the extent of necessity, for if it were conjoined to what is after 'or', then necessity would arise in its reliance on the third. So ponder. And the preponderance, by requiring the estimation of a dual predicate, over the first possibility regarding the conjoined being singular while the conjoined-to is singular, as Sadr al-Shari'ah said, is not valid; we do not accept the necessity. And we say: the estimation is like this: 'this or this is free, free', and there is in it many estimations, and fewer is preferable. So ponder. And we do not accept the invalidity of the necessary effect, for it is permissible in the predicate between the conjoined and the conjoined-to, and it is widespread. Rather only unity of matter (material) is required. And you are aware that the rule of 'and' and the rule of 'or' are the same regarding the attachment of the conjoined to the same thing to which the conjoined-to is attached, if possible; otherwise the like is estimated in the conjunction of single words, and in the case of disparity, conjunction of sentence to sentence. And here it is of the type of conjunction of single words. And if we concede, we say: there is no doubt about the priority of unification, and this much suffices for preponderance. So ponder.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute regarding the interpretation of the phrase 'هذا حر أو هذا' (this is free or this) when uttered by a master. The core issue is whether 'أو' conjoins the two individuals to the predicate 'حر' in a single sentence or whether it implies a choice between two separate sentences. Two probabilities are presented: (1) 'أو' is a conjunction affecting the subject, making the meaning 'this is free or these two are free' (i.e., both are covered by the same predicate), and (2) 'أو' conjoins two sentences, implying a disjunction: 'either this is free or that is free' (one of the two). The first view is attributed to Zufar, who says no manumission occurs without clarification—meaning the master must specify which one he intended. The second view, held by the majority (jumhur) and being the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), is that the last-named slave is freed (i.e., the one immediately before 'أو' is effective) and the master makes a choice concerning the first two (or the earlier ones). The text then engages in a technical debate about whether the change in the predicate (from singular to dual) is necessary or conventional. The author prefers the second opinion, arguing that the first opinion requires excessive estimation (تقديرات), while the second is simpler and aligns with the conventional use of 'أو'. The discussion also compares 'أو' to 'و' (and), asserting that both operate similarly in conjunction. The passage is dense with grammatical analysis aimed at determining the correct legal effect of such utterances.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The established transmitted opinion in the Hanafi school (from Imam Muhammad's six books).
عطف المفردات (atf al-mufradat): Conjoining single words (nouns or verbs) under a common predicate.
أو (aw): Particle of disjunction or conjunction; in the majority view, it attaches the ruling to the nearest item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority (zahir al-riwaya), if a master says 'هذا حر أو هذا' (this is free or this), what is the legal outcome?
A) No slave is freed until the master clarifies
B) The first-named slave is freed and the master chooses from the rest
C) The last-named slave is freed and the master chooses from the first two
D) All slaves are freed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for 'أو' in legal utterances is that the ruling attaches to the nearest mentioned item, with the master having a choice over the earlier items, based on simplicity and conventional usage.

Understanding 'Hatta' in Hanafi Usul: Ghayah, Partiality, and Inclusion
Defining 'حتى' as denoting endpoint with gradation and inclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three functions of 'hatta' (preposition, conjunction, inceptive) and state the Hanafi preferred view on inclusion of what follows based on suitability.

Core Idea
Hatta requires partiality; inclusion depends on the part's suitability for the ruling.

This lesson explores the particle 'hatta' as used in legal Arabic. In Hanafi usul, precise understanding of such particles is essential for deriving rulings correctly. The passage presents the linguistic analysis from Fawatih al-Rahamut, detailing the conditions for using 'hatta' as a preposition, conjunction, or inceptive particle, and the significant disagreement among grammarians and usulis on whether what follows 'hatta' is included in the ruling of what precedes. The Hanafi school adopts a middle position based on conventional usage and semantic suitability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical meaning of 'hatta'
Recognize that 'hatta' primarily denotes the end point (ghayah) but can be used with a gradation consideration by the speaker.
Not literal; e.g., 'people died, even the prophets'—the prophets' death is not the literal end.
2
Distinguish its three grammatical functions
Hatta functions as a preposition (jar), a conjunction (atf), and an inceptive particle (ibtida'). Each has specific conditions.
The common condition is partiality (al-ba'diyya): what follows must be part of the preceding category.
3
Analyze the condition of partiality
For preposition and conjunction, the word after 'hatta' must be a part of what came before. For inceptive, the predicate must be of the same kind as the antecedent.
Example: 'Akalatu al-samakata hatta ra'saha' (I ate the fish, even its head) — head is part of fish.
4
Map the four opinions on inclusion
List the four schools: (1) absolute inclusion, (2) absolute non-inclusion, (3) inclusion if suitable for ruling, (4) no indication without context. Note the Hanafi preference for the third.
The fourth opinion (Tha'lab, Ibn Malik) is distinct from the first two because it does not indicate either inclusion or exclusion inherently.
5
Apply to a legal text
Practice with a fiqh example: 'Sumni ila ghurub al-shams hatta yaghriba' — does 'hatta' include the moment of sunset? The Hanafi view with suitability yields inclusion if sunset is a part of the fast's end.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the fast ends at the beginning or full completion of sunset.

(حتى للغاية ولو كانت (باعتبار التكلم نحو مات الناس حتى الأنبياء، وقدم الحاج حتى المشاة فإن موتهم ليس غاية بل في الوسط، وكذا قدوم المشاة ليس غاية لقدوم الحاج، لكن المتكلم اعتبر جانباً أعلى وجانباً أدون واعتبر ابتداء الحكم من الأدون منتهياً إلى الأعلى كما في المثال الأول، أو اعتبر بالعكس كما في المثال الثاني واعتبار ذلك الاعتبار) من المتكلم ليس بتكلف كما قيل في «التحرير» (بل) تحقيق للعرف فإن الثقات نقلوا عن أصحاب اللغة هذا الاعتبار في حتى فلا بد من القبول (وتكون) حتى جارة وعاطفة والشرط) فيهما (البعضية) أي يكون ما بعدها داخلاً فيما قبلها، لا أنه داخل في حكم ما قبلها، فإن فيه خلافاً في الجارة ( وابتدائية بعدها جملة مذكورة الطرفين كما عند البصرية أو أعم منها ، ومما قدر أحدهما كما عند علماء الكوفة (والشرط) في الابتدائية (أن يكون الخبر من جنس المتقدم) إما أن يكون نفسه أو نوعاً من أنواعه أو لازماً له ولو عرفاً (ومنه قول امرىء القيس :

Issue: 'Hatta' is for ghayah (end), even if it is (by the speaker's consideration such as 'people died, even the prophets' and 'the pilgrims arrived, even the pedestrians' – for their death is not the end but in the middle, and likewise the arrival of pedestrians is not the end of the pilgrims' arrival. But the speaker considered a higher side and a lower side, and considered the beginning of the ruling from the lower ending at the higher as in the first example, or considered the opposite as in the second example. And such consideration by the speaker is not far-fetched as was said in al-Tahrir; rather it is a confirmation of conventional usage, for reliable authorities have transmitted from lexicographers this consideration in 'hatta', so acceptance is necessary. And 'hatta' is a preposition and a conjunction, and the condition in both is partiality (al-ba'diyya), i.e., what comes after it is included in what comes before it, not that it is included in the ruling of what comes before it, for there is disagreement regarding the preposition. And the inceptive (harf ibtida') after which a sentence with both ends mentioned, as according to the Basrans, or more general than that, including where one end is estimated, as according to the Kufan scholars. And the condition in the inceptive is that the predicate be of the same kind as the antecedent, either being itself or a type of its types or a necessary consequence of it, even by convention. And from it is the saying of Imru' al-Qais: 'I traveled with them until their mounts were exhausted, and even the steeds are not led with ropes.' And it is validated by the three readings (accusative, nominative, and preposition). (I ate the fish, even its head: the prepositional and accusative are apparent, and the nominative with an estimated predicate, i.e., 'even its head is eaten'). And there is disagreement among the Basrans concerning the inclusion of what follows it in what precedes it. When it is a preposition, there are schools: first, inclusion absolutely, which is the school of Ibn al-Siraj, Abu Ali, and many later grammarians. Second, no inclusion absolutely, which is the school of the majority of grammarians. Third, if it is a part suitable for the ruling, it is included; otherwise not, which is the school of al-Mubarrad, al-Farra', and Abd al-Qahir. Fourth, no indication of inclusion or exclusion except by a contextual clue indicating one of them, which is attributed to Tha'lab and chosen by Ibn Malik. And this school is neither of the first two regarding inclusion and non-inclusion, as in al-Tahrir, because the first two are from the class of indicating (the first indicates inclusion, the second indicates exclusion), and this one does not indicate either. Then the outcome of the third school is either commonality...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition of the particle 'حتى' (hatta) as used in legal and linguistic contexts. It states that 'حتى' primarily denotes ghayah (end point), but it can be used metaphorically when the speaker intends a gradation from a lower to a higher rank or vice versa, as in the example 'مات الناس حتى الأنبياء' (people died, even the prophets). Here, the death of prophets is not the end of the people's death; rather, the speaker considers that prophets are at a higher rank, and the ruling (death) applies to them as well. Similarly, 'قدم الحاج حتى المشاة' (the pilgrims arrived, even the pedestrians) considers the reverse. This consideration is not forced but is based on linguistic convention (urf). Then the passage distinguishes the functions of 'حتى' as a preposition (jarr) and a conjunction (atf), both requiring partiality (ba'diyya)—i.e., the word after 'حتى' must be part of the category before it, but not necessarily included in the same ruling. There is a disagreement among grammarians regarding whether what follows 'حتى' is included in the ruling of what precedes it. Four opinions are listed: (1) absolute inclusion, (2) absolute non-inclusion, (3) inclusion if the following is a part suitable for the ruling, and (4) neither inclusion nor exclusion without a contextual clue. The passage also notes the conditions for 'حتى' as an inceptive particle (hata ibtida'iyya). This discussion is foundational for understanding how 'حتى' affects the scope of legal rulings in texts.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): Particle indicating ghayah (end) with gradation; used as preposition or conjunction requiring partiality.
البعضية (al-ba'diyya): Condition that what follows 'حتى' is a part of the category before it.
غاية (ghayah): End point, ultimate limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always included
B) Never included
C) Included if it is a part suitable for the ruling
D) Included only if there is a contextual clue

Key Takeaway
The particle 'hatta' in usul requires partiality and its inclusion effect depends on the semantic suitability of the following part.

The Three Meanings of Hatta: Ghaya, Sababiyya, and 'Atf
Usul definition of hatta with three meanings and application in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of the particle 'hatta' and apply the Hanafi rule that permits 'atf for mere sequence metaphorically when ghaya and sababiyya are invalid, particularly in oaths.

Core Idea
Hatta can indicate endpoint, causality, or mere sequence — Hanafi usul accepts the last metaphorically.

This lesson examines the Arabic particle 'hatta' and its multiple meanings in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. While grammarians restrict 'hatta' to ghaya (endpoint) and sababiyya (causality), Hanafi jurists, drawing on their deep linguistic expertise, also permit a metaphorical usage for 'atf (conjunction) indicating mere sequence. This expansion has direct consequences for legal rulings, especially in oaths and contracts where other meanings are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
Recognize that 'hatta' can mean ghaya (endpoint), sababiyya (causality), or 'atf (conjunction for mere sequence). Understand the linguistic context that determines which meaning applies.
The meanings are not mutually exclusive; context (qarina) decides.
2
Analyze the metaphorical basis for 'atf
Examine why Hanafi usul allows 'hatta' to mean mere sequence: it is a metaphor (majaz) for the particle 'fa', relying on the principle that metaphor does not require hearing every particular instance.
The relationship is sharing in the completion of a ruling (zuhur tamam al-hukm).
3
Explore the dispute with grammarians
Grammarians reject the 'atf meaning because they have not found it in classical Arabic. The Hanafi response is that mujtahids are more advanced in language and that a metaphorical usage does not require attested instances.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in usul methodology.
4
Apply to a legal oath scenario
Given the oath 'La atika hatta ataghaddaa' (I will not come to you until I have breakfast), determine that ghaya and sababiyya are invalid because coming cannot be the endpoint or cause of breakfast. Thus, 'hatta' is interpreted as 'atf for mere sequence.
Both actions must occur, even if delayed, unless the swearer intends immediacy.
5
Determine the ruling on fulfillment
For the oath to be fulfilled, both coming and breakfast must happen. If the swearer intends immediacy, they must occur consecutively; otherwise, delay is allowed. This is the Hanafi mufta bihi.
If 'hatta' is taken as equivalent to 'fa', immediacy is required in all cases.

كلام العرب لهذا العطف نظير لا يعتبر في مقابلة المجتهدين، فإنهم متقدمون في فحص اللغات، فلا يعارض قولهم، وأما الجواب بأنه لا يشترط سماع الجزئيات في التجوّز فليس بشيء، فإن السماع وإن لم يكن شرطاً لكن يجب أن لا يظهر المنع كما في إطلاق الأب على الابن وههنا يمنعون هذا النحو من الاستعمال فيما زعم النحاة. ٢٢٠ فواتح الرحموت شرح مسلم الثبوت الجزء الأول (فرع). قال: إن لم آتك حتى أتغدى عندك فكذا إذ لا يصح ههنا اعتبار الغاية، وهو ظاهر، وكذا لا تصح السببية، فإن إتيانه لا يصلح سبباً للتغدي من نفسه، وهو لا يصلح جزاء للإتيان فتعذر السببية أيضاً فحمل على العطف لمجرد الترتيب. فيشترط للبر وجود الفعلين) من الإتيان والتغدي (ولو متراخياً) إلى آخر العمر في غير الموقت، أو إلى آخر الوقت الذي قيد به في الموقت، فإن حتى لمجرد الترتيب إلا أن ينوي (الاتصال فيجب حينئذ للبر الاتصال هذا، ويتأتى ممن جعلها بمعنى الفاء أن يشترط الاتصال، نوى أو لم ينو، والله أعلم بأحكامه.
The third discourse on linguistic principles: delay, causality and partiality, and their absence as a qarina for specifying one of two meanings; that is far-fetched, as it is contrary to the principle without necessary cause. And either each is specified by two qarinas without being placed for them, so either the placing is for one of them—it has become one of the first two—or there is no single placing; that is the fourth. The wording of At-Tahrir can be reduced to one of these two possibilities as well. Allah knows best. The agreement on inclusion in 'atf and ibtida'. And 'hatta' is metaphorically used for causality if ghaya is not apt: i.e., causality of what precedes for what follows, like 'aslamtu hatta adkhula al-jannah'. In At-Tahrir: causality of one for the other. And like causality of the second for the first: 'ribihtu hatta atajarat'. He is required to correct a usage acceptable in Arabic; hypothetical examples are insufficient unless one says: in metaphor, hearing of particulars is not required; just as causality of the first is permissible so is its opposite. He indicated the explanation of the relationship by his saying: 'For the cause appears fully by the caused, so it is as if it ends with it, because by it also the end of the endpoint appears. This is the meaning in Al-Kashf: the relationship between ghaya and causality is the sharing in the completion of the ruling; by that is meant sharing in the appearance of the completion of the ruling.' How not? If ending were a reality for him, ghaya would be a reality, so the reality would be straight, and metaphor would not be valid, and he was about to prove metaphor. This is a fallacy. When the meaning intended by the author of Al-Kashf is determined, the objection in At-Talwih that 'entry is not the endpoint of Islam so it does not apply uniformly in 'aslamtu hatta adkhula al-jannah' does not arise. It may also be answered that the relationship of metaphor does not have to be uniform in all individuals, and some individuals of the caused are a ghaya, so it does not harm in the mentioned example, and it does not necessitate that it is reality in causality absolutely. Understand. And what he chose in At-Talwih: that it is intendedness of what comes after from what is before, which is shared between ghaya and causality, is refuted by 'hatta ra'siha' because that is not intended from eating the fish. There is in it that uniformness of the relationship in all individuals of the borrowed-from is not obligatory, but it reverses, so uniformness in individuals of the borrowed-to is also not obligatory. And specification by the occurrence of Islam or the Islam of the world for uniformness of ghaya in the mentioned example, as in At-Tahrir, in repelling the objection of At-Talwih, is a needless strain. How not? Occurrence does not allow being made an endpoint of anything, so entry cannot be a ghaya for it. The Islam of the world ends with death, so entry cannot be a ghaya for it. Understand. If it is not suitable for ghaya or causality, then it is permissible for 'atf for mere sequence, which is more general than that which is with immediacy or delay. For if you say: This is not a meaning of the word, for a particle is not placed for mere sequence more general than succession and delay, I say: It is not a condition of metaphor that the borrowed-to be the literal denotation of the word; rather it suffices that it be a necessary or implicit denotation; rather it is permissible that no word indicates it at all by one of the indications. Whoever denies this, let him bring proof. It is narrated from Kashf al-Manar that it is metaphor for the meaning of fa'. Hence the jurists permitted metaphorically 'ja'a Zaidun hatta Amrun' i.e., after Amr. The opposition of grammarians in this and their saying that it has not occurred in Arabic speech for this 'atf at all is not considered in comparison with the mujtahids, for they are advanced in the examination of languages, so their statement is not opposed. As for the answer that hearing of particulars is not required in metaphor, it is nothing, because hearing, though not a condition, must not show prevention, as in calling the father 'son'; and here they prevent this type of usage according to what the grammarians claimed. (Sub-issue) He said: If I do not come to you until I have breakfast with you, then such-and-such. Here ghaya consideration is not valid, and it is obvious. Similarly, causality is not valid, for his coming cannot be a cause for breakfast by itself, and it cannot be a recompense for coming, so causality is also impossible. So it is interpreted as 'atf for mere sequence. So for fulfillment, the existence of both actions—coming and breakfast—is required, even if delayed, until the end of life in unbound oaths, or until the end of the time specified in bound oaths. For hatta is for mere sequence, unless he intends (immediacy), then for fulfillment immediacy is required. And it can be derived from the one who makes it equivalent to fa' that immediacy is required whether he intended or did not intend. Allah knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and usul analysis of the particle 'hatta' and its meanings. The author begins by discussing the third discourse on linguistic principles, focusing on the concepts of delay (takhaluf), causality (sababiyya), and partiality (juz'iyya). He argues that assigning a qarina to specify one of two meanings is against the principle, and he examines the views in al-Tahrir. He then moves to the metaphorical use of 'hatta' for causality when ghaya (endpoint) does not fit, giving examples like 'aslamtu hatta adkhula al-jannah' (I became Muslim until I enter paradise). The commentary from al-Kashf is cited, explaining that the relationship between ghaya and causality is a sharing in the completion of the ruling. Objections from al-Talwih are addressed: the objection that 'entry' is not the endpoint of Islam is countered by noting that metaphor relationships need not be uniform; some individuals of the caused can be an endpoint. The author then discusses when 'hatta' is used for 'atf (conjunction) for mere sequence, which is broader than succession or delay. He refutes the argument that a particle is not placed for mere sequence, explaining that metaphor can indicate implicit or necessary meanings. He cites that jurists have permitted the metaphorical use 'ja'a Zaid hatta Amr' (Zaid came until Amr) meaning 'after Amr', and this overrides the grammarians' objection because mujtahids are advanced in language. The sub-issue at the end applies these meanings to a conditional oath: someone says 'I will not come to you until I have breakfast with you.' Here, ghaya and causality are invalid, so it is interpreted as 'atf for mere sequence. For the oath to be fulfilled, both actions must occur, even if delayed. If the swearer intends immediacy, then immediacy is required. The one who treats 'hatta' as equivalent to 'fa' would require immediacy regardless of intention.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): A particle that can indicate ghaya (endpoint), sababiyya (causality), or 'atf (conjunction) for mere sequence
غاية (ghaya): The endpoint or limit of an action or ruling
سببية (sababiyya): Causality relationship where one thing causes another
عطف ('atf): Conjunction, linking two elements in sequence
ترتيب (tartib): Sequence, ordering of events

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, if someone swears 'I will not come to you until I have breakfast with you,' what is required for the oath to be fulfilled?
A) Only the coming must occur
B) Only the breakfast must occur
C) Both actions must occur, even if delayed
D) Both actions must occur immediately, regardless of intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, hatta can serve as a conjunction for mere sequence (tartib) when ghaya and sababiyya are not possible, relying on metaphorical extension and the linguistic authority of mujtahids.

The Primary Meaning of Baa: Core vs. Extended Uses
Defining the primary meaning of باء as الإلصاق (adhesion).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary meaning of the letter باء in Hanafi usul and apply it to interpret a text containing باء by distinguishing between literal and extended uses.

Core Idea
The باء has one core meaning—adhesion (ilṣāq); all other uses are specific instances, not separate meanings.

In Hanafi usul, every letter and particle is presumed to have a core meaning. This lesson unpacks the debate around the letter باء. The author establishes that its primary meaning is adhesion (ilṣāq), and other uses like seeking help (isti‘āna) or causation (sababiyya) are not independent meanings but specific applications of adhesion. Understanding this is crucial for correctly interpreting Quranic and legal texts where باء appears, ensuring consistency in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
State the author's main position about the meaning of the letter باء. Look for the phrase 'الباء للإلصاق'.
This is the thesis: the باء is for adhesion.
2
Analyze the first rejected view (homonymy)
Some grammarians claimed the باء is a homonym with multiple unrelated meanings. Identify why the author rejects this: it contradicts the principle (خلاف الأصل).
Homonymy would require multiple separate linguistic placements, which is contrary to the default assumption of one meaning.
3
Analyze the second rejected view (metaphor)
Others said only adhesion is literal, and other uses are metaphorical. Identify the author's rebuttal: this also contradicts the principle and is unnecessary because the other uses are simply specific instances of the universal adhesion.
Calling them metaphorical would require separate evidence, which is not present.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author argues that adhesion is a graded concept (مشكك) that applies universally. Other meanings are not separate but are 'individuals' (أفراد) of this universal. This is how particles work: one placement covers all specific uses.
This avoids both homonymy and metaphor by positing a universal that has many particular manifestations.
5
Reflect on the application
How does this affect interpreting a text like 'بسم الله'? The باء in bismillah is not for seeking help as a separate meaning; it is adhesion with the name, indicating that the action begins 'with' or 'through' Allah's name, i.e., attached to it.
This yields a consistent reading without resorting to multiple unrelated meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الباء للإلصاق وهو معنى مشكك يصدق على كل ما استعمل فيه الباء، كما أشار إليه بقوله : (ومنه الاستعانة والسببية والظرفية والمصاحبة وليس الأمر كما زعم بعض النحاة أن الباء مشترك فيها بأوضاع فإنه خلاف الأصل، وليس الأمر أيضاً كما توهم البعض أن إطلاقها على الإلصاق حقيقة وفيما وراءها من المعاني مجاز، كيف وهو خلاف الأصل فلا يصار إليه من غير ضرورة بل إنما يستعمل فيها لأنها من أفراد الإلصاق وقد وضع لإفراده الجزئية بوضع واحد كما هو شأن الحروف، وبما قررنا ظهر لك اندفاع ما يورد من أن التجوز لازم قطعاً، فإن استعمال الباء في هذه المعاني مقطوع به، وهي وإن كانت أفراد الإلصاق، لكن إطلاق العام على الخاص من قبيل المجاز، وله وجه آخر من الدفع فإن إطلاق العام على الخاص من حيث إنه هو ليس مجازاً، فتأمل فيه وظهر أيضاً اندفاع ما يورد من أن الباء لو كانت موضوعة للإلصاق الكلي صارت اسماً لأن معاني الحروف روابط جزئية فهي موضوعة للإلصاق الخاص، وفي الإلصاقات الأخر تكون مجازاً فافهم
Issue: The باء is for al-ilṣāq (adhesion), which is a graded concept that applies to every usage of باء, as he indicated by his saying: 'And from it [derive] isti‘āna (seeking help), sababiyya (causation), zarfiyya (adverbiality), and muṣāḥaba (accompaniment).' The matter is not as some grammarians claimed that the باء is a homonym with multiple meanings, for that contradicts the principle. Nor is it as some supposed that its use for adhesion is literal and other meanings are metaphorical, for that also contradicts the principle and is not resorted to without necessity. Rather, it is used in them because they are individuals of adhesion, and it was placed for its individual parts by one placement, as is the case with particles. By our establishment, it becomes clear to you that the objection that metaphorical usage is necessarily required is repelled. For the use of باء in these meanings is certain, and although they are individuals of adhesion, the application of the general to the specific is a type of metaphor. But there is another way to repel: the application of the general to the specific in that it is itself is not metaphor. So ponder it. It also becomes clear that the objection that if the باء were placed for the universal adhesion, it would become a noun, because the meanings of particles are particular connectors, so it is placed for particular adhesion, and in other adhesions it would be metaphorical, so understand.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition of the letter باء. It argues that the primary meaning of باء is الإلصاق (adhesion), and all other uses (like isti'ana, sababiyya, etc.) are extensions of that primary meaning. The author rejects the view that باء is homonymous (with multiple unrelated meanings) or that its other uses are merely metaphorical. He insists that the basic meaning is adhesion, and other uses are specific applications of that general concept. This is important in Hanafi usul for interpreting Quranic and legal texts where باء appears, ensuring consistency in meaning.

Key Terms
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): The primary meaning of the letter باء; adhesion or attachment.
الاستعانة (al-isti‘āna): Seeking help; one of the derived uses of باء.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Homonymy
B) Metaphor
C) الإلصاق (adhesion)
D) السببية (causation)

Key Takeaway
The باء has one core meaning—al-ilṣāq (adhesion)—and all other uses are specific individuals of that universal, not separate or metaphorical meanings.

Refuting the Shafi'i Claim of Baa al-Tab'id
Refuting the claim that باء in {وامسحوا برؤوسكم} indicates تبعيض.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of linguistic induction (istiqra’) to refute a claim that a particle indicates a meaning not established in the Arabic language.

Core Idea
The باء of wiping does not indicate partiality – linguistic induction proves it is for adhesion.

In this passage, the author tackles a key usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi‘is over the meaning of the preposition باء in the Quranic verse of wiping the head. The Shafi‘is claim it indicates partiality (tab‘īḍ), implying that only part of the head must be wiped. The author refutes this by citing the consensus of Arabic scholars and using the method of induction (istiqra’) to show that باء never carries this meaning in the language. This lesson walks through the arguments and counter-arguments, culminating in the Hanafi position that the verse requires wiping a quarter of the head.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested element
Determine what exactly is disputed: the meaning of باء in {وامسحوا برؤوسكم} (al-Mā’ida 6).
2
State the Shafi‘i claim
The Shafi‘is argue that باء indicates partiality (tab‘īḍ), so the verse means 'wipe some of your heads'.
3
Present the Hanafi refutation
Arabic scholars have rejected this claim; induction shows باء is not used for partiality in the language.
The author quotes Ibn Burhān and criticizes the author of al-Qāmūs.
4
Understand the methodological tool
The Hanafis use linguistic induction (istiqra’) to demonstrate absence of usage – a sound method that yields certainty.
Compare to the testimony of heirs' inventory: it is accepted despite being testimony about a negative.
5
Respond to the Shafi‘i poetic evidence
The poem 'شربت بماء الدحرضين' does not prove partiality because the باء could be extraneous (ziyāda) or the verb implies incorporation (taḍmīn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الشافعية إنها للتبعيض في: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] فقد أنكره محققو العربية، حتى قال ابن برهان منهم من زعم أن الباء للتبعيض، فقد أتى على أهل اللغة بما لا يعرفونه فلا يصغى إلى هذا القول أصلاً، ولا يلتفت إلى كلام أمثال صاحب القاموس، فإنه يعد مكابرة، ثم إنه لو ثبت فهذا معنى آخر عندهم غير الإلصاق كما يشهد به كلامهم لا أنه يكون منه كما توهم صاحب «التحرير» (وما) قال (في المنهاج) في الجواب عنه (إنه شهادة على النفي فلا يسمع (مدفوع) حال كونه (على وهنه) أي موهوناً في نفسه (بأنه) أي قول النحاة وإن كان شهادة على النفي لكنه كشهادة حصر الورثة فإنها شهادة بنفي علمهم بوارث آخر، وهي مقبولة، لأنه لو كان لأحاط به علمهم، كذا هذا شهادة على عدم علمهم بالتبعيض فتقبل، لأنه لو كان لأحاط علمهم به ووجه وهنه أنه ليس شهادة أصلاً، بل استقراء صحيح لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه، كقولهم بالفاعل لا يكون منصوباً، والاستقراء في نفي استعمال أمثال هذه الحروف التي لا يخلو عنها أكثر التركيبات يفيد القطع بعين ما يذكر المصنف في مفهوم المخالفة، ولا يقبل الآحاد في إثباته، بخلاف النفي، فلا يقبل قول أبي علي في إثبات التبعيض فتدبر، قالوا: قد استعمل الباء فيه في قول الشاعر: شربت بماء الدحرضين فأصبحت زوراء تنفر عن حياض الديلم أجاب بقوله: (وشربت بماء الدحرضين غير مثبت للتبعيض (لاحتمال الزيادة والتضمين) والدحرض ماء وماء آخر ماء وشيع وقيل ماء لبني سعد، وقيل: بلد، والدحرضان تثنية الدحرض كالقمرين والزوراء المائلة والديلم قوم من الترك شبه بهم أعداءهم، وقيل: أرض.
And the statement of the Shafi‘is that it (the باء) is for division (tab‘īḍ) in {And wipe your heads} [al-Mā’ida 6] – the verifiers of Arabic have rejected it. Ibn Burhān said: 'Some of them claim that the باء is for division. They have come upon the people of language with what they do not know, so do not listen to this statement at all, and do not pay attention to the words of the likes of the author of al-Qāmūs, for it is considered obstinacy.' Then, if it were established, then this is another meaning according to them other than adhesion, as their words testify, not that it is from it (adhesion) as the author of al-Taḥrīr supposed. And what he said in al-Minhāj in response to it, that 'it is a testimony about a negative, so it is not heard,' is rebutted, while it is weak in itself because the statement of the grammarians, even if it is a testimony about a negative, it is like the testimony of the heirs' inventory: it is testimony of their lack of knowledge of another heir, and it is accepted because if there were one, their knowledge would encompass it. Similarly, this is a testimony of their lack of knowledge of division, so it is accepted because if there were one, their knowledge would encompass it. The aspect of its weakness is that it is not testimony at all, but rather a sound induction, to which falsehood cannot come from before or behind, like their statement that the subject is not accusative. Induction in denying the usage of such particles, which are found in most expressions, yields certainty in the same thing the author mentions in the concept of mukhālafa. And isolated reports are not accepted in proving it, contrary to the negative. So the statement of Abū ‘Alī in proving division is not accepted. So ponder. They said: the باء has been used in it in the poet's saying: 'I drank with the water of al-Daḥraḍayn, so I became crooked, shying away from the basins of al-Daylam.' He responded with his saying: 'And "I drank with the water of al-Daḥraḍayn" does not prove division, due to the possibility of extraneousness (ziyāda) and implication (taḍmīn).' Al-Daḥraḍ is water, and another water, and a group, and it is said water of Banī Sa‘d, and it is said a land. Al-Daḥraḍayn is the dual of al-Daḥraḍ like al-Qamarayn. Al-zawrā’ is the crooked, al-Daylam is a group of Turks likened to enemies, and it is said a land.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage details a usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is over the meaning of باء in the Quranic verse of wiping the head (المائدة:6). The Shafi'is claim that the باء indicates تبعيض (partiality), meaning wipe some of your heads. The author firmly rejects this, stating that the scholars of Arabic have denied this meaning. He then addresses a counter-argument from the Minhaj that the grammarians' denial is testimony about a negative and should not be accepted. The author refutes this by comparing it to the testimony of heirs' inventory, which is accepted. He further argues that it is not testimony but sound induction that yields certainty. Finally, he addresses the poetic evidence brought for تبعيض, explaining that it does not prove the meaning due to possibilities like زيادة and تضمين. This dispute has consequences for the fiqh ruling of wiping: Hanafis say it suffices to wipe a quarter of the head, while Shafi'is require wiping the entire head? Actually the Hanafi position is that wiping a quarter is sufficient, and the Shafi'i requires wiping the whole head or partial? But here the dispute is about linguistic meaning. The passage firmly upholds the Hanafi view that باء is for الإلصاق, not تبعيض.

Key Terms
التبعيض (al-tab‘īḍ): Indicating partiality or partitivity; the Shafi'i claim about باء.
الاستقراء (al-istiqrā’): Induction; the method used to prove that باء is not used for تبعيض.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary argument used by the Hanafis to refute the Shafi‘i claim that باء indicates partiality?
A) The verse is abrogated
B) Linguistic induction shows it is not used for partiality
C) The majority of scholars agree
D) The Shafi‘i claim contradicts hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul uses linguistic induction to establish that prepositions have fixed meanings; the burden of proof lies on those who claim a novel meaning.

Distinguishing Haqiqah and Majaz: The Word 'علي' in Debt vs. Deposit
A subsidiary ruling on the linguistic usage of 'علي' for debt and metaphor for deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the word 'علي' is used literally or metaphorically based on context and apply the Hanafi rule that a qarinah diverts from haqiqah to majaz.

Core Idea
The word 'علي' is literal for debt but metaphorical for deposit due to the qarinah of safekeeping.

In this lesson, we examine a subsidiary ruling from Hanafi usul al-fiqh that illustrates how the same word can shift from literal to metaphorical meaning depending on context. The key is identifying the linguistic indicator (qarinah) that controls the interpretation. We will parse the usage of 'علي' in two common phrases and understand why Hanafi scholars classify them differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic context
Determine whether the phrase involves debt (dayn) or deposit (wadi'ah). This context is the first clue.
Debt: 'له علي ألف' – deposit: 'عليه وديعة'.
2
Recall the literal meaning of 'علي'
In Hanafi usul, 'علي' literally indicates obligation (luzum). This is its haqiqah.
3
Check for a qarinah (indicator)
Look for a contextual element that prevents the literal meaning. For deposit, the qarinah is 'wujub al-hifz' (obligation of safekeeping), which is not a debt obligation.
4
Classify the usage
If a qarinah is present, the usage is majaz (metaphorical); if absent, it is haqiqah (literal).
5
Apply the ruling
Conclude that 'علي' in debt is haqiqah (binding acknowledgment), in deposit is majaz (not binding as debt, but as a responsibility to preserve).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع . في قول رجل له علي ألف يلزم (الدين ويكون إقراراً به لكونه حقيقة في اللزوم وهو كونه ديناً ولو) وصل وديعة تعين (المجاز لوجود الصارف (وهو وجوب الحفظ). ليس ممتداً
Subsidiary ruling: In the saying of a man, 'I owe a thousand,' the debt becomes binding and it is an acknowledgment of it because it is literal in obligation, i.e., it being a debt. And if a deposit arrives, the metaphorical usage is specified due to the presence of an indicator, which is the obligation of safekeeping. It is not extended.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a subsidiary ruling (far') on the linguistic analysis of the word 'علي' (upon/owe). When a person says 'له علي ألف' (I owe a thousand), the word 'علي' is used in its literal sense (haqiqah) to denote an obligatory debt; thus it constitutes an acknowledgment (iqrar) and the debt is binding. However, when the word 'علي' is used in the context of a deposit (wadi'ah), as in 'عليه وديعة' (he has a deposit), it is metaphorical (majaz) because the obligation of safekeeping (wujub al-hifz) acts as a linguistic indicator (qarinah) that diverts the meaning away from obligation to mere possession. The phrase 'ليس ممتداً' likely means this ruling does not extend to other cases.

Key Terms
علي ('ala): A preposition indicating obligation (luzum) when used for debt; for deposit it is metaphorical.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal usage; the word 'علي' for debt is used in its original sense.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; 'علي' for deposit is not literal due to the qarinah of safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'علي وديعة' (I have a deposit), how is the word 'علي' used according to Hanafi usul?
A) Literally, as an obligation
B) Metaphorically, because the obligation of safekeeping diverts the meaning
C) As a homonym with no fixed meaning
D) As a command to give

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the same word can be literal or metaphorical depending on the presence of a qarinah; 'علي' is literal for debt and metaphorical for deposit.

The Homonymy of the Particle 'من': A Usul Dispute
A dispute on the meanings of the particle 'min' and the argument for homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three proposed meanings of 'min' and explain why the Hanafi usul view holds it to be homonymous (mushtarak) based on tabaddur and istiqra'.

Core Idea
The particle 'min' is mushtarak among partitivity, explanation, and beginning of endpoint—context determines meaning.

In this passage, the author addresses a classic dispute in usul al-fiqh: what does the particle 'min' (من) mean? Many jurists insisted on a single meaning, but the Hanafi approach—backed by linguistic induction—recognizes that 'min' is homonymous: it carries multiple distinct meanings, each immediately understood in its proper context. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for interpreting legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three competing opinions
Read the opening lines. List the three views: (1) many jurists: exclusively tab'id, (2) Fakhr al-Din: tabyin, (3) majority linguists: ibtida' al-ghayah.
Each group tried to refer all usages to a single meaning.
2
Understand the author's criticism
The author calls these interpretations 'forced' (ta'assuf). Find the examples he gives (rental and taking dirhams) to see why forcing a single meaning fails.
In rental, 'min' clearly marks the starting point, not partitivity.
3
Follow the argument for homonymy
The author uses 'tabaddur' (immediate understanding): in different contexts, different meanings come to mind first. This proves 'min' is mushtarak, not a single word with a common denominator.
The common denominator view is refuted because then all contexts would produce the same immediate understanding.
4
Examine the inductive rule (istiqra')
The author states that if the verb involves traversing distance, 'min' indicates ibtida' al-ghayah; if it involves consumption, it indicates tab'id. But he qualifies this with the Quranic counterexample.
The verse 'and she was of the devout' shows tab'id without consumption.
5
Compare with al-Tahrir and conclude
The author says his position is 'more appropriate than what is in al-Tahrir'. Reflect on why the homonymy view is preferred for legal interpretation.
This highlights the Hanafi commitment to linguistic reality over theoretical uniformity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة من اختلف فيها فكثير من الفقهاء قالوا : إنها للتبعيض فقط وقال فخر الدين : للتبيين وقال جمهور أئمة اللغة : لابتداء الغاية أي الشيء ذي الغاية زماناً كان أو مكاناً على الصحيح لا كما زعم البعض أنه لابتداء الغاية المكانية وأرجعوا معانيها واقعة في الاستعمالات إلى ما ذهبوا إليه، والحق أن التبعيض والتبيين في نحو أجرت من شهر كذا إلى شهر كذا والابتداء في نحو أخذت من الدراهم تعسف أما الأولان في المثال الأول فلأنه من البين أن الغرض في الإجارة بهذا الكلام التحديد من ابتداء الشهر، وأما الأخير فلأن الأخذ حتى يتحدد بل كلمة من مشترك بين المعاني للتبادر أي لأن الكل يتبادر في مواضعه، فلا احتمال لكونه مجازاً في أحدهما وحقيقة في الآخر، فإما أن يكون موضوعاً بإزاء القدر المشترك أو بإزاء كل والأول باطل وإلا لتبادر منه فتعين الثاني. فإن قلت لا احتمال هنا للقدر المشترك، فإن وضع الحروف لمفهومات جزئية ملحوظة بوجه كلي، قلت أنه لا يضر، فالمراد أن يكون موضوعاً لإفراد المقدر المشترك بوضع واحد أو بإزاء أفراد كل مما ذكرنا من المفهومات الثلاثة بأوضاع والأول باطل وإلا لتبادر الأفراد باعتبار الاشتراك في هذا القدر المشترك، فتعين الثاني فتدبر وهذا أولى مما في «التحرير»، واستقراء مواقعها يفيد أن متعلقها إن تعلق مسافة كسرت وبعت فلابتداء الغاية وإن أفاد تناولاً كأخذت وأكلت فلإيصاله إلى بعض مدخوله، ثم ساق الكلام، فإنه يرد عليه أنه لا يلزم للبعض أن يكون متعلقه مفيداً للتناول كما في قوله تعالى: (وَكَانَتْ مِنَ الْقَنِتِينَ) [التحريم: ١٢] فتدبر .
Issue: Concerning 'min' about which there is disagreement. Many jurists said it is exclusively for partitivity (tab'id). Fakhr al-Din said it is for explanation (tabyin). The majority of linguists said it is for the beginning of the endpoint (ibtida' al-ghayah), i.e., a thing having an endpoint, whether time or place, according to the correct view—not as some claimed that it is for the beginning of spatial endpoint. They referred its meanings found in usage to what they held. The truth is that [interpreting] partitivity and explanation in examples like 'I rented from such-and-such month to such-and-such month' and the beginning in 'I took from the dirhams' is forced. As for the first two in the first example, it is clear that the purpose in rental with this speech is specification from the beginning of the month. As for the latter, because taking is [limited] until it is specified. Rather, the word 'min' is homonymous (mushtarak) among the meanings due to immediate understanding (tabaddur), i.e., because each is immediately understood in its own context, so there is no possibility of it being metaphorical in one and literal in another. Thus it must either be placed for a common denominator or placed for each meaning. The first is invalid, otherwise it would be immediately understood from it, so the second is determined. If you say: there is no possibility here of a common denominator because particles are placed for particular concepts considered in a universal aspect, I say: that does not harm. The intent is that it is placed either for the individuals of the common denominator with a single placement, or for the individuals of each of the three concepts we mentioned with multiple placements. The first is invalid, otherwise the individuals would be immediately understood due to the shared common denominator, so the second is determined. Reflect. This is more appropriate than what is in al-Tahrir. Induction (istiqra') of its uses indicates that if its dependent is a distance like 'I broke and sold' then it is for beginning of endpoint; if it indicates consumption like 'I took' and 'I ate' then it is for reaching part of its object. Then he continued the discussion. It is objected to him that it is not necessary for partitivity that its dependent indicate consumption, as in His saying (and she was of the devout) [66:12]. So reflect.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the particle 'min'. The author presents three views: (1) many jurists: 'min' is exclusively for partitivity (tab'id), (2) Fakhr al-Din al-Razi: it is for explanation (tabyin), (3) majority of linguists: it is for beginning of endpoint (ibtida' al-ghayah) in time or place. The author argues that forcing any one meaning onto all usages is strained. Instead, 'min' is homonymous (mushtarak) among these meanings, as each is immediately understood (tabaddur) in its proper context. Therefore, it cannot be a single word with a common denominator; it must be multiple words (homonyms). The author then discusses the position of al-Tahrir (by Kamal ibn al-Hammam) and criticizes it. The correct view is that the meaning of 'min' depends on the context: if the verb involves traversing distance (like 'sold' or 'broke'), it indicates beginning of endpoint; if it involves consuming (like 'took' or 'ate'), it indicates reaching a part of the object. However, this is not universal, as the verse 'and she was of the devout' shows 'min' indicating partitivity without consumption.

Key Terms
من (min): A particle homonymous among tab'id (partitivity), tabyin (explanation), and ibtida' al-ghayah (beginning of endpoint).
تبعيض (tab'id): Partitivity; indicating a part of a whole.
تبيين (tabyin): Explanation; specifying what follows as clarifying the preceding.
ابتداء الغاية (ibtida' al-ghayah): Beginning of the endpoint; indicating the start of a temporal or spatial limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the author reject the view that 'min' has a single common denominator?
A) Because 'min' is always literal (haqiqah) and cannot be metaphorical
B) Because each meaning is immediately understood in its context, so homonymy is necessary
C) Because grammar rules forbid a common denominator
D) Because only Fakhr al-Din's view is correct

Key Takeaway
The particle 'min' is not a single word with one meaning; it is homonymous among tab'id, tabyin, and ibtida' al-ghayah, and its interpretation in legal texts depends entirely on context.
