The Definite Article with Plural: Istighraq, 'Ahd, and Jins
Explains the hierarchy of meanings: istighraq, 'ahd, jins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting the definite article 'al' with plural nouns by distinguishing between istighraq, 'ahd dhihni, and jins, and evaluate the dispute with Taftazani.

Core Idea
When 'al' enters a plural and no external group is known, it indicates generality first; if impossible, it indicates the genus—never the mentally known.

In this passage, the author establishes the precise hierarchy for the definite article when attached to a plural noun. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correct interpretation of Quranic verses and legal texts. We will see how induction (istiqra') and logical reasoning support the Hanafi position against the objection of Taftazani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible meanings
The definite article with plural can indicate: (1) a known specific group (al-'ahd al-khariji), (2) all individuals (al-istighraq), or (3) the genus (al-jins). A fourth category, al-'ahd al-dhihni (mentally known), is rejected by the Hanafis.
2
Understand the hierarchy
If an external known group exists, 'al' indicates that group. If not, then generality (istighraq) is given precedence when possible. If generality is impossible (e.g., the predicate cannot apply to all individuals), then the article indicates the genus.
Generality is impossible when the statement is not about each individual, e.g., 'ar-rijalu khairun min an-nisa' does not mean every single man is better than every single woman.
3
Analyze the refutation of Taftazani
Taftazani argued that the mentally known ('ahd dhihni) takes precedence over generality. The author refutes this by showing that 'ahd dhihni has no real benefit—it is like an indefinite. The property of 'al' is to nullify plurality, and keeping the plural would nullify the article.
The logical argument: if you keep the plural meaning, the article is cancelled; if you change to singular, plurality is lost. The genus meaning preserves both partially.
4
Apply the rule to examples
In 'ar-rijalu khairun min an-nisa', 'al' cannot indicate generality (not all men are better) nor an external group, so it indicates genus. In 'rakibtu al-khayl' (I rode horses), the article also indicates genus, not a mentally known group, because the act of riding applies to some horses, not a specific imagined set.
These examples fall under the genus meaning per the Hanafi position.
5
Extend to definite construct phrases
The same categories and rulings apply when the plural is mudaf to a definite (e.g., 'ghulamu al-rajuli'). The rule is analogous: without a known group, the article indicates generality or genus.
The author states: 'This, and the categories and rulings mentioned concerning the definite article apply exactly to [a noun] added to a definite [genitive], so draw an analogy upon it.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التفاوت في الاصطلاح، ولا يجب تطابقه فسقط ما في التلويح أن الحكم بتقديم المعهود الذهني على الاستغراق مما لم يذهب إليه أحد ولا هو صحيح في نفسه، فإن جهة تقدم العهد على الجنس، إنما هو انتفاء الفائدة في إرادة الجنس والعهد الذهني مما لا فائدة فيه أصلا فإنه معنى كالنكرة فافهم وإذا دخل اللام على الجمع ولم يكن هناك جماعة معهودة أبطل اللام معنى الجمعية وأفاد استغراق الآحاد إن أمكن، لأنه الراجح كما تقدم، وإلا أفاد تعريف الجنس سواء تحقق في واحد أو أكثر، وسواء كان مناط الحكم نفسه مع قطع النظر عن الأشخاص كما في الرجال خير من النساء، أو كان مناط الحكم الجنس من حيث تحققه في فرد ما، كما ركبت الخيل ولبست الثياب، فدخل في هذا أمثلة العهد الذهني وسقط ما اعترض به في التلويح من أن مثل ركبت الخيل من قبيل المعهود الذهني الذي فيه الإشارة إلى حصة ما معهودة في الذهن وإذ قد عدّ في المعرف بلام الجنس فأي معنى للمعهود الذهني الذي هو مقدم على الاستغراق وأي شيء هو فافهم والدليل عليه شهادة الاستقراء، فإنه عند عدم إمكان العهد والاستغراق يتبادر الجنس في كلام الله وكلام الفصحاء ، وأيضاً استدلوا بأنه لو لم يحمل على الجنس وبقي الجمع فيه على الجمعية لغا حرف العهد أعني اللام وإن حمل على الجنس المقيد بالوحدة لغا الصيغة، فلا بد من الحمل على الجنس فيه ليبقى اللام معمولاً ويكون مدخوله أقرب إلى الجمع، لأنه كما يصدق على الواحد يصدق على الجماعة، واعترض عليه بأنه لم لا يحمل على المعهود الذهني ويشار إلى جماعة ما معهودة في الذهن، فحينئذ يبقى اللام والجمع كلاهما على معنييهما وجوابه أنك قد عرفت أن من خواص اللام أن يبطل الجمعية، فلو أبقى الجمع لنا قطعاً وأيضاً أن العهد الذهني عهد تقديري، وليس معناه حقيقة، ولذا يعامل بالمعهود الذهني معاملة النكرة فلغا قطعاً، والحاصل أن اللام يبطل الجمعية بالاستقراء الغير المكذوب، فلو أبقى الجمع على معناه الجمعي بطل اللام بالكلية ولا يظهر له أثر في تغيير المدخول، ولو غير إلى معنى المفرد بطل الجمعية بالكلية، ويصير مجازاً أبعد، فحمل الجمع على الجنس الشامل للواحد والكثير من قبيل عموم المجاز، وصار المجاز أقرب إلى الحقيقة هذا والأقسام والأحكام التي ذكرت في اللام بعينها جارية في المضاف إلى المعرفة فقسه عليه
Regarding the difference in terminology, and its correspondence is not necessary, so what is in al-Talwih falls, that the ruling of giving precedence to the mentally known (al-ma'had al-dhihni) over generality (al-istighraq) is something no one has espoused and it is not correct in itself. For the reason for precedence of [specific] known over genus is merely the lack of benefit in intending the genus, and the mentally known has no benefit at all, as it is a meaning like an indefinite noun, so understand. And when the definite article enters upon a plural and there is no known group, the definite article nullifies the meaning of plurality and benefits generality of individuals if possible, because it is the preponderant as previously; otherwise it benefits definition of the genus, whether it is realized in one or more, and whether the basis of the ruling itself regardless of persons as in 'men are better than women', or whether the basis of the ruling is the genus in terms of its realization in some individual, as in 'I rode horses' and 'I wore clothes'. So examples of the mentally known enter into this, and the objection in al-Talwih falls, that such as 'I rode horses' is of the type of mentally known which points to a specific portion known in the mind, and since it has been counted among what is defined by the definite article of genus, then what meaning is there for the mentally known which is given precedence over generality? And what is it? So understand. The evidence for it is the testimony of induction: for when the [specific] known and generality are not possible, the genus immediately appears in the speech of Allah and the speech of eloquent speakers. Also they argued that if it were not taken to mean genus and the plural were kept on its plurality, the letter of definite article would be canceled; and if it were taken to mean genus restricted by oneness, the form would be canceled. So it must be taken to mean genus so that the definite article remains operative and its entry is closest to the plural, because as it applies to one it applies to a group. It was objected: why not take it as mentally known and point to some group known in the mind? Then both the definite article and the plural would keep their meanings. The answer is that you have known that it is a property of the definite article to nullify plurality; so if we kept the plural, we would definitely lose; and also the mentally known is a hypothetical known, not a true meaning; therefore it is treated like an indefinite and becomes null definitely. The upshot is that the definite article nullifies plurality by undisputed induction; so if we kept the plural on its collective meaning, the definite article would be completely nullified and would show no effect in changing the entry; and if we changed it to the meaning of singular, the plurality would be completely nullified and it would become a more distant metaphor. So taking the plural to mean the genus encompassing one and many is a kind of universal metaphor, and the metaphor becomes closer to reality. This, and the categories and rulings mentioned concerning the definite article apply exactly to [a noun] added to a definite [genitive], so draw an analogy upon it.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the meaning of the definite article 'al' when attached to a plural noun. The author establishes a hierarchy: first, if there is a known specific group (al-'ahd), the article refers to that group. If not, then it indicates generality (istighraq) if possible. If neither is possible, it indicates the genus (jins). He refutes Taftazani's claim in al-Talwih that the 'mentally known' (al-'ahd al-dhihni) is given precedence over generality, arguing that mental reference has no real benefit and is like an indefinite. The proof is inductive: when context prevents both a known group and all-inclusive generality, the genus is immediately understood, as in 'men are better than women' or 'I rode horses'. The article nullifies the collective meaning of the plural; keeping the plural would cancel the article, while changing to singular nullifies plurality. Thus the genus interpretation is the closest metaphor to reality. This rule also applies to a definite construct phrase (mudaf ila ma'rifa), so the same categories and rulings run in it.

Key Terms
اللام (al-lam): The definite article that when attached to a plural noun nullifies the collective meaning and can indicate generality (istighraq), a specific known group ('ahd), or the genus (jins), depending on possibility.
العهد الذهني (al-'ahd al-dhihni): A hypothetical mental reference to a specific but not externally known group, treated like an indefinite and having no real benefit.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): The meaning of the definite article that includes all individuals of the plural (generality).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the definite article 'al' is attached to a plural noun and there is no known group nor possibility of generality (istighraq), what does it indicate according to the Hanafi position?
A) The mentally known ('ahd dhihni)
B) The specific group ('ahd khariji)
C) The genus (jins)
D) Nullification of the article

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definite article with plural indicates either a known group, generality, or genus, in strict order, and the mentally known is rejected.

Definition of 'Ayy' and Pronoun Agreement
Defines the partitive noun 'ayy' and its grammatical behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the partitive meaning of 'ayy' based on the definiteness of its governed noun and apply the Hanafi rule of pronoun agreement.

Core Idea
Ayy denotes a part of the whole when definite, an individual when indefinite; pronoun agrees with the governed noun.

This passage introduces the Hanafi usul definition of the partitive noun 'ayy' and clarifies how its meaning shifts with definiteness. It also establishes a key grammatical rule for pronoun agreement, which is essential for interpreting legal expressions. Understanding this foundation is crucial before delving into the dispute about generality that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two states of 'ayy'
When 'ayy' is annexed to a definite noun (e.g., 'ayyu al-rijali'), it indicates a part of that set. When annexed to an indefinite noun (e.g., 'ayyu rajulin'), it indicates any single individual.
This distinction is based on the definiteness of the mudaf ilayh.
2
Apply the pronoun agreement rule
When the governed noun is indefinite, the pronoun must agree with it because 'ayy' stands for that individual. When it is definite, 'ayy' does not stand for the whole but a part, so the pronoun agrees with the entire governed noun set.
Example: 'ayyu rajulin darabaka' → 'darabaka' (singular); 'ayyu al-rijali darabaka' → 'darabaka' (singular referring to the set).
3
Note the Hanafi linguistic principle
The Hanafi school treats 'ayy' as a partitive noun by default, not as a generalizing particle. This affects how conditional statements in legal texts are interpreted.
This principle contrasts with views that 'ayy' is originally general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (أيّ) لجزء المضاف إليه معرفاً ولجزئي منه نكرة فمعنى أي الرجال أي جزء من مجموع الرجال، ومعنى أي رجل أي واحد من أفراد رجل ويجب مطابقة الضمير للمضاف إليه في (الثاني) أي فيما إذا كان نكرة لأنه حينئذٍ عبارة عنه فيقال: أي رجل ضربك وأتي رجال ضربوك (و) يجب مطابقة الضمير له في الأول أي فيما إذا كان معرفة، فيقال: أي الرجل وأي الرجال ضربك، لأنه حينئذ ليس عبارة عنه، بل عن جزء من أجزائه (قيل : يعم) أي (بالوصف) كما هو شأن سائر النكرات (وقيل) لا ، بل وضع ابتداء للعموم) للفرق الظاهر بين قولنا : أعط رجلاً جاءك للسؤال، وأتي رجل جاءك للسؤال، وعند خصوص الوصف يخص، أما عند الفريق الأول فظاهر، وأما عند هذا الفريق فلأنه صارف له عن العموم. (فرع. يعتق الكل إذا ضربوا في قوله : أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر) لأن الوصف وهو الضرب عام لهم فعند وجود الشرط يتناول حكم الحرية كلهم ( بخلاف في ضربته) كلمة في من سهو الناسخ أي هذا بخلاف أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر فإنه لا يعتق فيه، إلا الأول في صورة (الترتيب أو ما يعينه المولى في صورة المعية لأن الوصف لغيرهم) وهو المخاطب، لأنه الضارب (وهو خاص) فلا يعم الحكم (وأورد) عليه لا نسلم أن الوصف لغيرهم، بل الوصف لهم وهو المضروبية وعام كيف المضروبية تعم كالضاربية) وهي صفة لهم (فافهم) وتفصيل هذا المقام يطلب من الكتب المبسوطة للكرام .
Issue: (Ayy) [indicates] a part of the governed noun when definite, and a part of it [in a different sense] when indefinite. So the meaning of 'ayyu al-rijali' is any part of the totality of men, and the meaning of 'ayyu rajulin' is any one individual of 'rajul'. And pronoun agreement is required with the governed noun in the second case — i.e., when it is indefinite — because then it is an expression for it; so one says: 'ayyu rajulin darabaka' and 'ayyu rijaalin darabooka'. And pronoun agreement with it is required in the first case — i.e., when it is definite; so one says: 'ayyu al-rajuli wa ayyu al-rijaali darabaka' because then it is not an expression for him but for a part of his parts. (It was said: it is general) i.e., by description, as is the case with other indefinites. (And it was said: No, rather it is originally placed for generality) due to the apparent difference between our saying: 'give a man who came to you' for request, and 'any man who came to you' for request. When the description is particular, it particularizes. As for the first group, it is apparent; as for this group, it is because it diverts it from generality. (Branch: All are freed if they beat in his statement: 'Any of my slaves who beats you is free') because the description — beating — is general for them, so when the condition occurs, the ruling of freedom applies to all of them. (Unlike 'in I beat him'?) The word 'in' is from the scribe's oversight, i.e., this is unlike 'any of my slaves whom I beat is free' because then only the first is freed in the case of order, or what the master specifies in the case of simultaneity, because the description is for others — i.e., the addressee, since he is the beater — and it is specific, so the ruling is not general. (And it was objected: we do not concede that the description is for others; rather the description is for them — being beaten — and it is general; how can being beaten be general like beating?) It is a description for them; so understand. And the details of this topic are sought from the detailed books of the noble ones.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'ayy' (any/which) as a partitive noun. When the governed noun is definite (e.g., 'ayyu al-rijali'), it means any part of that definite set; when indefinite, it means any single individual. Pronoun agreement differs accordingly. There is a dispute: some say 'ayy' with an indefinite governed noun is general by virtue of being indefinite (like other indefinites), while others say it is originally placed for generality, and a particular description can restrict it. The branch applies this to manumission by conditional statement: if a master says 'any of my slaves who beats you is free,' and they all beat, all are freed because the description (beating) is general. But if he says 'any of my slaves whom I beat is free,' only the first beaten is freed (if sequential) or as the master specifies (if simultaneous), because the description (being beaten) is specific to the one beaten. An objection is raised that 'being beaten' can also be general, but the author dismisses it.

Key Terms
أي (ayy): A partitive noun meaning 'any' or 'which'; indicates a part of the mudaf ilayh when definite, an individual when indefinite
مطابقة الضمير (mutabaqat al-damir): Agreement of pronoun with the governed noun in gender and number

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'أي رجل جاءك' (ayyu rajulin ja'aka), what does 'ayy' indicate?
A) A part of a specific group of men
B) Any single individual man
C) All men collectively
D) A specific man known to the speaker

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'ayy' as a partitive noun governs its meaning and pronoun agreement, forming the basis for interpreting conditional legal statements.

Generality of 'Ayy': Dispute and Application in Manumission
Presents a dispute on the basis of generality of 'ayy' and its application in a manumission case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two views on whether 'ayy' with an indefinite governed noun is general by description or originally general, and apply the Hanafi position to a manumission case.

Core Idea
The generality of 'ayy' hinges on the description; when the description is particular, the ruling is particular.

After establishing the basic definition, the passage presents a dispute about the source of generality in 'ayy' when used with an indefinite governed noun. The Hanafi view holds that it is general by description (like other indefinites), while an opposing view claims it is originally placed for generality. This theoretical difference has a concrete impact on a manumission case involving conditional statements, as illustrated by the branch.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First position: 'ayy' with an indefinite governed noun is general by virtue of the description (al-wasf). Second position: 'ayy' itself is originally placed for generality, and a particular description restricts it.
The dispute is about the default status: is it general inherently or via description?
2
Analyze the manumission branch
If a master says 'Any of my slaves who beats you is free' (using 'ayy' with definite governed noun 'عبيدي'), and all slaves beat, all are freed because the description 'beats' applies to all. If he says 'Any of my slaves whom I beat is free', only the first beaten is freed (if sequential) or as specified, because the description 'I beat' is particular to the one beaten.
The definiteness of the governed noun here is actually with possessive pronoun, so it is definite. So the rule from previous lesson applies: 'ayy' indicates a part. But the description's scope determines the ruling.
3
Understand the objection
An objection is raised: why is 'being beaten' not considered a general description? The author counters that the description is for others (the master), not for the slaves, so it remains specific.
This highlights the importance of attributing the description correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Issue: (Ayy) [indicates] a part of the governed noun when definite, and a part of it [in a different sense] when indefinite. According to the Hanafi view in the dispute, why is 'ayy' with an indefinite governed noun general?
A) Because 'ayy' itself is a general word
B) Because of the description that accompanies it
C) Because it is an indefinite noun
D) Because of the context of the statement

The Reality of Synonymy in Language
Proves existence of synonymy in language by inductive necessity and rhetorical benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on synonymy, identify its proofs, and evaluate the objection that synonymy is useless.

Core Idea
Synonymy exists in language, proven by inductive necessity and serving rhetorical goals beyond mere definition.

This passage addresses a foundational linguistic issue in usul al-fiqh: whether two words can have identical meanings. Some deny this, arguing it would be pointless. The Hanafis respond with both inductive evidence and the practical benefits of synonymy in rhetoric, poetry, and Quranic style. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyze textual commands in istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Define synonymy and antonymy
Identify the two categories of word pairs from the passage: synonyms (identical concepts) and antonyms (different concepts). Note the examples: burr/qamh (synonyms), natiq/fasih (near-synonyms), human/horse (antonyms).
The definition guards against terms like 'definition and defined' which are not synonyms.
2
State the dispute
A group denied synonymy on the grounds that it would be useless: once one word defines a meaning, another adds nothing. Understand their entailment.
This is a dispute about linguistic reality, not a specific fiqh ruling.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response (first part)
The Hanafis argue that even if one definition suffices, the other is not absolutely useless because each word still defines (just not a new definition). The objection only negates one definition after another, not all benefit.
This is a logical counter: rejection of the claim of total futility.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (second part)
The main benefits are rhetorical: saj' (rhymed prose), tajanus (paronomasia), preserving meter in poetry, and qalb (inversion). Examples from Arabic usage and Quran are given.
These benefits are not limited to definition; they enhance style and meaning.
5
Note the practical consequence in usul
The existence of synonymy affects how we interpret multiple textual commands. If two words are truly synonyms, they do not create separate obligations. The Hanafi rule is that synonymy is contrary to the original principle, so when in doubt, prefer the non-synonymous interpretation (e.g., metaphor).
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the applied impact on istinbat.

(الفصل الرابع) (وهو) أي المفرد (بالقياس إلى لفظ آخر إما (مرادف له أو (مباين) له (لأنه إما أن يتحد مفهومهما من كل وجه احترز به عن نحو الحد والمحدود (كالبر والقمح أولا) يتحد مفهومهما، بل يتعدد وإن كان من وجه ما سواء صدقا على ذات كالناطق والفصيح) أولا، كالإنسان والفرس، فعلى الأول هما مترادفان وعلى الثاني متباينان . مسألة (الترادف واقع) في اللغة (بالضرورة الاستقرائية كالتأكيد أي كما أن التأكيد واقع بالضرورة (خلافاً لقوم لا يعبأ بهم (قالوا) لو وقع لوقع من غير فائدة واللازم باطل، أما الملازمة فلأن الواحد قد حصل به التعريف للمعنى فالآخر أيضاً لتعريف هذا المعنى، و(لا فائدة في تعريف المعرف (قلنا غاية ما لزم انتفاء تعريف واحد بعد تعريف الآخر، ولا يلزم منه انتفاء الفائدة مطلقاً، إذ لا ينتفي التعريف أي تعريف كل (بدلاً على أن فائدته في المحسنات البديعية (لا تخفى على المتتبع ولا نسلم أيضاً أن الفائدة منحصرة في تعريف المعنى، وتلك المحسنات كالسجع) في قولك : ما أبعد ما فات وما أقرب ما هو آت ولو كان لفظ انقضى فقط مرفوعاً لهذ المعنى لفات السجع (وكالمجانسة، كقولك: اشتريت البر وأنفقته في البر) ولو أقيم لفظ القمح مقام البرّ فاتت ومنه قول العارف الكامل الشيخ ابن الفارض الحموي قدس الله سره : فطوفان نوح عـنـد نـوحـي كـأدمـعـي وإيقاد نيران الخليل كلوعتي ومنه ظهر فائدة أخرى من المحافظة على الوزن (وكالقلب نحو) قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَّكَ فكير [المدثر : ۳] دونه في فعظم (نعم) هو ) أي الترادف (على خلاف الأصل حتى إذا تردد لفظ) بينه وبين غيره كالمجاز فيحمل على غيره لقلته بالنسبة إلى أغياره.
The Fourth Chapter: (And it is) i.e., a single word (in relation to another word) either (synonymous with it or) (antonymous with it) (because either their concepts are identical in every respect — guarding against terms like definition and defined — (like al-burr and al-qamh) or (their concepts are not identical, rather multiple, even if in some respect they apply to the same essence, like al-natiq and al-fasih) or not, like human and horse. In the first case they are synonyms, in the second antonyms. Issue: (Synonymy is real) in language (by inductive necessity) like emphasis — i.e., just as emphasis is necessarily real, (contrary to a group who are not regarded). (They said: if it occurred, it would occur without benefit, and the necessary is false. As for the entailment, because one word has achieved the definition of the meaning, so the other also defines this meaning, and (there is no benefit in defining what is already defined). We say: at most what follows is the negation of one definition after the other, and from that the negation of benefit absolutely does not follow, since definition — i.e., definition of each — is not negated. (And also) its benefit lies in rhetorical embellishments (which are not hidden from the investigator), and we do not concede that benefit is limited to defining meaning. Those embellishments such as (sa_j') in your saying: 'How distant is what has passed and how near is what is coming'; if only the word 'inqada' were used for that meaning, the sa_j' would be lost. (And like tajanus) in your saying: 'I bought wheat (al-burr) and spent it in piety (al-birr)'; if the word al-qamh were substituted for al-burr, it would be lost. Among them is the saying of the perfect mystic Sheikh Ibn al-Farid al-Hamawi, may Allah sanctify his soul: ... And from it another benefit appears of preserving meter. (And like qalb) towards His saying, exalted: 'And your Lord, magnify' [al-Muddaththir:3] — without it in 'fa'zum'? (Yes, it is) i.e., synonymy (is contrary to the original principle, so when a word is ambiguous) between it and something else, like metaphor, it is interpreted as the other due to its scarcity relative to its alternatives.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage begins by defining synonyms and antonyms: if two words have identical concepts in every respect (e.g., burr and qamh for wheat), they are synonyms; if concepts differ even if sometimes applying to the same essence (e.g., natiq and fasih), they are not synonyms but near-synonyms; if completely different (human/horse), antonyms. Then it addresses the reality of synonymy. Some deny synonymy claiming it is pointless. The Hanafi response is multiple: (1) even if one definition is sufficient, the other is not useless; (2) synonyms serve rhetorical purposes like saj' (rhymed prose), tajanus (paronomasia), preserving meter in poetry, and qalb (inversion). An example from Qur'an is given. Finally, he notes that synonymy is contrary to the original principle (because language normally has one word per meaning), so when a word is ambiguous between synonymy and metaphor, it is interpreted as metaphor due to scarcity of true synonyms.

Key Terms
ترادف (taraduf): Two or more words with identical meaning in all respects
تباين (tabayun): Words with different meanings
محسنات بديعية (muhassinat badi'iyya): Rhetorical embellishments such as saj', tajanus, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example of tajanus (paronomasia) is given to show the benefit of synonymy?
A) 'How distant is what has passed...'
B) 'I bought wheat and spent it in piety'
C) 'And your Lord, magnify'
D) The poetry of Ibn al-Farid

Key Takeaway
Synonymy is a linguistic reality proven by inductive necessity and serves crucial rhetorical functions; it is not a useless redundancy.

Classification of Single Words: Synonyms vs. Different in Hanafi Usul
Defines two categories of single word relations: synonymous and different

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any two words as synonymous (murādif) or different (mubāyin) using the Hanafi usul criterion of complete conceptual identity.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, synonyms are words with identical concepts from every aspect; all other word pairs are different (mubāyin).

Understanding when two words are synonyms or different is foundational for interpreting legal texts. The Hanafi school employs a precise criterion: complete conceptual identity. This lesson unpacks the definition with examples from both pure synonyms and words that only partially overlap.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two words being compared
Take the two words and recognize their lexical meanings. For example, al-burr and al-qamḥ both refer to wheat.
2
Check conceptual identity from every aspect
Determine whether the two words share exactly the same meaning without any difference in nuance, scope, or connotation.
The phrase 'from every aspect' (min kulli wajh) is crucial—partial overlap does not count.
3
Classify as murādif or mubāyin
If the concepts are fully identical, they are murādif (synonyms). Otherwise, they are mubāyin (different).
Al-burr and al-qamḥ are murādif; al-nāṭiq and al-faṣīḥ are mubāyin.
4
Handle edge cases: shared referent but different concepts
Words like al-nāṭiq (rational) and al-faṣīḥ (eloquent) both apply to humans but have distinct meanings. They are mubāyin, not synonyms.
Do not confuse co-extensiveness with synonymy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع (وهو) أي المفرد (بالقياس إلى لفظ آخر إما (مرادف له أو (مباين) له (لأنه إما أن يتحد مفهومهما من كل وجه احترز به عن نحو الحد والمحدود (كالبر والقمح أولا) يتحد مفهومهما، بل يتعدد وإن كان من وجه ما سواء صدقا على ذات كالناطق والفصيح) أولا، كالإنسان والفرس، فعلى الأول هما مترادفان وعلى الثاني متباينان .
The fourth chapter: (It) i.e., the single word (in relation to another word is either (synonymous with it or (different from it (because either their concepts are identical from every aspect—this excludes the definition and the defined, like wheat and wheat—or first) their concepts are not identical but multiple, even if from one aspect they both apply to the same essence, like the articulate and the eloquent, or not, like man and horse. In the first case they are synonyms, in the second they are different.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage sets up a fundamental classification in usul al-fiqh: when comparing two single words, they are either synonyms (maradif) or different (mubayin). The author explains that synonyms are words whose concepts are identical from every angle, giving the example of 'al-burr' and 'al-qamh' (both mean wheat). Different words are those whose concepts are not identical even if they sometimes refer to the same object, like 'al-natiq' (rational) and 'al-fasih' (eloquent)—both apply to humans but have distinct meanings. If they do not even share the same object, like 'al-insan' (human) and 'al-faras' (horse), they are clearly different. This classification is essential for later discussions on literal and metaphorical meaning, and for understanding when words can be substituted for one another.

Key Terms
مرادف (murādif): A word that is identical in meaning to another word from every aspect
مباين (mubāyin): A word that differs in meaning from another, even if they sometimes apply to the same object

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) al-insān and al-faras
B) al-burr and al-qamḥ
C) al-nāṭiq and al-faṣīḥ
D) al-insān and al-nāṭiq

Key Takeaway
Two words are synonyms only if their concepts are identical in every respect; otherwise they are different (mubāyin).

Definition of 'Amm: Ibn al-Humam's Precision vs. Abu al-Husayn's Approach
Definition of 'amm and critique of two definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of 'amm according to Ibn al-Humam and explain why it improves upon Abu al-Husayn's definition.

Core Idea
'Amm indicates the encompassing of individuals of a concept, not any word that encompasses all it is fit for.

A precise definition of 'amm is fundamental to Hanafi usul. This passage compares two definitions, revealing subtle criteria for generality that have direct consequences for legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the two definitions
Identify the definitions of 'amm given by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and Ibn al-Humam.
Abu al-Husayn's definition: 'the word that encompasses all it is fit for' (with the added condition 'by one word formation' in al-Minhaj). Ibn al-Humam: 'what indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept.'
2
Analyze the problem with Abu al-Husayn's definition
Note that words like 'kull' and 'jami'' do not encompass all possible individuals, only those of the added noun. Also, numbers like 'ten' encompass parts, not individuals.
These cases show that Abu al-Husayn's definition is not universal.
3
Explain Ibn al-Humam's solution
His definition uses 'individuals of a concept', which correctly includes 'kull' (since it adds to a noun) and excludes numbers (since they refer to parts).
4
Understand the critique of the added condition in al-Minhaj
The condition 'by one word formation' was added to exclude homonyms used in multiple meanings, but the author critiques it as incomplete and inconsistent.
This shows the refinement process in Hanafi usul.
5
Conclude on the preferred definition
The text states Ibn al-Humam's definition is 'more correct' (aswab).
This is the Hanafi relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلقاً (الفصل الخامس) (وهو) أي المفرد باعتبار) وحدة المسمى المدلول له وتعدده خاص وعام، قال أبو الحسين البصري في تفسير العام :(العام) اللفظ المستغرق لما يصلح له، وزاد في المنهاج وقال : اللفظ المستغرق لما يصلح له بوضع واحد وإنما زاد لئلا يخرج عن الحد (المشترك إذا استغرق لما يصلح له باعتبار معنى واحد دون معنى آخر فإنه لا يستغرق لما يصلح له أنه عام (قيل) في حواشي ميرزاجان»: إنما زاد لذلك (ولئلا يدخل المشترك إذا مع أريد به جميع معانيه) . فإن إرادتها صحيحة عنده، فهو مستغرق لما يصلح له باعتبار الأوضاع (أقول) لا يصح إخراج هذا المشترك، فإنه من أفراد المحدود إذ قال في شرح المختصر العام : : عند الشافعي قسمان : قسم متفق الحقيقة، وقسم مختلف الحقيقة، يعني المشترك المستعمل في معانيها كلها وفيه ،نظر، أما أولاً : فإنه على هذا لا هذا التقييد أصلاً، وإن يصح كان للصيانة عن خروج المشترك المستغرق باعتبار معنى واحد فإنه كما يصونه عنه مخرج المشترك المستغرق لمعنيين، وأما ثانياً فلأن مقصوده أن المحدود القسم الخاص المتفق الحقيقة، فلا بد من إخراج القسم الآخر هذا واعلم أنه حدده الشيخ ابن الهمام بأن العام ما دل على استغراق أفراد مفهوم، وهذا أصوب من تعريف أبي الحسين، فإنه غير جامع للفظي كل وجميع فإنهما لا يستغرقان لما يصلحان له من الإفراد بل لإفراد ما أضيفا إليه، والمراد بالاستغراق أعم من الاستغراق الاجتماعي والانفرادي هذا ثم أورد نحو عشرة) فإنه مستغرق لما يصلح له من الآحاد التي هي أجزاؤه، ولا يتوجه هذا على تعريف الشيخ أصلاً لقوله : إفراد مفهوم، بدل قوله : ما يصلح له وأجيب بأن المراد بالصلوح المعتبر في الحد (صلوح الكلي للجزئيات لا للأجزاء، فالعام ما يستغرق لما يصلح له من الجزئيات فآل إلى تعريف الشيخ
Absolutely. The fifth chapter. (And it) i.e., the individual word, with respect to the unity and multiplicity of the named object, is particular and general. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said in the interpretation of the general: 'The general is the word that encompasses all it is fit for.' And he added in al-Minhaj and said: 'The word that encompasses all it is fit for by one word formation.' He added this so that it would not be excluded by the limit (the common word if it encompasses what it is fit for by one meaning without another meaning, for it does not encompass what it is fit for as a general term). (It was said) in the glosses of Mirzajan: 'He added it for that reason, and so that the common word would not enter if all its meanings are intended.' For intending them is correct according to him, so it is encompassing what it is fit for with respect to the word formations. (I say): This exclusion of the common word is not correct, for it is an instance of the defined term, since he said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: 'The general according to al-Shafi'i is of two types: a type of uniform nature, and a type of diverse nature,' meaning the common word used in all its meanings. And there is a consideration in it. Firstly, that based on this, there is no need for this restriction at all, and if it is correct, it is for the preservation from the exclusion of the common word that encompasses by one meaning, for just as it preserves from that, it excludes the common word encompassing two meanings. Secondly, because his intent is that the defined term is the specific type of uniform nature, so it is necessary to exclude the other type. And know that Shaykh Ibn al-Humam defined it as: 'The general is what indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept.' And this is more correct than the definition of Abu al-Husayn, for it does not include the words 'kull' and 'jami'' because they do not encompass what they are fit for in terms of individuals, but rather the individuals to which they are added. And 'encompassing' is more general than social encompassing and individual encompassing. Then he adduced the example of 'ten' for it encompasses the units that are its parts. And this does not apply to the Shaykh's definition at all, because of his saying 'individuals of a concept' instead of 'what it is fit for.' And it was answered that what is meant by 'fitness' in the definition is the fitness of the universal for particulars, not for parts. So the general is what encompasses what it is fit for in terms of particulars. Thus it resolves to the Shaykh's definition.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition lesson on the term 'amm (general). It begins by noting that individual words (mufrad) are divided into particular (khas) and general ('amm). Abu al-Husayn al-Basri defines 'amm as 'the word that encompasses all it is fit for.' A later addition in al-Minhaj specifies 'by one word formation' to exclude homonyms (mushtarak) that encompass multiple meanings but not in a single sense. The author (of the commentary) criticizes this addition, arguing that it does not properly exclude certain types of homonyms. He then presents Ibn al-Humam's definition: 'what indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept.' This is considered superior because it accounts for words like 'kull' (every) and 'jami'' (all), which do not encompass all possible individuals but rather the individuals of the thing they are added to. The definition of Abu al-Husayn is criticized for not covering such words. The passage also debates whether 'ten' is a general term; Ibn al-Humam's definition excludes it (since it deals with parts, not individuals), while Abu al-Husayn's might include it. The resolution is that 'fitness' in the definition refers to universals with particulars, not to parts, bringing it in line with Ibn al-Humam's definition. This lesson is crucial for understanding the precise definition of 'amm in Hanafi usul, which differs from other schools.

Key Terms
العام (al-'āmm): According to Ibn al-Humam: what indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept. According to Abu al-Husayn: the word that encompasses all it is fit for (with added condition of 'by one word formation').
الاستغراق (al-istighrāq): Encompassing; comprehensive coverage of all individuals or parts. In the definition of 'amm, it is the key property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Humam, why is the word 'ten' not considered a general term?
A) Because it is a number and cannot be general.
B) Because it encompasses its parts (units) not individuals of a concept.
C) Because it is always specific to a limited quantity.
D) Because it does not have a meaning when used alone.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'amm is precise: it indicates the encompassing of individuals of a concept, excluding numbers and properly including words like 'kull'.

Definitions of General ('Aam) in Hanafi Usul: A Critical Survey
Exposition and critique of definitions of al-'aam

Learning Objective
Identify the key definitions of al-'aam from major Hanafi and Shafi'i scholars, and apply the Hanafi criterion of 'individuals of a concept' to evaluate them.

Core Idea
A word is general only if it indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept, not merely everything that is 'suitable' for it.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut delves into the very definition of 'general' in usul al-fiqh. Different scholars have proposed definitions, but the Hanafi school, particularly through Ibn al-Humam, refines the definition to focus on 'individuals of a concept' rather than a vague 'suitability'. You will learn to distinguish the Hanafi approach from Shafi'i definitions and understand the criteria that make the Hanafi definition more precise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the definition
The passage aims to define al-'aam precisely to include correct examples (like 'kull' and 'jami'') and exclude incorrect ones (like homonyms used in one meaning or numbers).
A definition must be comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani') of its instances.
2
List the major definitions presented
Four definitions are critiqued: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (Shafi'i), Ibn al-Humam (Hanafi), Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi (Hanafi), and al-Ghazali (Shafi'i).
Focus on how each defines 'encompassment' and 'suitability'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique of the Shafi'i definitions
Abu al-Husayn's definition fails for words like 'kull' and 'jami'' because they do not encompass what is suitable for themselves (they are added to another). Ghazali's definition fails for non-existent things and relative pronouns.
The Hanafi definition using 'individuals of a concept' resolves these issues.
4
Understand the solution for definite collective nouns
On the chosen Hanafi view, the definite article (al-) cancels the collective meaning, so 'al-rijal' indicates individuals of the concept 'man'.
This makes definite plurals general, which is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses.
5
Apply the definition to test cases
Test words like 'asharah' (ten), 'al-qawm' (the people), 'al-rijal' (the men) against Ibn al-Humam's definition to see if they qualify as 'aam.
Numbers fail because they cover parts, not particulars; 'qawm' lacks a singular so it is general by meaning according to al-Bazdawi.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

استغراق أفراد مفهوم، وهذا أصوب من تعريف أبي الحسين، فإنه غير جامع للفظي كل وجميع فإنهما لا يستغرقان لما يصلحان له من الإفراد بل لإفراد ما أضيفا إليه، والمراد بالاستغراق أعم من الاستغراق الاجتماعي والانفرادي هذا ثم أورد نحو عشرة) فإنه مستغرق لما يصلح له من الآحاد التي هي أجزاؤه، ولا يتوجه هذا على تعريف الشيخ أصلاً لقوله : إفراد مفهوم، بدل قوله : ما يصلح له وأجيب بأن المراد بالصلوح المعتبر في الحد (صلوح الكلي للجزئيات لا للأجزاء، فالعام ما يستغرق لما يصلح له من الجزئيات فآل إلى تعريف الشيخ (وهي) أي العشرة لا يصلح للآحاد صلوح الكلي للجزئيات ولا يستغرق العشرات) أي جزئياته، فإن الكلام في المنكر، وأما المعرف المستغرق فمن إفراد المحدود فلا نقض بدخوله، فإن قلت : فعلى هذا يخرج الجمع المعرف المستغرق، فإن استغراقه للآحاد ليست جزئيات ،له قال : ( وعموم الرجال باعتبار أن اللام يبطل معنى الجمعية) ويجعله بمعنى المفرد (كما هو الحق) المختار من المذهب، فحينئذٍ جزئياته الآحاد لا المجموع (وقيل) عمومه (باعتبار) تناوله للجماعات وحينئذ لا إيراد وبالجملة : إن الجمع المستغرق متناول لجزئياته، أما على المختار فلأن جزئياته الآحاد وهو مستغرق لها وعلى غير المختار، فجزئياته الجماعات، وهو مستغرق لها، فلا إشكال على المذهبين ( أو ) عموم الرجال باعتبار أن (المراد) بما يصلح له جزئيات مفهوم نفس ذلك اللفظ الدال ( كلا رجل والرجل (أو) جزئيات (ما اشتمل عليه ذلك اللفظ إما حقيقة كالرجال) فإنه مشتمل على مفرده، وهو الرجل (أو حكماً امرأة من غير لفظه وهو غير مشتمل عليه حقيقة، لكنه في حكم المشتمل لكونه في معنى المشتمل (أقول يشكل على هذا الجواب بعموم اسم الجمع كالقوم فإنه لا يستغرق لجزئياته ولا جزئيات ما اشتمل عليه لعدمه، فإنه ليس له مفرد ولو تقديراً حتى يكون مشتملاً عليه فافهم . قال الإمام فخر الإسلام) في تفسيره: (هو ما انتظم جمعاً من المسميات) انتظاماً استغراقياً أم لا (لفظاً كالرجال، أو معنى كالقوم والجمع المنكر عنده) رحمه الله تعالى (منه) أي من العام فلا يتوجه الإشكال ،بدخوله وقال الإمام حجة الإسلام أبو حامد محمد الغزالي) رحمه الله تعالى وأذاقنا ما أذاقه : العام (اللفظ الواحد) احترز به عن المتعدد الدال من جهة واحدة على شيئين فصاعداً احترز بالجهة الواحدة عن المفرد المنكر فإنه دال على المتعدد من جهات، وفي إطلاقات، ولا أنه للاحتراز عن المشترك يصح المستعمل في معان فإنه رحمه الله تعالى لا يجوز هذا الاستعمال (وأورد) عليه (أولاً) المعدوم) المعرّف المستغرق، فإنه لا يدل على شيء فضلاً عن شيئين فصاعداً (فإن مدلوله ليس بشيء، والجواب أنه شيء لغة فإن أهل اللغة يطلقون الشيء على المعدوم أيضاً (وإن لم يكن شيئاً (كاملا) فإن أهل الكلام لا يطلقون الشيء عليه، وفيه خفاء، فإنه قال في «المواقف» : إن اللغة شاهدة لنا بأنه لا يسمى المعدوم في اللغة شيئاً وأيد عليه تأييدات فالأولى أن يقال إنه وإن لم يكن شيئاً حقيقة ووضعاً لكنه شيء مجازاً، وهذا المجاز شائع منفهم فلا يمتنع استعماله في التعريفات (و) أورد (ثانياً) الموصول بصلته عام و الحال أنه ليس بلفظ واحد) فلا يصدق عليه أنه من إفراد المحدود (والجواب أن العام هو الموصول) وحده المقترن مع (كالمعرف) (باللام فإنه وحده عام حال اقترانه باللام والصلاة مبينة لعمومه كاللام والموصول وحده لفظ ،واحد وليس العام المجموع المركب من الموصول والصلة ثم إن هذا لا يراد غير مختص بتحديد هذا الحبر الإمام العارف بل وارد على الجميع فيحتاج إلى هذا الجواب، فإن العام من أقسام المفرد كما لا يخفى على المتأمل (قد يجاب) في شرح المختصر بأن المراد بوحدة اللفظ أن لا يتعدد بتعدد المعاني فالعام حينئذ ما دل على متعدد ولم يتعدد اللفظ حسب الحد مع الصلة
(Fifth Chapter) (And it), i.e., the singular word, with respect to the unity and multiplicity of the named referent, is particular and general. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said in his interpretation of the general: "The general is the word that encompasses everything that is suitable for it." And [the author] added in al-Minhaj and said: "The word that encompasses everything that is suitable for it by one single mode of indication." He added this only to exclude from the definition the homonym when it encompasses what is suitable for it with respect to one meaning and not another, for it is not general. It was said in the glosses of Mirzajan: He added this for that reason and so that the homonym would not enter when all its meanings are intended, for intending all of them is valid according to him, so it encompasses what is suitable for it with respect to the modes of indication. I say: The exclusion of this homonym is not correct, for it is among the individuals of the defined term, since he said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: "al-'Aam according to al-Shafi'i is of two types: one with a unified reality, and one with a diverse reality," meaning the homonym used in all its meanings. There is a consideration. First: on this [definition] this restriction does not exist at all, and if it is valid, it is for safeguarding against the exclusion of the homonym encompassing with respect to one meaning, for just as it protects against it, it excludes the homonym encompassing two meanings. Second: because his intention is that the defined term is the particular type with a unified reality, so the other type must be excluded. Know that Shaykh Ibn al-Humam defined it as: "al-'Aam is that which indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept." This is more correct than the definition of Abu al-Husayn, for it does not include the wording of 'kull' and 'jami'', for they do not encompass what is suitable for them from the individuals, rather they encompass the individuals of what they are added to. And what is meant by encompassment is more general than social encompassment and individual encompassment. Then he brought up the example of 'asharah' (ten), for it encompasses what is suitable for it from the units that are its parts. This does not apply to the Shaykh's definition at all because of his saying 'individuals of a concept' instead of his saying 'what is suitable for it'. And it was answered that what is meant by suitability in the definition is the suitability of the universal to the particulars, not to the parts. So al-'aam is what encompasses what is suitable for it from the particulars, thus it comes down to the definition of the Shaykh. (And it), i.e., ten, is not suitable for the units as the suitability of the universal to the particulars, and it does not encompass the tens? i.e., its particulars. For the discussion is about the indefinite. As for the definite covering, it is among the individuals of the defined term, so there is no refutation by its inclusion. If you say: On this, the definite collective noun would be excluded, for its encompassing of the units is not through particulars of it. He said: "And the generality of 'al-rijal' is considered because the lam (definite article) cancels the meaning of plurality and makes it in the meaning of the singular, as is the correct chosen view of the madhhab. So then its particulars are the units, not the collective. And it was said its generality is considered by its covering the groups, and then there is no objection. In summary: The definite collective noun encompasses its particulars. According to the chosen view, its particulars are the units and it encompasses them. According to the other view, its particulars are the groups and it encompasses them. So there is no problem in either madhhab. (Or) the generality of 'al-rijal' is considered because what is meant by 'what is suitable for it' is the particulars of the concept of that very wording, like 'kullu rajul' and 'al-rajul', or the particulars of what that wording contains, either literally like 'al-rijal', for it contains its singular, which is 'al-rajul', or virtually, like 'imra'ah' from other than its wording, which is not literally contained, but is in the ruling of the contained because it is in the meaning of the contained. I say: This answer is problematic with the generality of collective nouns like 'al-qawm', for it does not encompass its particulars nor the particulars of what it contains, because it has no singular even by estimation so that it would contain it. So understand. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said in his interpretation: "It is that which comprises a plural of names, whether in a covering manner or not, in wording like 'al-rijal' or in meaning like 'al-qawm'." And the indefinite collective noun according to him (may Allah have mercy on him) is part of it, i.e., al-'aam, so the objection regarding its entry does not arise. And Imam Hujjat al-Islam Abu Hamid Muhammad al-Ghazali (may Allah have mercy on him and give us what He gave him) said: "al-'Aam is the single word" – this excludes the multiple (i.e., more than one word) that indicates from one aspect two or more things. 'From one aspect' excludes the indefinite singular, for it indicates multiplicity from different aspects and in different applications. And it [i.e., the restriction] is for exclusion of the homonym when used for meanings, for he (may Allah have mercy on him) does not permit such usage. (And objections are raised) against him: (First) the definite 'ma'dum' (non-existent thing) – it does not indicate anything, let alone two or more things, for its referent is nothing. The answer: It is a thing linguistically, for the linguists apply the word 'thing' to the non-existent as well, even if it is not a thing (complete). For the theologians do not apply 'thing' to it. There is subtlety here, for he said in al-Mawaqif that language does not call the non-existent a thing, and [the author] supported it with confirmations. So it is better to say that even if it is not a thing in reality and lexical usage, it is a thing metaphorically, and this metaphor is widespread and understood, so it is not forbidden to use it in definitions. (And) a second objection: The relative pronoun with its relative clause is general, yet it is not a single word. So it does not apply that it is from the individuals of the defined term. The answer: The general is the relative pronoun alone, like the definite noun with the lam, for it alone is general when associated with the lam, and the relative clause clarifies its generality like the lam. The relative pronoun alone is a single word, and the general is not the compound consisting of the relative pronoun and the clause. This is not specific to the definition of this great scholar but applies to all, so this answer is needed. For al-'aam is one of the divisions of the singular, as is clear to the contemplative. (It may be answered) in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that by 'single word' is meant that it does not vary with variation of meanings, so then al-'aam is what indicates multiple things and the wording does not vary. According to the limit with the clause.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of definitions of 'aam (general) in Hanafi usul. It opens with the fifth chapter on general and particular, then presents the definition of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Shafi'i scholar): a word that encompasses everything suitable for it. The author of the Minhaj adds 'by a single mode of indication' to exclude homonyms used in all their meanings. The commentator critiques this, citing Ibn al-Humam's definition (Hanafi): 'what indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept', which is deemed superior because it handles words like 'kull' and 'jami'' correctly. The text then discusses whether definite collective nouns like 'al-rijal' are general, concluding that on the chosen Hanafi view the definite article cancels plurality, making the particulars units. It addresses objections regarding 'qawm' (which has no singular) and presents the definitions of Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and Imam al-Ghazali. Ghazali's definition ('single word') is critiqued for not covering non-existing things and relative pronouns; responses are given. The commentary follows the order: definitions, critiques, objections, and answers, always prioritizing the Hanafi position (Ibn al-Humam, al-Bazdawi) over Shafi'i definitions.

Key Terms
العام (al-'aam): A word that covers all individuals that are eligible for it in one utterance; according to Ibn al-Humam: what indicates encompassing of individuals of a concept.
المستغرق (al-mustaghriq): That which covers all relevant items; the coverage can be collective or distributive.
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A homonym: a single word used for multiple distinct meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, how is al-'aam defined?
A) The word that encompasses everything suitable for it
B) The single word that indicates two or more things
C) What indicates the encompassing of the individuals of a concept
D) What comprises a plural of names

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of al-'aam focuses on 'individuals of a concept', making it precise enough to include words like 'kull' and exclude numbers like 'asharah'.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
Definitions of al-'Am by Fakhr al-Islam and al-Ghazali
Critiquing definitions of 'am by Fakhr al-Islam and Ghazali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of al-'am by Fakhr al-Islam and Imam al-Ghazali, and explain how they address plural forms and collective nouns.

Core Idea
Al-'am is a single word indicating multiple individuals from one aspect, but must include collective nouns without singular forms.

In this lesson, we examine two major definitions of al-'am (the general) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: those of Fakhr al-Islam and al-Ghazali. We will see how each definition handles the challenge of plural forms and collective nouns, and why the Hanafi school prefers a particular view.

Guided Path
1
Context: Plural form and the definite article
The passage begins by addressing how the definite plural like 'al-rijal' is made singular by the 'alif-lam', so its particulars are individuals, not groups. This sets the stage for defining 'am.
This clarifies why 'al-rijal' is considered 'am—it indicates all men as individuals.
2
Fakhr al-Islam's definition
Fakhr al-Islam defines 'am as 'what comprises a group of named entities' whether by wording (like 'al-rijal') or by meaning (like 'al-qawm'—a collective noun). He includes the indefinite plural as well.
His definition is broad and includes collective nouns without singular forms.
3
Al-Ghazali's definition
Al-Ghazali defines 'am as 'the single word that indicates two or more things from one aspect.' He excludes the indefinite singular (which indicates from multiple aspects) and does not allow homonyms (mushtarak) used in multiple meanings.
This definition is more restrictive; it requires a single word and one aspect.
4
Hanafi position and comparison
The Hanafi school leans toward Fakhr al-Islam's inclusive definition, as it better accommodates collective nouns and indefinite plurals that are essential for covering all individuals in general rulings.
This preference affects how general texts are interpreted in fiqh.

تأييدات فالأولى أن يقال إنه وإن لم يكن شيئاً حقيقة ووضعاً لكنه شيء مجازاً، وهذا المجاز شائع منفهم فلا يمتنع استعماله في التعريفات (و) أورد (ثانياً) الموصول بصلته عام و الحال أنه ليس بلفظ واحد) فلا يصدق عليه أنه من إفراد المحدود (والجواب أن العام هو الموصول) وحده المقترن مع (كالمعرف) (باللام فإنه وحده عام حال اقترانه باللام والصلاة مبينة لعمومه كاللام والموصول وحده لفظ ،واحد وليس العام المجموع بأن المراد بوحدة اللفظ أن لا يتعدد بتعدد المعاني فالعام حينئذ ما دل على متعدد ولم يتعدد اللفظ حسب تعدد المعاني، والموصول مع صلته يدل على الكثير ،دفعة لا أن واحداً منهما على واحد والآخر على أخر كما لا يخفى (وقيل) في شرح الشرح إن أريد بالدلالة على شيئين الدلالة بالمطابقة فأمثال (هذا أي الموصولات (لا) يدل على شيئين بالمطابقة فإنها موضوعة لمفهوم كلي لتستعمل في الجزئيات، فلا تكون الدلالة عليها مطابقة ( وإن أريد بها (الأعم) من المطابقة والتضمن (دخل الألفاظ التي لها مدلولات تضمنية فإنها تدل على شيئين فصاعداً بالتضمن وهي الأجزاء، قيل : هذا الإشكال وارد على أصل التعريف، ولا تخصيص له بهذا الجواب، ويمكن أن يقال : لعل وجه إيراده على الجواب أنه يخرج الموصول بقيد وحدة اللفظ، ولا يتوجه هذا الإيراد إلا إذا ثبت أن الموصول داخل فيه، وقد أثبت دخوله بهذين الجوابين، فأورد هذا الإيراد، ويمكن الجواب عنه بأن المراد بالشيئين فصاعداً الفردان من مدلول اللفظ فصاعداً، وظاهر أن الأجزاء الدالة هي عليها تضمناً ليست إفراداً، ومعنى قوله : فصاعداً، أن لا تقف الدلالة إلى حد، وحينئذٍ أيضاً يخرج الألفاظ الدالة على الأجزاء بالتضمن، فإنها واقفة عند حد، إذ لا ماهية مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية، كذا في الحاشية»، وقد يجاب بأن المراد الدلالة المطابقة مجازية كانت أو حقيقية أي الدلالة على تمام المستعمل فيه، والموصولات تدل على كل مطابقة لكن إجمالاً من حيث أنه فرد لمفهومه الذي جعل عنواناً لها، وهذا إنما يتم لو جعل كل فرد موضوعاً له استقلالاً، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العام المخصوص حقيقة في الباقي فإنه مستعمل في الموضوع له، وبهذا الجواب يندفع أيضاً لو قرر سؤال «شارح الشرح بأنه إن أريد أن الدلالة مطابقة له لا يصدق على الموصول، بل على فرد من أفراد العام، لأن اللفظ الواحد لا يدل على الكثير، وإن أريد الأعم دخل الألفاظ الموضوعة بإزاء معنى مركب فافهم، وما أورد أن العام لو كان موضوعاً للكل لكان كل واحد واحد مدلولاً تضمنياً، فلا يسري الحكم إليها فإنه لا يلزم من الحكم على الكل الحكم على الأجزاء، فالجواب عنه أنه وإن لا يلزم عقلاً لكنه يلزم في خصوص الألفاظ العامة لغة، فإن الألفاظ العامة إنما وضعت للكل، لأن يجعل محط الحكم كل واحد واحد في الاستعمال، ورب شيء لا يلزم عقلاً يلزم لغة وبالعكس فتدبر (وأجيب) في حواشي ميرزاجان باختيار شق المطابقة والقول بأن الموصولات موضوعة لمعان جزئية مما يصدق عليه الصلة بوضع عام واحد، لا أنها موضوعة لمعان كلية لتستعمل في الجزئيات كما زعم المورد فإذا أريد بها الجميع) من الجزئيات التي وضعت بإزائها (دل) على الجميع (مطابقة لأنها مستعملة فيما وضعت لها، (أقول) فيه نظر، فإن غاية ما لزم منه أن الكل مما وضعت له، فيكون الاستعمال في كل بدلاً مطابقة والمطابقة) في كل بدلاً لا تستلزم المطابقة في كل معاً فإنه لم يوضع لكل معاً (فتدبر). والتحقيق أن حال الموصول حال المعرف باللام ،بعينه، فهو ربما يقصد به الشيء المعين الموصوف بالصلة المعهود، وقد يقصد كل فرد مما اتصف بالصلة، وقد يقصد جنس المتصف بها، وقد يقصد الفرد المبهم منه المعهود في الذهن والعام ليس إلا ما قصد به المعنى الثاني، ثم إن الأفراد الموصوفة بصلة تغاير الموصوفة بصلة أخرى وهكذا فوضعت الموصولات
And it is the third article on linguistic principles. (It) that is, the ten does not fit for individuals as the universal fits for particulars, and does not encompass the tens, i.e., its particulars. For the discussion is on the indefinite. As for the definite encompassing, it is from the individuals of the limited (definition), so no refutation by its inclusion. If you say: accordingly, the definite encompassing plural is excluded, for its encompassing of individuals are not its particulars. He said: (And the generality of 'men' is because the 'alif-lam' invalidates the meaning of plurality) and makes it in the meaning of singular (as is the truth) chosen from the madhhab. Then its particulars are individuals, not the total. (And it is said) its generality (by virtue of) its encompassing groups, and then no objection. In summary: the definite encompassing plural covers its particulars: on the chosen view, its particulars are individuals and it encompasses them; on the non-chosen, its particulars are groups and it encompasses them, so no problem on either view. (Or) the generality of 'men' is because (the intended) by what fits it is the particulars of the concept of that very word signifying (like 'every man' and 'the man') (or) particulars of (what that word includes, either literally like 'men') for it includes its singular, which is 'man', (or virtually like 'woman' from other than its wording, which is not literally included but is in the ruling of inclusion because it is in the meaning of the included). (I say) this answer is problematic with the generality of a collective noun like 'al-qawm' (the people), for it does not encompass its particulars nor the particulars of what it includes, due to its lack thereof, for it has no singular even by estimation to be inclusive of it, so understand. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said in his commentary: (It is what comprises a group of named entities) whether comprehensively or not, (in wording like 'men', or in meaning like 'al-qawm', and the indefinite plural according to him—may Allah have mercy on him—(is from it), i.e., from the general, so no objection arises by its inclusion. And Imam Hujjat al-Islam Abu Hamid Muhammad al-Ghazali—may Allah have mercy on him and let us taste what He gave him—said: The general (is the single word), guarding against the multiple that indicates from one aspect two or more things. He guarded by 'one aspect' against the indefinite singular, for it indicates multiplicity from multiple aspects and in multiple senses. And it is not for guarding against the homonym that can be used in multiple meanings, for he—may Allah have mercy on him—does not permit such use. (It was objected) against it (firstly) the non-existent definite encompassing, for it does not indicate anything, let alone two or more things. (For its signification is nothing, and the answer is that it is a thing linguistically, for the people of language apply the term 'thing' to the non-existent also), even if it is not a thing (perfectly) for the people of kalam do not apply 'thing' to it. And there is subtlety in this, for he said in 'al-Mawaqif': Language testifies for us that the non-existent is not called a 'thing' in language, and he supported it with confirmations. So it is better to say that even if it is not a thing really and conventionally, it is a thing metaphorically, and this metaphor is common and understood, so its use in definitions is not impossible. (And it was objected) secondly (the relative pronoun with its clause is general, while it is not a single word), so it does not apply that it is from the individuals of the limited. (And the answer is that the general is the relative pronoun) alone when combined with (like the definite) (with the 'alif-lam', for it alone is general when combined with the 'alif-lam', and the prayer explains its generality like the 'alif-lam', and the relative pronoun alone is one word, and the general is not the composite of the relative and the clause. Then this objection is not specific to this scholar's definition but applies to all, so it needs this answer, for the general is from the divisions of the singular, as is not hidden from the thoughtful. (It may be answered) in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that what is meant by 'unity of word' is that it does not become multiple with multiplicity of meanings; then the general is what indicates multiplicity without the word becoming multiple according to the multiplicity of meanings. And the relative pronoun with its clause indicates the many at once, not that one of them indicates one and the other another, as is not hidden. (And it was said) in Sharh al-Sharh: If what is meant by indication of two things is indication by conformity, then examples like (these, i.e., relative pronouns) (do not) indicate two things by conformity, for they are placed for a universal concept to be used in particulars, so their indication of them is not by conformity. (And if what is meant is the broader meaning) including conformity and inclusion, (then words that have inclusive significations enter, for they indicate two or more things by inclusion, and they are parts). It is said: This objection applies to the original definition, and is not specific to this answer. And it can be said: Perhaps the reason for raising it against the answer is that it excludes the relative pronoun with the condition of unity of word, and this objection does not arise unless it is established that the relative pronoun is included in it. And it has been proven included by these two answers, so this objection was raised. And it can be answered that what is meant by 'two things or more' is two individuals of the term's signification or more. And it is apparent that the parts indicated by inclusion are not individuals. And the meaning of his saying 'or more' is that indication does not stop at a limit. Then also words indicating parts by inclusion are excluded, for they stop at a limit, since no essence is composed of infinite parts. Thus in the gloss. (And it may be answered) that what is meant is indication by conformity, whether metaphorical or literal, i.e., indication of the entirety of what is used for. And relative pronouns indicate with conformity but in aggregate because they are individuals of their concept which is made a title for them. This is only completed if each individual is made the subject independently, and then it follows that the specified general is literal in the remaining, for it is used in what it is placed for. And by this answer is also repelled if the question is formulated as 'Sharh al-Sharh' that if it is intended that the indication by conformity is valid, it does not apply to the relative pronoun but to an individual of the general's individuals, for one word does not indicate many. And if the broader meaning is intended, words placed for a composite meaning enter. So understand. And what was objected that if the general were placed for the whole, each single would be an inclusive signification, then the ruling does not apply to them, for from judging the whole it does not necessitate judging the parts. So the answer is that even if it is not necessitated rationally, it is necessitated specifically for general words in language, for general words are only placed for the whole so that the ruling is attached to each single in usage. And sometimes what is not necessitated rationally is necessitated linguistically, and vice versa, so ponder. (And it was answered) in the glosses of Mirzajan by choosing the aspect of conformity and saying that relative pronouns are placed for particular meanings which are true for the clause with a single general placing, not that they are placed for universal concepts to be used in particulars as the objector claimed. So if by them are intended all (the particulars for which they are placed), they indicate) all (by conformity because they are used for what they are placed for). (I say) there is reflection in this, for the utmost that follows from it is that the whole is from what they are placed for; then usage in each individual is by conformity, but conformity in each individual does not entail conformity in all together, for it was not placed for all together (so ponder). And the investigation is that the case of the relative pronoun is exactly the case of the definite with 'alif-lam'. Sometimes it is intended for the specific thing described by the clause that is known, sometimes for every individual characterized by the clause, sometimes for the genus of those characterized, and sometimes for the ambiguous individual known in the mind. And the general is only what is intended for the second meaning. Then the individuals characterized by one clause differ from those characterized by another clause, and so on. Thus the relative pronouns were placed.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the definition of 'al-'am' (the general) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The passage examines two major definitions: that of Imam Fakhr al-Islam (who defines 'am as what comprises a group of named entities, whether in wording or meaning) and that of Imam al-Ghazali (who defines it as a single word indicating two or more things from one aspect). The commentator follows the author's order, first addressing issues with the preceding discussion on plural forms and the definite article, then presenting Fakhr al-Islam's definition, then Ghazali's definition, and then listing objections: (1) the non-existent (al-ma'dum) when definite and encompassing—since it indicates nothing, it contradicts the requirement of indicating two or more things; (2) the relative pronoun (al-mawsul) with its clause—since it is not a single word but a compound. For each objection, multiple answers are given from Hanafi scholars. The first objection is answered by noting that 'thing' is applied to non-existents metaphorically in Arabic. For the second objection, key answers include: (a) the general is the relative pronoun alone, not the compound; (b) unity of word means the word does not multiply with meanings, and the relative pronoun with its clause indicates many at once; (c) alternatively, relative pronouns indicate by conformity but only in aggregate. The passage concludes with the investigation (al-tahqiq) that relative pronouns function like definite nouns with 'alif-lam'—they can have multiple intentions, and 'am is only the intention of covering every individual. Throughout, the commentary reflects the Hanafi madhhab's precision in defining usul terms, showing how each definition must be airtight against counterexamples.

Key Terms
العام (al-'am): A single word that indicates two or more things from one aspect, or what comprises a group of named entities (Fakhr al-Islam).
الموصول (al-mawsul): Relative pronoun (e.g., alladhi) that with its clause indicates multiple individuals; it is considered a single word in the definition of 'am when combined with the clause.
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): The non-existent; in this context, the definite noun indicating a non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mu'arraf al-mustaghriq) is challenged as not indicating anything.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Ghazali's definition of al-'am, which of the following is a necessary condition?
A) The word must be plural.
B) The word must be definite with alif-lam.
C) The word must indicate two or more things from one aspect.
D) The word must have a singular form.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'am must accommodate collective nouns and indefinite plurals, rejecting the notion that a singular form is required.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Objections to the Definition of al-'Am: Al-Ma'dum and Al-Mawsul
Critiquing definitions of 'am by Fakhr al-Islam and Ghazali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main objections to al-Ghazali's definition of al-'am (non-existent and relative pronoun), and explain the Hanafi responses.

Core Idea
The definition of al-'am must account for non-existent subjects and relative pronouns as legitimate general terms.

Imam al-Ghazali's definition of al-'am as a single word indicating two or more things from one aspect faces two strong objections: the case of the non-existent definite noun (al-ma'dum) which seems to indicate nothing, and the relative pronoun (al-mawsul) which appears to be a compound, not a single word. In this lesson, we explore the sophisticated Hanafi responses that defend the definition.

Guided Path
1
First objection: the non-existent (al-ma'dum)
A definite noun like 'al-ma'dum' (the non-existent) when used generally (e.g., 'every non-existent') does not indicate anything, so it claims to be 'am but fails to indicate two or more things. The objection is that the definition would wrongly exclude such a term or the term is not truly 'am.
This objection questions whether 'thing' can apply to non-existents.
2
Hanafi response to al-ma'dum
The response is that 'thing' is applied to non-existents metaphorically in Arabic language, even if not literally. This metaphor is common and understood, so the term does indicate (metaphorically) two or more non-existent individuals. Thus, the definition holds.
The passage notes a debate: some linguists deny this, but the Hanafi position accepts metaphorical usage based on widespread custom.
3
Second objection: the relative pronoun (al-mawsul)
Relative pronouns like 'alladhi' are not a single word when combined with their clause (e.g., 'alladhi ja'a' = 'the one who came'). Since the definition requires a single word, the relative pronoun seems to be a compound, not a single word.
This objection applies not only to Ghazali's definition but to all definitions of 'am.
4
Multiple Hanafi responses to al-mawsul
Several responses are given: (a) The general is the relative pronoun alone, not the compound; the clause only specifies which individuals are meant, just as the definite article 'alif-lam' does. (b) 'Unity of word' means the word does not multiply with meanings; the relative pronoun with its clause indicates many at once without the word becoming multiple. (c) Indication by conformity (mutabaqa) applies but only in aggregate; or alternatively, relative pronouns are placed for universal concepts. The preferred view is that relative pronouns function like definite nouns with 'alif-lam' and can be used for the general meaning.
The final investigation (al-tahqiq) clarifies that the relative pronoun can be intended in four ways, and only the second intention (every individual characterized by the clause) makes it 'am.
