يخفى (وقيل) في شرح الشرح إن أريد بالدلالة على شيئين الدلالة بالمطابقة فأمثال (هذا أي الموصولات (لا) يدل على شيئين بالمطابقة فإنها موضوعة لمفهوم كلي لتستعمل في الجزئيات، فلا تكون الدلالة عليها مطابقة ( وإن أريد بها (الأعم) من المطابقة والتضمن (دخل الألفاظ التي لها مدلولات تضمنية فإنها تدل على شيئين فصاعداً بالتضمن وهي الأجزاء، قيل : هذا الإشكال وارد على أصل التعريف، ولا تخصيص له بهذا الجواب، ويمكن أن يقال : لعل وجه إيراده على الجواب أنه يخرج الموصول بقيد وحدة اللفظ، ولا يتوجه هذا الإيراد إلا إذا ثبت أن الموصول داخل فيه، وقد أثبت دخوله بهذين الجوابين، فأورد هذا الإيراد، ويمكن الجواب عنه بأن المراد بالشيئين فصاعداً الفردان من مدلول اللفظ فصاعداً، وظاهر أن الأجزاء الدالة هي عليها تضمناً ليست إفراداً، ومعنى قوله : فصاعداً، أن لا تقف الدلالة إلى حد، وحينئذٍ أيضاً يخرج الألفاظ الدالة على الأجزاء بالتضمن، فإنها واقفة عند حد، إذ لا ماهية مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية، كذا في الحاشية»، وقد يجاب بأن المراد الدلالة المطابقة مجازية كانت أو حقيقية أي الدلالة على تمام المستعمل فيه، والموصولات تدل على كل مطابقة لكن إجمالاً من حيث أنه فرد لمفهومه الذي جعل عنواناً لها، وهذا إنما يتم لو جعل كل فرد موضوعاً له استقلالاً، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العام المخصوص حقيقة في الباقي فإنه مستعمل في الموضوع له، وبهذا الجواب يندفع أيضاً لو قرر سؤال «شارح الشرح بأنه إن أريد أن الدلالة مطابقة له لا يصدق على الموصول، بل على فرد من أفراد العام، لأن اللفظ الواحد لا يدل على الكثير، وإن أريد الأعم دخل الألفاظ الموضوعة بإزاء معنى مركب فافهم، وما أورد أن العام لو كان موضوعاً للكل لكان كل واحد واحد مدلولاً تضمنياً، فلا يسري الحكم إليها فإنه لا يلزم من الحكم على الكل الحكم على الأجزاء، فالجواب عنه أنه وإن لا يلزم عقلاً لكنه يلزم في خصوص الألفاظ العامة لغة، فإن الألفاظ العامة إنما وضعت للكل، لأن يجعل محط الحكم كل واحد واحد في الاستعمال، ورب شيء لا يلزم عقلاً يلزم لغة وبالعكس فتدبر (وأجيب) في حواشي ميرزاجان باختيار شق المطابقة والقول بأن الموصولات موضوعة لمعان جزئية مما يصدق عليه الصلة بوضع عام واحد، لا أنها موضوعة لمعان كلية لتستعمل في الجزئيات كما زعم المورد فإذا أريد بها الجميع) من الجزئيات التي وضعت بإزائها (دل) على الجميع (مطابقة لأنها مستعملة فيما وضعت لها، (أقول) فيه نظر، فإن غاية ما لزم منه أن الكل مما وضعت له، فيكون الاستعمال في كل بدلاً مطابقة والمطابقة) في كل بدلاً لا تستلزم المطابقة في كل معاً فإنه لم يوضع لكل معاً (فتدبر). والتحقيق أن حال الموصول حال المعرف باللام ،بعينه، فهو ربما يقصد به الشيء المعين الموصوف بالصلة المعهود، وقد يقصد كل فرد مما اتصف بالصلة، وقد يقصد جنس المتصف بها، وقد يقصد الفرد المبهم منه المعهود في الذهن والعام ليس إلا ما قصد به المعنى الثاني، ثم إن الأفراد الموصوفة بصلة تغاير الموصوفة بصلة أخرى وهكذا فوضعت الموصولات
And it is the third article on linguistic principles. (It) that is, the ten does not fit for individuals as the universal fits for particulars, and does not encompass the tens, i.e., its particulars. For the discussion is on the indefinite. As for the definite encompassing, it is from the individuals of the limited (definition), so no refutation by its inclusion. If you say: accordingly, the definite encompassing plural is excluded, for its encompassing of individuals are not its particulars. He said: (And the generality of 'men' is because the 'alif-lam' invalidates the meaning of plurality) and makes it in the meaning of singular (as is the truth) chosen from the madhhab. Then its particulars are individuals, not the total. (And it is said) its generality (by virtue of) its encompassing groups, and then no objection. In summary: the definite encompassing plural covers its particulars: on the chosen view, its particulars are individuals and it encompasses them; on the non-chosen, its particulars are groups and it encompasses them, so no problem on either view. (Or) the generality of 'men' is because (the intended) by what fits it is the particulars of the concept of that very word signifying (like 'every man' and 'the man') (or) particulars of (what that word includes, either literally like 'men') for it includes its singular, which is 'man', (or virtually like 'woman' from other than its wording, which is not literally included but is in the ruling of inclusion because it is in the meaning of the included). (I say) this answer is problematic with the generality of a collective noun like 'al-qawm' (the people), for it does not encompass its particulars nor the particulars of what it includes, due to its lack thereof, for it has no singular even by estimation to be inclusive of it, so understand. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said in his commentary: (It is what comprises a group of named entities) whether comprehensively or not, (in wording like 'men', or in meaning like 'al-qawm', and the indefinite plural according to him—may Allah have mercy on him—(is from it), i.e., from the general, so no objection arises by its inclusion. And Imam Hujjat al-Islam Abu Hamid Muhammad al-Ghazali—may Allah have mercy on him and let us taste what He gave him—said: The general (is the single word), guarding against the multiple that indicates from one aspect two or more things. He guarded by 'one aspect' against the indefinite singular, for it indicates multiplicity from multiple aspects and in multiple senses. And it is not for guarding against the homonym that can be used in multiple meanings, for he—may Allah have mercy on him—does not permit such use. (It was objected) against it (firstly) the non-existent definite encompassing, for it does not indicate anything, let alone two or more things. (For its signification is nothing, and the answer is that it is a thing linguistically, for the people of language apply the term 'thing' to the non-existent also), even if it is not a thing (perfectly) for the people of kalam do not apply 'thing' to it. And there is subtlety in this, for he said in 'al-Mawaqif': Language testifies for us that the non-existent is not called a 'thing' in language, and he supported it with confirmations. So it is better to say that even if it is not a thing really and conventionally, it is a thing metaphorically, and this metaphor is common and understood, so its use in definitions is not impossible. (And it was objected) secondly (the relative pronoun with its clause is general, while it is not a single word), so it does not apply that it is from the individuals of the limited. (And the answer is that the general is the relative pronoun) alone when combined with (like the definite) (with the 'alif-lam', for it alone is general when combined with the 'alif-lam', and the prayer explains its generality like the 'alif-lam', and the relative pronoun alone is one word, and the general is not the composite of the relative and the clause. Then this objection is not specific to this scholar's definition but applies to all, so it needs this answer, for the general is from the divisions of the singular, as is not hidden from the thoughtful. (It may be answered) in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that what is meant by 'unity of word' is that it does not become multiple with multiplicity of meanings; then the general is what indicates multiplicity without the word becoming multiple according to the multiplicity of meanings. And the relative pronoun with its clause indicates the many at once, not that one of them indicates one and the other another, as is not hidden. (And it was said) in Sharh al-Sharh: If what is meant by indication of two things is indication by conformity, then examples like (these, i.e., relative pronouns) (do not) indicate two things by conformity, for they are placed for a universal concept to be used in particulars, so their indication of them is not by conformity. (And if what is meant is the broader meaning) including conformity and inclusion, (then words that have inclusive significations enter, for they indicate two or more things by inclusion, and they are parts). It is said: This objection applies to the original definition, and is not specific to this answer. And it can be said: Perhaps the reason for raising it against the answer is that it excludes the relative pronoun with the condition of unity of word, and this objection does not arise unless it is established that the relative pronoun is included in it. And it has been proven included by these two answers, so this objection was raised. And it can be answered that what is meant by 'two things or more' is two individuals of the term's signification or more. And it is apparent that the parts indicated by inclusion are not individuals. And the meaning of his saying 'or more' is that indication does not stop at a limit. Then also words indicating parts by inclusion are excluded, for they stop at a limit, since no essence is composed of infinite parts. Thus in the gloss. (And it may be answered) that what is meant is indication by conformity, whether metaphorical or literal, i.e., indication of the entirety of what is used for. And relative pronouns indicate with conformity but in aggregate because they are individuals of their concept which is made a title for them. This is only completed if each individual is made the subject independently, and then it follows that the specified general is literal in the remaining, for it is used in what it is placed for. And by this answer is also repelled if the question is formulated as 'Sharh al-Sharh' that if it is intended that the indication by conformity is valid, it does not apply to the relative pronoun but to an individual of the general's individuals, for one word does not indicate many. And if the broader meaning is intended, words placed for a composite meaning enter. So understand. And what was objected that if the general were placed for the whole, each single would be an inclusive signification, then the ruling does not apply to them, for from judging the whole it does not necessitate judging the parts. So the answer is that even if it is not necessitated rationally, it is necessitated specifically for general words in language, for general words are only placed for the whole so that the ruling is attached to each single in usage. And sometimes what is not necessitated rationally is necessitated linguistically, and vice versa, so ponder. (And it was answered) in the glosses of Mirzajan by choosing the aspect of conformity and saying that relative pronouns are placed for particular meanings which are true for the clause with a single general placing, not that they are placed for universal concepts to be used in particulars as the objector claimed. So if by them are intended all (the particulars for which they are placed), they indicate) all (by conformity because they are used for what they are placed for). (I say) there is reflection in this, for the utmost that follows from it is that the whole is from what they are placed for; then usage in each individual is by conformity, but conformity in each individual does not entail conformity in all together, for it was not placed for all together (so ponder). And the investigation is that the case of the relative pronoun is exactly the case of the definite with 'alif-lam'. Sometimes it is intended for the specific thing described by the clause that is known, sometimes for every individual characterized by the clause, sometimes for the genus of those characterized, and sometimes for the ambiguous individual known in the mind. And the general is only what is intended for the second meaning. Then the individuals characterized by one clause differ from those characterized by another clause, and so on. Thus the relative pronouns were placed.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the definition of 'al-'am' (the general) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The passage examines two major definitions: that of Imam Fakhr al-Islam (who defines 'am as what comprises a group of named entities, whether in wording or meaning) and that of Imam al-Ghazali (who defines it as a single word indicating two or more things from one aspect). The commentator follows the author's order, first addressing issues with the preceding discussion on plural forms and the definite article, then presenting Fakhr al-Islam's definition, then Ghazali's definition, and then listing objections: (1) the non-existent (al-ma'dum) when definite and encompassing—since it indicates nothing, it contradicts the requirement of indicating two or more things; (2) the relative pronoun (al-mawsul) with its clause—since it is not a single word but a compound. For each objection, multiple answers are given from Hanafi scholars. The first objection is answered by noting that 'thing' is applied to non-existents metaphorically in Arabic. For the second objection, key answers include: (a) the general is the relative pronoun alone, not the compound; (b) unity of word means the word does not multiply with meanings, and the relative pronoun with its clause indicates many at once; (c) alternatively, relative pronouns indicate by conformity but only in aggregate. The passage concludes with the investigation (al-tahqiq) that relative pronouns function like definite nouns with 'alif-lam'—they can have multiple intentions, and 'am is only the intention of covering every individual. Throughout, the commentary reflects the Hanafi madhhab's precision in defining usul terms, showing how each definition must be airtight against counterexamples.

Key Terms
العام (al-'am): A single word that indicates two or more things from one aspect, or what comprises a group of named entities (Fakhr al-Islam).
الموصول (al-mawsul): Relative pronoun (e.g., alladhi) that with its clause indicates multiple individuals; it is considered a single word in the definition of 'am when combined with the clause.
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): The non-existent; in this context, the definite noun indicating a non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mu'arraf al-mustaghriq) is challenged as not indicating anything.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) does not indicate anything?
A) The non-existent does not exist, so it cannot be a subject of a general term.
B) The term 'thing' is applied to non-existents metaphorically in Arabic.
C) The objection is invalid because the non-existent is not a general term.
D) The definition of 'am does not require indication of two or more things.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul tradition defends the definition of 'am against objections through nuanced linguistic analysis, showing that both non-existent referents and relative pronouns qualify as general terms.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Do Relative Pronouns Indicate Generality? The Hanafi Response
Responding to objections that mawsulat enter the definition of 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether mawsulat (relative pronouns) are included in the definition of 'amm and evaluate the objections and responses.

Core Idea
Mawsulat indicate generality by mutabaqah when used to encompass all individuals described by the clause.

In defining 'amm (general), some scholars object that relative pronouns (mawsulat) cannot indicate multiple individuals because they are universal concepts. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response, showing that mawsulat can indeed indicate generality through a single placement for the whole set. We will trace the arguments and counterarguments, culminating in the Hanafi verification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core objection
The objector claims that mawsulat (e.g., 'alladhi') are placed for universal concepts to be used for particulars, so they do not indicate multiple individuals by mutabaqah (direct correspondence). Determine what is being contested in the definition of 'amm.
This objection targets the definition of 'amm as indicating two or more things.
2
Analyze the two possible interpretations of 'indication'
The passage considers two interpretations: if 'indication' means mutabaqah, mawsulat might not apply; if it includes tadammun (implication), then parts of composite words would enter, which is undesirable. Understand the dilemma.
This sets up the need for a refined answer.
3
Examine the Hanafi defense via placement
The key Hanafi defense is that mawsulat are placed for all individuals described by the clause with a single general placement. When used to encompass all, they indicate the whole set by mutabaqah. Compare this to the definite article (al-) which can also indicate generality.
This is the verification (tahqiq) that the text calls 'the truth from which truth does not depart'.
4
Distinguish from homonyms and metaphors
The passage distinguishes mawsulat from homonyms (mushtarak) and metaphorical uses. Unlike homonyms, mawsulat can be used for all individuals without violating usage conditions.
This reinforces the uniqueness of mawsulat.
5
Apply to a Quranic example
Consider the Quranic address 'alladhina amanu' (those who believe). According to the Hanafi view, this includes all believers, and the ruling (e.g., a command) applies to each one generally. Identify the practical consequence.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical result) of the dispute.

تضمنياً، فلا يسري الحكم إليها فإنه لا يلزم من الحكم على الكل الحكم على الأجزاء، فالجواب عنه أنه وإن لا يلزم عقلاً لكنه يلزم في خصوص الألفاظ العامة لغة، فإن الألفاظ العامة إنما وضعت للكل، لأن يجعل محط الحكم كل واحد واحد في الاستعمال، ورب شيء لا يلزم عقلاً يلزم لغة وبالعكس فتدبر (وأجيب) في حواشي ميرزاجان باختيار شق المطابقة والقول بأن الموصولات موضوعة لمعان جزئية مما يصدق عليه الصلة بوضع عام واحد، لا أنها موضوعة لمعان كلية لتستعمل في الجزئيات كما زعم المورد فإذا أريد بها الجميع) من الجزئيات التي وضعت بإزائها (دل) على الجميع (مطابقة لأنها مستعملة فيما وضعت لها، (أقول) فيه نظر، فإن غاية ما لزم الأفراد الموصوفة بصلة تغاير الموصوفة بصلة أخرى وهكذا فوضعت الموصولات بإزاء الأفراد الموصوفة بصلة كلها والأفراد الموصوفة بصلة أخرى كلها إلى ما لا نهاية لها من الجملات بالوضع العام مرة واحدة فالمستعمل المستغرق لجميع أفراد متصفة بصلة دالة عليها مطابقة، وكذا المستغرق لأفراد صلة أخرى أيضاً دال عليها مطابقة وليس المقصود أن الوضع لكل واحد مما اتصف بالصلة بدلاً، وأن الوضع لكل بدلاً لا يستلزم كون الدلالة على الكل معاً مطابقة بل إنها موضوعة لكل معاً فالمستعمل فيها يدل عليها مطابقة، هذا هو التحقيق الذي لا يتجاوز عنه الحق، وأما الألفاظ المجازية المستغرقة لأفراد معانيها المجازية فالجواب عنها إما بأن يراد بالمطابقة الدلالة على تمام المستعمل فيه حقيقياً كان أو مجازياً، وإما بما قال المصنف إن الألفاظ المذكورة وإن كانت مجازاً بالنسبة إلى المفهومات الكلية لكنها حقيقة في العموم، فيكون العموم مدلولاً مطابقياً، لكن على الثاني يخرج الألفاظ الخاصة المستعملة في العموم مجازاً، نحو النكرة المستعملة في الإثبات المستغرقة ولا بأس به، فإن الظاهر أن التعريف للعام الحقيقة هذا وقد يجاب بأن الموصول موضوع لحصة حصة مما اتصف بالصلة من غير اعتبار الاجتماع والبدلية، وإلا لما صح الاستعمال في واحد منهما، بل فإذا أريد الكل كان كما أريد من المشترك جميع المعاني إلا أن المشترك شرط استعماله أن لا يجمع المعاني في الإرادة فيكون هذا الاستعمال فيه خطأ، وههنا لما لم يشترط في الاستعمال هذا الشرط كان حقيقة، لأنه استعمال صحيح فيما وضع له، ولعل هذا الجواب تنزلي، والحق ما أفدنا سابقاً فتأمل وتشكر
Multiple meanings, and the relative pronoun together with its clause indicates many [individuals] at once, not that one indicates one and another indicates another, as is not hidden. And it was said in Sharh al-Sharh: If what is meant by 'indication of two things' is indication by mutabaqah (whole correspondence), then examples of these, i.e., the relative pronouns, do not indicate two things by mutabaqah, for they are placed for a universal concept to be used for particulars, so their indication of them is not by mutabaqah. And if what is meant is more general than mutabaqah and tadammun (inclusion), then words that have implicative meanings enter, for they indicate two or more things by tadammun, and these are the parts. It was said: This objection applies to the original definition, and there is no specification for it by this answer. And it can be said: Perhaps the reason for its being raised against the answer is that it excludes the relative pronoun by the condition of the unity of the word, and this objection does not arise unless it is established that the relative pronoun is included in it, and its inclusion has been established by these two answers, so this objection was raised. And it can be answered that by 'two things or more' is meant the two individuals from the meaning of the word and above, and it is apparent that the parts indicated by tadammun are not individuals. And the meaning of his saying 'or more' is that the indication does not stop at a limit, and then also the words that indicate parts by tadammun are excluded, for they are stopped at a limit, since there is no essence composed of infinite parts. Thus in the hashia. And it can be answered that what is meant is indication by mutabaqah, whether metaphorical or real, i.e., indication of the entirety of what it is used for, and the relative pronouns indicate each [individual] by mutabaqah but in a summary way in that it is an individual of its concept which is made a title for them. And this is only complete if each individual is made a meaning for it independently, and then it follows that the particularized general is true in the remaining because it is used for what it is placed for. And by this answer, it also repels even if the question of Sharh al-Sharh is established: that if what is meant is that the indication is mutabaqah, it does not apply to the relative pronoun, but to an individual of the general's individuals, because the single word does not indicate many; and if what is meant is the more general, then words placed for a composite meaning enter. So understand. And what was raised that if the general is placed for the whole, then each one would be an implied meaning, so the ruling does not extend to them, because the ruling on the whole does not necessitate the ruling on the parts. The answer to that is that although it is not necessary intellectually, it is necessary in the case of general words linguistically, for general words are only placed for the whole so that the ruling is made on each one in usage. And sometimes something not necessary intellectually is necessary linguistically, and vice versa, so reflect. And it was answered in the hashia of Mirzajan by choosing the side of mutabaqah and the statement that the relative pronouns are placed for particular meanings that satisfy the clause with a single general placement, not that they are placed for universal concepts to be used for particulars as the objector claimed. So if by it is meant all the particulars for which it is placed, it indicates all by mutabaqah because it is used for what it is placed for. I say: There is consideration in it, for the utmost that follows from it is that the whole is part of what it is placed for, so the usage in each individual is by mutabaqah, and mutabaqah in each individual does not entail mutabaqah in all together, for it was not placed for all together. So reflect. And the verification is that the case of the relative pronoun is exactly the case of the definite article with lam. So sometimes what is intended by it is the specific thing described by the known clause, and sometimes every individual described by the clause, and sometimes the genus of those described, and sometimes the indefinite individual of it known in the mind. And general is only what is intended by the second meaning. Then the individuals described by one clause differ from those described by another clause, and so on. So the relative pronouns are placed for the individuals described by each clause and the individuals described by another clause, ad infinitum from the sentences, with a general placement once. So the one who uses it to encompass all individuals described by a clause indicates them by mutabaqah, and likewise the one who uses it to encompass individuals of another clause also indicates them by mutabaqah. And the intention is not that the placement is for each individual described by the clause individually, and that placement for each individually does not entail that the indication of all together is by mutabaqah; rather, they are placed for all together, so the one who uses them indicates them by mutabaqah. This is the verification from which truth does not depart. As for the metaphorical words that encompass individuals of their metaphorical meanings, the answer is either that what is meant by mutabaqah is indication of the entirety of what it is used for, whether real or metaphorical, or by what the author said that the mentioned words, although metaphorical with respect to universal concepts, are real in generality, so generality is a mutabaqah meaning. But on the second, specific words used metaphorically for generality are excluded, like the indefinite used in the affirmative for totality, and there is no harm in that, for it is apparent that the definition is for the real general. And it can be answered that the relative pronoun is placed for each portion of what is described by the clause without considering combination or substitution, otherwise it would not be correct to use it in one of them. But if the whole is intended, it is like when all the meanings of a homonym are intended, except that in the homonym the condition of its usage is that the meanings are not combined in intention, so this usage is wrong. Here, since this condition is not stipulated in usage, it is real, because it is a correct usage for what it is placed for. Perhaps this answer is concessive, and the truth is what we previously conveyed, so reflect and be grateful.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding whether mawsulat (relative pronouns) enter into the definition of 'amm (general) as words that indicate two or more things. The objector argues that mawsulat do indicate multiple individuals (e.g., 'alladhi' can refer to any person with a certain attribute), so they should be included. The responder provides several lines of defense. First, if 'indication by mutabaqah' is meant (i.e., the whole meaning), then mawsulat do not indicate the set of all individuals by mutabaqah because they are universal concepts applied to particulars. If 'indication' includes tadammun (implication), then parts of composite meanings would enter, which is undesirable. The discussion proceeds through multiple answers, including that mawsulat are placed for each individual independently, or for all individuals together as a set. The verification is that mawsulat are like definite nouns: they can be used for a specific individual, every individual, the genus, etc. When used for all individuals described by a clause, they indicate that totality by mutabaqah. The passage concludes by stating that this is the correct view and that metaphorical uses of general words are handled separately. The Hanafi position is that mawsulat can be general when intended to encompass all individuals fitting the description, and this is consistent with the definition of 'amm.

Key Terms
مطابقة (mutabaqah): Indication of the whole meaning of a word, i.e., the complete referent.
تضمن (tadammun): Indication of a part of the meaning, e.g., 'house' indicates 'roof' by implication.
الموصول (al-mawsul): Relative pronoun (e.g., alladhi, allati) that together with its sila (relative clause) refers to an antecedent.
عام ('amm): A word that indicates two or more individuals without limit by a single usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the verification (tahqiq) in this passage, how do mawsulat (relative pronouns) indicate generality when used to encompass all individuals described by the sila?
A) They indicate each individual separately by tadammun.
B) They indicate the whole set of individuals by mutabaqah, because they are placed for all individuals together.
C) They only indicate a specific individual, not a general set.
D) They indicate the entire set by iltizam (concomitance).

Key Takeaway
Relative pronouns (mawsulat) can indicate generality by mutabaqah for the whole set when intended, and this is the Hanafi verification that aligns them with definite nouns.

Excluding the Dual from the Definition of 'Amm
Usul dispute: dual form and definition of 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the dual (muthanna) is excluded from the definition of 'amm in Hanafi usul and apply the distinction to analyze a parallel case in agency law.

Core Idea
The dual is not general because it is fixed at two, while 'amm requires the potential for indefinitely more.

One objection to the definition of 'amm is that the dual form (muthanna) indicates two things and thus would be included. The Hanafi response hinges on interpreting 'two or more' as 'two and above'—meaning the word must be capable of indicating more than two. The dual is confined to exactly two, so it fails. This lesson unpacks the objection, the response, and a revealing counterexample from agency law to solidify the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize that the dual is raised as a counterexample to the definition of 'amm because it indicates two things.
The definition requires indicating 'two or more' – the dual seems to meet this literally.
2
State the Hanafi response
Understand that the definition means 'two and above' (i.e., with potential for more), which the dual cannot do.
The dual is limited to exactly two; it does not allow for the possibility of three or more.
3
Analyze the agency counterexample
Follow the objector's scenario: 'Sell for two dirhams or more.' If 'or more' is attached to the price, the agent would not be compliant selling for exactly two. But in practice, he is compliant.
This seems to contradict the Hanafi interpretation of 'fasa'idan' (and above).
4
Distinguish the agency case from the definition
The answer explains that in agency, 'fasa'idan' is a circumstantial accusative ('the price going upward'), not attached to 'two dirhams'. So the authorization includes exactly two dirhams and allows increases.
In contrast, in the definition of 'amm, 'two or more' is directly attached to the indication: the word indicates two and can indicate more.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: words in the dual form (e.g., 'two witnesses') are specific, not general, and cannot be extended to one or three without separate evidence.
This avoids misapplying general rulings to fixed numbers.

And a third objection was raised by the entry of the dual, for it indicates two things. And the answer: It does not indicate two or more together, for what is meant by 'indication of two or more' is indication of them and what is above them, and the dual is not suitable for what is above two, so it does not enter the definition. It was said: That necessitates that if someone sold for two dirhams in the case when a man appointed an agent and said to him 'Sell for two dirhams or more,' he would not be compliant because its meaning according to what you mentioned is selling for two dirhams and what is above them, so the permission did not include selling for two dirhams, so no compliance. But the truth is contrary to that, because he is definitely compliant such that the sale is executed, and the owner has no right of rescission. And it is answered that it is not possible to connect 'or more' in that, i.e., in the agency, to 'two dirhams' because they do not ascend, for the two dirhams do not become what is above them. Rather, the 'ascending' is the price by increase upon them. And if it is not correct, it is said that it is a circumstantial accusative with a deleted operator, and the meaning is 'the price goes ascending'. So the agency is for selling for two dirhams and permission for increase in the price. Unlike our case here, for there is no distracting factor from the apparent meaning, because the indicator accepts increase with respect to the indicated, so the indicator increases with the increase of the indicated. So it is valid to say: it indicates two and what is above them, so it does not change from the apparent. And it is not hidden that the connection in our case also to 'two things' is not correct due to difference in parsing. Also, it is not necessary that the connection to 'two dirhams' be that they are ascending; rather, it only requires that the sale be in exchange for the two dirhams and the ascending, so there is no impossibility in the connection. Perhaps he meant by 'connection' its linguistic meaning. And the intention is that it is not correct for it to attach to two dirhams because they do not ascend. Rather, 'or more' attaches to the deleted [word], so the agency to sell for two dirhams is complete, and this is another report. As for our case here, it is correct for the attachment to the two things, for they ascend by virtue of being indicated, and this also is not sufficient, for the two things themselves do not ascend.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection to the definition of 'amm, which is that the dual (muthanna) indicates two things and thus would enter the definition if it requires indicating two or more. The response is that the definition requires indicating two or more (meaning two and above, i.e., the possibility of more), but the dual is limited to exactly two, so it does not qualify. The objector then raises a counterexample using a legal scenario: if someone authorizes an agent to sell for 'two dirhams or more,' the agent would be able to sell for two dirhams according to common understanding, but if 'fasa'idan' means 'and above,' then the authorization would only cover selling for more than two, not exactly two, which contradicts actual practice. The responder clarifies that in the agency context, 'fasa'idan' is not attached to 'two dirhams' but is a circumstantial phrase meaning 'the price going upward,' so the authorization includes selling for two dirhams and allows increases. In the definition of 'amm, however, the phrase 'two or more' is attached to the indication itself: the word indicates two and can indicate more (i.e., it is not limited to two). The dual does not allow for more than two, so it is excluded. The Hanafi position is that the dual is not a general word because its indication is fixed at two. This avoids the objection.

Key Terms
مثنى (muthanna): Linguistic dual form indicating exactly two individuals or items.
فصاعداً (fasa'idan): Arabic phrase meaning 'and above' or 'or more,' indicating an increase beyond the mentioned number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it indicates only one thing, not two.
B) Because it indicates two things but not more than two, whereas 'amm requires the possibility of more.
C) Because the dual is not a word, it is a grammatical form.
D) Because the definition of 'amm requires the word to indicate at least three things.

Key Takeaway
The dual is not general; its indication is limited to exactly two, so it is excluded from the definition of 'amm.

The Fourth Critique: Definite and Indefinite Plurals and the Definition of 'Aam
Defense of 'aam definition against objection from definite/indefinite plurals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definite and indefinite plurals are not automatically considered general ('aam) in Hanafi usul and identify the condition of comprehensiveness (istighraq).

Core Idea
A general term must indicate all individuals of a description comprehensively; definite plurals indicate specific known individuals, not all.

This lesson unpacks a fourth objection against the Hanafi definition of 'aam. The objector points out that definite and indefinite plurals also indicate multiple individuals—so why aren't they general? The Hanafi response sharpens the definition by adding the crucial condition of 'comprehensiveness' (istighraq). We will walk through the two-tiered answer and examine how annexed plurals fit into this framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize the fourth critique: definite plurals (like 'al-rijal') and indefinite plurals (like 'rijalun') also indicate two or more, so they should qualify as 'aam under the original definition.
The objection targets the definition that 'aam is 'what indicates two and above' without further condition.
2
Analyze the first answer
The author first accepts the objection (التزام), stating that in his terminology, these plurals are indeed considered general. This avoids a verbal dispute.
This shows that terminology can resolve the critique without substantive change.
3
Analyze the second answer
On a concessive level (تنزل), the definition is refined: 'indication of two and above together with comprehensive inclusion (معاً بالاستغراق)'. Under this, definite plurals are excluded because they point to specific known individuals, not all individuals of the description.
The key addition is 'معاً بالاستغراق'—the term must encompass all instances of its category.
4
Examine the annexed plural
Annexed plurals like 'scholars of the city' first restrict the set by annexation, then indicate all individuals within that restricted set. The text argues that this is different from the definite plural because the annexation gives a specific common matter ('scholar of the city') that is not further qualified.
Annexed plurals are 'aam within their restriction, but the definite plural without annexation lacks comprehensiveness entirely.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
The truth (as the author states) is that there is no real difference between definite and indefinite plurals: both are 'aam only if they indicate all individuals of a description comprehensively. The decisive factor is whether the term carries comprehensiveness (istighraq).
This resolves the objection by clarifying that 'aam requires both plurality and comprehensive coverage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) أورد (رابعاً) النقض (بالجمع المعهود والمنكر) فإنهما دالان على اثنين فصاعداً (وأجيب) أوّلاً (بالتزامه) فإنهما عامان عنده قدس سره، ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح (و) ثانياً على التنزل (بأن المراد الدلالة على اثنين فصاعداً (معاً بالاستغراق، ولا يشكل بالجموع المضافة مثل علماء البلد) فإنه غير مستغرق لأفراده ،كلها بل لبعضها كالمعهود للفرق البين بين الإفراد للمخصوص على الإطلاق كما في الجمع المضاف، فإنه اعتبر الخصوصية أوّلاً بالإضافة، ثم اعتبر استغراقه لجميع أفراده وبين الإفراد للمطلق على الخصوص كما في الجمع المعهود، فإنه اعتبر مطلقاً، وأريد بعض مسمياته، فلا شائبة للاستغراق فيه فما في «التحرير») أنه عرف العام بما دل على مسميات باعتبار أمر اشتركت فيه مطلقاً ضربة فقوله مطلقاً، احتراز عن الجمع المعهود، فإنه يدل على أفراد مخصوصة لا مطلقاً، ويرد عليه الجمع المضاف، فإن مسمياته مستغرقة مقيداً لا مطلقاً، وأجيب بأن المشترك فيه عالم البلد ولم يقيد بشيء بخلاف المعهود، فإن المشترك فيه الرجال مثلاً، وقد اعتبر تقييده والحق أن لا فرق بين الجمع المعهود
(And) a fourth objection is raised: the critique by the definite and indefinite plurals, for they both indicate two and above. And it is answered: first, by accepting it, for they are general according to him—may his secret be sanctified—and there is no contention in terminology. And second, by way of concession, that what is meant is indication of two and above together with comprehensive inclusion, and it is not problematic due to annexed plurals like 'scholars of the city', for it is not comprehensive of all its individuals, but rather some of them, like the definite plural, due to the clear difference between specifying a particular universally, as in the annexed plural, where particularity is considered first by annexation, then its comprehensiveness over all its individuals is considered; and between specifying a universal particularly, as in the definite plural, where it is considered universally but some of its individuals are intended, so there is no trace of comprehensiveness in it. So in 'al-Tahrir', he defined 'aam as that which indicates entities by virtue of a matter in which they share universally at once. So his saying 'universally' is a guard against the definite plural, for it indicates specific individuals, not universally. And the annexed plural is objected upon it, for its entities are comprehensively covered when qualified, not universally. And it is answered that the common matter is 'scholar of the city' and it is not qualified by anything, unlike the definite plural, for the common matter there is 'men' for example, and its qualification has been considered. And the truth is that there is no difference between the definite plural.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses a fourth objection to the definition of 'aam: that definite plurals (like 'al-rijal'—the men) and indefinite plurals (like 'rijalun'—men) also indicate two or more, thus they should be general. The answer has two levels: first, the author (مصنف) accepts the objection, stating that these plurals are indeed general according to his terminology, so there is no disagreement. Second, on a concessive level (تنزل), he refines the definition by adding the condition 'together with comprehensive inclusion' (معاً بالاستغراق). Under this, the definite plural (المعهود) is excluded because it indicates specific individuals without comprehensive scope—e.g., 'the men' may refer to a known group, not all men. Similarly, the indefinite plural (المنكر) when used in affirmation (اثبات) can be general if it covers the whole class, but if it is not comprehensive, it is not 'aam. The passage then discusses annexed plurals (الجموع المضافة) like 'scholars of the city'—these are first restricted by annexation, then consider comprehensive of that restricted group, so they are general within that context but not universally. The author from 'al-Tahrir' defined 'aam as indicating entities 'universally' (مطلقاً) to exclude definite plurals that point to a specific set. However, the annexed plural is problematic because it is comprehensive within a restriction, not universally. The author responds that the common matter (مشترك) in annexed plurals is not qualified—e.g., 'scholar of the city' is a single description—so it differs from the definite plural where the description is qualified by prior knowledge. Ultimately, the author (in 'Fawatih al-Rahmut') says the truth is that there is no real difference, implying the definition works for all valid plurals that are comprehensive. The commentary clarifies that the Hanafi position consistently requires الاستغراق (comprehensiveness) as a condition for 'aam, distinguishing it from terms that merely indicate multiple individuals without covering all.

Key Terms
الجمع المعهود (al-jam' al-ma'hood): A definite plural that refers to specific known individuals (e.g., 'the men' in a known context)
الجمع المنكر (al-jam' al-munkar): An indefinite plural (e.g., 'rijaalun' men) that can be general if used in affirmative context with comprehensive intention
الجمع المضاف (al-jam' al-mudaf): An annexed plural (e.g., 'scholars of the city') which first restricts by annexation then indicates all individuals within that restriction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the definite plural (معهود) like 'الرجال' not considered a general term ('aam) under the refined definition?
A) Because it is not a single word.
B) Because it does not indicate 'two and above'.
C) Because it indicates specific individuals without comprehensive inclusion of all men universally.
D) Because it is a metaphorical expression.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a term is 'aam only if it indicates comprehensively all individuals of a description; definite and indefinite plurals are not automatically 'aam unless they meet this condition.

Defending the Definition of 'Amm: Dual and Plural Objections
Defense of 'amm definition against dual/plural objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate objections to the Hanafi definition of 'amm by distinguishing between dual, indefinite plural, and definite plural forms, and apply the concept of istighraq (comprehensive inclusion) to resolve them.

Core Idea
‘Amm must indicate two or more with comprehensive inclusion; the dual is fixed at two, so it is not ‘amm.

In Hanafi usul, defining 'amm as 'a single word indicating two or more' raises objections: doesn't the dual (muthanna) also fit? What about definite and indefinite plurals? This passage from the commentary systematically addresses these objections, refining our understanding of 'amm by requiring comprehensive inclusion (istighraq) and distinguishing between types of plurals. You will learn to navigate these nuances and see why the definition stands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection from dual
The objector claims the definition 'single word indicating two or more' fits the dual form as much as it fits 'amm. Understand how the dual only indicates exactly two, not more.
The dual is a fixed number; 'amm allows for two OR more.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response via custom analogy
The author uses the example 'sell for two dirhams or more' to show that custom interprets such expressions as a choice between two and more, not as a single price. This demonstrates that the dual alone cannot cover the 'more' part.
The analogy highlights the difference between a fixed pair and a variable range.
3
Evaluate the necessity of the condition 'two'
Critics argue that removing 'two' from the definition would still cover all 'amm terms because every 'amm indicates more than two. The author counters that without 'two', the phrase 'and upward' would be meaningless, and the condition is needed to include the indefinite plural which starts from two.
The condition is not superfluous; it ensures the indefinite plural is included.
4
Distinguish between types of plurals
The passage divides plurals into indefinite (munkar), definite (ma'hud), and additive (mudaf). Indefinite plurals can be 'amm if comprehensive, definite plurals refer to a known set and are not 'amm, and additive plurals may be 'amm if the addition is comprehensive.
This classification is key to resolving objections.
5
Apply the concept of istighraq
The definition requires that 'amm indicates its referents with comprehensive inclusion (istighraq). Definite plurals like 'the scholars of the city' lack istighraq because they refer to a specific group. Additive plurals gain istighraq after the qualification, not before.
Istighraq separates true 'amm from merely plural forms.

Source Text

فإنه اعتبر الخصوصية أوّلاً بالإضافة، ثم اعتبر استغراقه لجميع أفراده وبين الإفراد للمطلق على الخصوص كما في الجمع المعهود، فإنه اعتبر مطلقاً، وأريد بعض مسمياته، فلا شائبة للاستغراق فيه فما في «التحرير») أنه عرف العام بما دل على مسميات باعتبار أمر اشتركت فيه مطلقاً ضربة فقوله مطلقاً، احتراز عن الجمع المعهود، فإنه يدل على أفراد مخصوصة لا مطلقاً، ويرد عليه الجمع المضاف، فإن مسمياته مستغرقة مقيداً لا مطلقاً، وأجيب بأن المشترك فيه عالم البلد ولم يقيد بشيء بخلاف المعهود، فإن المشترك فيه الرجال مثلاً، وقد اعتبر تقييده والحق أن لا فرق بين الجمع المعهود والجمع مضافاً لأن عالم البلد أيضاً معهود وإرادة عهد دون عهد، لا تدل عليه العبارة ساقط) لأن المضاف بالإضافة العهدية يلتزم خروجه، وبالإضافة الاستغراقية لا عهد فيه، بل اعتبر تقيد الجنس أولاً، ثم اعتبر عمومه واستغراقه لجميع الإفراد المقيد فتدبر .
They both rise only by addition like two dirhams; just as rising in both is valid in consideration of signification so that two things and an additional thing are understood from it, so too is rising of two dirhams valid in consideration of being a price, by making them with an extra dirham a price. So there is no difference between the two forms, but the truth is that the immediate understanding in custom from such an expression is the choice between selling for two dirhams and for what is above it, and likewise the immediate understanding in the definition is indication of two things or what is above them. So then the objection by the dual is established in its place, and you may formulate the answer that the meaning in the agency is permission to sell at a price of two dirhams and at a price of more, so it is apparent that a single sale cannot be with two prices, and the delegate is not except for one sale, so choice between sales is necessary, contrary to our case, for indication of two things and more is possible with one word. This is not free from some unrest, and it can be said that the immediate understanding of such expressions is one of them by custom, so in the case of agency the intent is choice between two sales with two prices, and in our case it is indication of two or more, but with one word, and substitution is with respect to times. So the meaning of 'general' is a single word that indicates two at times and more at others, and the dual does not indicate more at all. This too is insufficient, for it then applies to the indefinite plural and does not encompass the comprehensive general, for it indicates all and does not indicate two at all. Then it was said: there is no need) then (for the condition of) 'two', so correction is necessitated, rather it suffices to say: a single word that indicates more than two (for there is no general that does not indicate what is above (two). But it is said that if the condition of two things were omitted, the connection of 'and upward' would not be valid, so there is need for it, even if there is no need for it in the predicate and converse. Yes, if the expression were changed to another form, there would be no need for it, but specifying a way of conveying the intent is not obligatory upon the speaker; he may convey it in a longer or shorter way. This (I say: the indefinite plural according to him (may his soul be sanctified) is general, and he says: the minimum of the plural is two. Perhaps this is from the scribe's oversight, and the correct is two, so the condition of 'two' is to include the indefinite plural and thus the benefit appears. 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' says: The indefinite plural used for two, if it were general, it would be included in the definition, so the dual would not be excluded and the objection would remain against it; and if it were not general, included in it, then no benefit appears at all. So reflect. (And) a fourth objection was raised (with the definite and indefinite plural) for they indicate two and upward. (And it was answered) first by accepting it, for they are general according to him (may his soul be sanctified), and there is no dispute in terminology. And second, by way of concession, that the intended meaning is indication of two and upward together by comprehensive inclusion, and there is no difficulty with additive plurals like 'the scholars of the city' for it is not comprehensive of all its individuals, but rather some of them, like the definite plural, there being a clear difference between specification for the particular in an absolute manner as in the additive plural, where specification is considered first by addition, then its comprehensive inclusion of all its individuals is considered, and between specification for the absolute in a particular manner as in the definite plural, for it is considered absolute, and some of its denotations are intended, so there is no trace of comprehension in it. So what is in 'al-Tahrir' that he defined the general as what indicates denotations by virtue of something in which they share absolutely. So his word 'absolutely' is a precaution against the definite plural, for it indicates specific individuals, not absolutely. And the additive plural is objected to it, for its denotations are comprehensive of the qualified, not absolutely. And it was answered that the shared thing is 'the scholar of the city' and it was not qualified by anything, unlike the definite plural, for the shared thing therein e.g., 'men', and its qualification was considered. And the truth is that there is no difference between the definite plural and the additive plural, because 'scholar of the city' is also definite, and intending one definite without another is not indicated by the expression, (dropped) because the additive with the definitive addition necessitates its exclusion, and with the comprehensive addition there is no definiteness, rather the qualification of the genus is considered first, then its generality and comprehension of all the qualified individuals is considered. So reflect.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a detailed defense of the definition of 'amm (general term) against multiple objections concerning dual and plural forms. The author begins by addressing an objection based on the dual (muthanna) such as 'two dirhams'. The objector had claimed that the definition of 'amm as 'a single word indicating two things or more' is equivalent to the dual, which only indicates two. The response argues that customarily, phrases like 'sell for two dirhams or more' imply a choice between two dirhams and a higher price, so the dual is not synonymous with 'amm because 'amm can indicate either two or more through a single word, while the dual is fixed at two. The author then examines whether the condition 'two' in the definition is necessary. Some argue that removing it would still capture all generals because every general indicates more than two. However, the author counters that without 'two', the phrase 'and upward' (فصاعدا) would be meaningless, and the condition serves to include the indefinite plural (الجمع المنكر) which, according to the preferred view, starts from two. A further objection involves the definite and indefinite plurals (الجمع المعهود والمنكر). The response first accepts that these are considered general by the author's school, avoiding terminological quibbles. Another answer is that the definition requires comprehensive inclusion (الاستغراق) of two or more together, which definite plurals like 'the scholars of the city' do not fulfill because they refer to a specific set, not all individuals. Additive plurals (الجموع المضافة) are distinguished because the addition specifies the individuals first, then assumes comprehensiveness 'all qualified individuals', whereas definite plurals are absolute but intend a subset. The passage concludes that the truth is there is no difference between definite and additive plurals in terms of definiteness; both can be interpreted as general if the context allows comprehensive inclusion of qualified individuals. This is a sophisticated example of Hanafi usul analysis clarifying the boundaries of 'amm.

Key Terms
العموم (al-umum): A single word that indicates two or more, either individually or collectively, depending on context and conditions like istighraq (comprehensive inclusion).
المثنى (al-muthanna): The dual form, indicating exactly two, and not more, so it is not considered 'amm in the comprehensive sense.
الجمع المنكر (al-jam' al-munkar): The indefinite plural, which according to some Hanafi scholars can indicate two or more and thus may be included under 'amm if used for two.
الجمع المعهود (al-jam' al-ma'hud): The definite plural referring to a known specific group, not comprehensive of all individuals, so it is not 'amm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that the dual (مثنى) cannot be considered 'amm?
A) Because it indicates exactly two, and 'amm requires indication of more than two.
B) Because it indicates exactly two, and 'amm requires indication of two or more with the potential for more.
C) Because the dual is grammatically restricted.
D) Because the dual cannot be used in general commands.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'amm is robust against objections by requiring comprehensive inclusion (istighraq) and distinguishing between dual and plural forms based on their capacity to indicate two or more.

The Reality of Generality in Meaning
Presenting the ikhtilaf on generality in meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether generality (al-umum) is real in meaning and evaluate the philosophical arguments (dalil aqli) used to support it.

Core Idea
Generality is real in meaning because the nature (tabi'ah) encompasses all individuals.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute: Is generality (al-umum) an attribute of meanings or only of words? While all agree that wording is general by reality, opinions diverge on meaning. The Hanafi school, following Abu Zayd al-Dabusi and al-Jassas, holds that generality is real in meaning, supported by linguistic usage and philosophical reasoning. Understanding this dispute clarifies how general terms like 'believers' or 'rain' are interpreted in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on generality in meaning
The text lists three views: (1) real in meaning, (2) metaphorical, (3) not applicable at all. Note which one the author prefers and why.
The third view is dismissed as having no known credible proponent.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from linguistic usage
The text cites examples: 'rain is general over countries', 'sound is general over hearers', 'the universal is general over particulars'. These show that generality is attributed to meanings in everyday language.
This proof relies on customary usage (urf), a valid source in Hanafi usul.
3
Understand the philosophical objection
Opponents argue that generality requires one identical thing covering many, which is impossible for external entities like rain in different places.
This objection is based on the impossibility of inherence (hulul) of one accident in multiple loci.
4
Examine the Hanafi response: the nature (tabi'ah) argument
The author responds that it is the nature of rain or sound that is one and that nature includes in all individuals. Alternatively, generality in language does not require strict identity but mere partial coverage.
The nature (tabi'ah) is a mental concept, not an external entity, so it can be general.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If generality is real in meaning, then terms that refer to meanings (like 'human' as a universal) can be described as general. This affects how 'amm is identified in Quranic and hadith texts.
For example, the word 'al-mu'minun' (the believers) is general over all believers in meaning, which impacts the scope of rulings.

Issue: Generality is real in wording [of the general term] and it is attributed to it as real by agreement. And does it also attribute to the meaning? There is disagreement. Some said: Yes, it attributes to it like wording, and that is the preferred position according to the author, and it is apparent from the words of al-Qadi Imam Abu Zayd in al-Asrar, and upon it is based the words of Imam Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, may Allah have mercy on them. And some said: it attributes to meaning metaphorically, and this is the view of most of the usuliyyun, among them Imam Fakhr al-Islam, may Allah have mercy on him. And some said: it does not attribute to meaning either as a reality or metaphorically, and this is something for which no known proponent among those who are counted is known. For us, generality in language and custom is absolute inclusion, and it is conceivable in meaning as in wording, like the generality of rain for countries, sound for hearers, and the universal for particulars. For if it is said: generality is the inclusive of one thing, and inclusive of one thing is not in rain and sound, rather the individuals are partial, for the rain that is in a country is other than that in another country, and the heard quality for a person existing in the air confined in his hearing is not the same as that existing in the air in another's hearing. Thus they said. I say: the matter is not as you claimed. Rather, the nature of rain and sound, one by an indistinct unity, includes within the individuals existing in multiple places. And it was said in answer: In language, generality does not consider inclusion of one thing; rather mere inclusion in the manner of partiality suffices. Then the commentator of al-Mukhtasar indicated that linguistic usage is easy, for it is known by induction. The dispute is only about one thing relating to many: is it correct or not? That is, the relation of one thing to many is not conceivable in external entities by agreement, but only in mental meanings. For the intellect does not refuse to permit the relation of a mental meaning to many. The usuliyyun deny their existence, so they prevented the attribution of inclusion and generality to meanings, and they differed in its formulation. So some, he is the commentator of al-Sharh, interpreted the relation (to many) as inherence. So the conclusion is that the inherence of one thing in many is not conceivable in external entities, but only in mental meanings. And they gave as reason for its inconceivability externally that a single accident does not inhere in multiple places. It is replied to him that there is no difference then between external meaning and mental meaning, so his statement is not valid. And this does not apply if by external entities he meant persons and by mental meanings the natures taken from where they are. And he specifically singled out persons as external because the mutakallimun deny mental existence, so they have no mental persons. And the intended by mental meanings are meanings that are comprehended by the mind, not existing in it. I say: Also it is possible that the meaning is a substance like rain, so it does not qualify for inherence at all. So reflect. And some, he is Mirzajan, interpreted the relation to many as predication (haml wa sidq). And this is not conceivable in entities, i.e., external persons, for the truth of a single specific external thing on multiple matters is not permissible, contrary to a mental concept, for from it are universals that are true of many. Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses a well-known usul dispute: Is generality (العموم) a property that can be attributed to the meaning (المعنى) as well as to the wording (اللفظ)? The author begins by stating the agreed-upon point that generality is real in wording. The disagreement centers on whether it can also apply to meaning. Three positions are cited: (1) Generality is real in meaning as well, which is the author's preferred view, attributed to Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Abu Bakr al-Jassas, and others. (2) It applies to meaning only metaphorically, held by most usuliyyun including Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi). (3) It does not apply at all, but the author notes no known credible proponent. The author then provides evidence for the first view by appealing to linguistic and customary usage: one says rain is general over countries, sound over hearers, and a universal (kulli) over particulars. This shows generality is conceivable in meanings. An objection is raised: generality requires inclusion of one identical thing in many, but rain in different countries is not the same entity, nor is sound heard by different people identical. The author responds that it is the nature (طبيعة) of rain or sound that is one (by an indistinct unity) and that nature includes in all the individual instances. Alternatively, a linguistic answer is that generality in language does not require one entity covering many; mere partial coverage (شمول على طريقة التبعيض) suffices. The underlying philosophical issue is whether one thing can relate to many. This is not possible in external entities (اعيان خارجية) but only in mental meanings (معاني ذهنية). The mutakallimun deny mental existence, so they cannot accept that generality inheres in meanings. The author analyzes two interpretations: (a) Some interpret the relation as inherence (حلول) of an accident in multiple places, which is impossible externally. (b) Others like Mīrzājān interpret it as predication (حمل وصدق), i.e., one concept can be true of many. This is also impossible for external individuals but possible for mental concepts. The author concludes that since external entities cannot have the attribute of generality in the sense of one thing covering many, but mental meanings can, it is valid to say meanings have generality. This is a classic Hanafi usul stance that, unlike the mutakallimun, allows for mental reality and thus attributes generality to meanings as real.

Key Terms
العموم (al-umum): Absolute inclusion (الشمول المطلق), which is attributable to both wording and meaning, meaning that something encompasses multiple items.
المعنى الذهني (al-ma'na al-dhihni): A mental meaning or concept that exists in the mind, as opposed to external entities. It can have attributes like generality because the mind can conceive of one concept applying to many.
الحلول (al-hulul): Inherence, i.e., the existence of one entity (like an accident) in multiple places. This is deemed impossible externally but possible in mental meanings.
الطبيعة (al-tabi'ah): The nature or essence of a thing, considered as one entity that includes in all its individuals, used to defend the attribution of generality to meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view in this passage, how is generality (al-umum) attributable to meanings like rain or sound?
A) By inherence of an accident in multiple loci.
B) Through the nature (tabi'ah) that is one and includes in individuals.
C) Only metaphorically, since meanings cannot be general.
D) By linguistic convention, not reality.

Key Takeaway
Generality (al-umum) is real in meaning because the one nature (tabi'ah) encompasses all individuals, a view grounded in Hanafi acceptance of mental existence.

Is 'Umum a Reality in Meanings? A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Analyzing three opinions on whether 'umum applies to meanings as a reality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions on whether 'umum is real in meanings, explain the arguments for and against, and locate the dispute within the broader Hanafi position on mental universals.

Core Idea
'Umum is haqiqah in meanings because absolute inclusion applies to natures, not just words.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut dives into a foundational usul al-fiqh debate: Is generality ('umum) a real property of the meanings themselves, or only of the words that express them? While all agree that 'umum is real in words, the status of meanings is contested. Three views emerge, and the author argues for the Hanafi position that meanings can indeed be general in reality—a claim rooted in the nature of universal concepts, not just verbal convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
List the three views presented: (1) 'umum is haqiqah in meanings as in words (author's chosen view); (2) 'umum is only metaphorical (majaz) in meanings (majority including Fakhr al-Islam); (3) 'umum is neither haqiqah nor majaz in meanings (unknown minority).
The first opinion is attributed to Abu Zayd and al-Jassas; the second to most usuliyun.
2
Analyze the definition of 'umum
The author defines 'umum as 'absolute inclusion' (mutlaq al-shumul). This definition is intentionally broad, applicable to both words and meanings. The key is that inclusion does not require personal unity (wahdah shakhsiyyah); partial inclusion suffices.
This definition counters the objection that 'umum requires one single thing to include multiple distinct individuals.
3
Examine the main objection
Opponents argue that 'umum requires the inclusion of one single thing (shumul amr wahid). In external entities like rain or sound, the rain in one place is different from rain elsewhere, so there is no single thing. Thus, 'umum cannot be real in meanings of external realities.
This objection relies on metaphysical assumptions about the unity of individuals.
4
Unpack the Hanafi response
The author counters that the nature (tabi'ah) of rain is one by vague unity (al-wahdah al-mubhamah) and manifests in multiple individuals. Inclusion of this nature suffices. Alternatively, partial inclusion (tab'id) is enough. The dispute then turns on the existence of mental entities and universals.
The author also invokes predication (haml) with negative predicates (ma'dulat) to show that universality does not require physical existence.
5
Connect to the deeper metaphysical dispute
The real issue is the existence of universal natures (kulliyat tabi'iyyah). The Hanafi school, following philosophical realism, affirms such natures in a qualified sense. The opposing mutakallimun deny mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), so they reject generality in meanings. Understanding this background clarifies the usul positions.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is whether takhsis (specification) applies to meanings or only to words. The Hanafi view allows specification of the meaning itself.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
والجمع مضافاً لأن عالم البلد أيضاً معهود وإرادة عهد دون عهد، لا تدل عليه العبارة ساقط) لأن المضاف بالإضافة العهدية يلتزم خروجه، وبالإضافة الاستغراقية لا عهد فيه، بل اعتبر تقيد الجنس أولاً، ثم اعتبر عمومه واستغراقه لجميع الإفراد المقيد فتدبر .

الأمر كما زعمت (بل (الطبيعة) من المطر والصوت الواحدة بالوحدة المبهمة (تعم في ضمن (الأفراد الموجودة في محال متعددة (وقيل) في الجواب (لم يعتبر في العموم لغة شمول أمر واحد بل يكفي الشمول على طريقة التبعيض ثم أفاد شارح المختصر أن الإطلاق اللغوي أمره سهل فإنه يعرف بالاستقراء (إنما النزاع في أمر واحد متعلق بمتعدد هل يصح أم لا (وذلك ) أي تعلق الأمر الواحد بمتعدد (لا يتصور في الأعيان الخارجية اتفاقاً و إنما يتصور في المعاني الذهنية) فإن العقل لا يأبى في تجويز تعلق معنى ذهني بمتعدد والأصوليون ينكرون وجودها فمنعوا اتصاف المعاني بالشمول والعموم واختلفوا في تقريره فحمل التعلق بعضهم) وهو شارح الشرح على الحلول فالحاصل أن حلول أمر واحد في متعدد لا يتصور في الأعيان الخارجية، وإنما يتصور في المعاني الذهنية (وعلل) عدم تصوره في الخارج بأن العرض الواحد لا يحل المحال المتعددة، ويرد عليه أنه لا فرق حينئذ بين المعنى الخارجي و المعنى (الذهني) فلا يصح قوله، وإنما يتصور في المعاني الذهنية، وهذا لا يرد لو أراد بالأعيان الخارجية الأشخاص وبالمعاني الذهنية الطبائع المأخوذة من حيث هي، وإنما خص الأشخاص بالخارجية لأن المتكلمين ينكرون الوجود الذهني، فلا أشخاص ذهنية عندهم والمراد بالمعاني الذهنية المعاني المعقولة للذهن لا الموجودة فيه (أقول: وأيضاً يجوز أن يكون المعنى (جوهراً كالمطر) فلا يتصف بالحلول أصلاً فتأمل، وحمل بعضهم وهو ميرزاجان التعلق بالمتعدد على الحمل والصدق) وذا لا يتصور في الأعيان أي الأشخاص الخارجية فإن صدق أمر واحد شخصي (خارجي على أمور لا يجوز بخلاف معقول ذهني إذ منه الكليات الصادقة على الكثير، والأصوليون ينكرون
المعقول الذهني لعدم قولهم بالوجود الذهني وبوجود الكليات في الأعيان (أقول: يرد عليه أن الصدق والحمل (لا يقتضي الوجود بل تكفي المعقولية كما في المعدولات) فإن المحمول فيها عدمي غير موجود مع كونه محمولاً على الموضوع والأصوليون لا ينكرون ذلك) الحمل، وكيف ينكر عاقل هذا ثم أقول : الصواب حمل التعلق على الوجود، والمعنى أن لا شمول للمعدوم إلا مجازاً فإن الشمول وجود أمر في متعدد (ووجود أمر في موجود متعدد لا يتصور في الأشخاص الخارجية فحمل الأعيان على الأشخاص وذلك ظاهر (وإنما يتصور في المعقولات الذهنية إذ منها الكليات الطبيعية التي قيل بوجودها فيتصور الشمول فيها للمتعدد (وجمهور الأصوليين ينكرون وجود الطبائع في الخارج على ما علم في مبحث الأمر) من «المختصر، فمنعوا شمول أمر واحد لمتعدد في المعاني (هذا) وعلى هذا فيرجع حقيقة النزاع إلى النزاع في وجود الطبائع، واعلم أنه لا شك أن العموم على هذا التقدير بمعنى وجود أمر واحد في متعدد، ولا شك أن الألفاظ لا تتصف به بهذا المعنى فلا يصلح تحرير النزاع المذكور آنفاً، فإن الكلام في العموم الذي يتصف به اللفظ اتفاقاً هل يتصف به المعنى؟ ولو أريد أن الأحرى بالنزاع هذا فهو كما ترى، إذ لا غرض للأصولي يعتد به، ثم إن الاستغراق حقيقة ليس إلا للمعنى، فإنه هو المنطبق على الأفراد واللفظ ليس مستغرقاً إلا باعتبار الدلالة على الإفراد فحينئذ لا يصح القول باتصاف المعنى بما يتصف به اللفظ حقيقة، ولا يصح التنازع أصلاً، اللهم إلا في اللفظ وما في «التحرير» أن مبنى النزاع أن من اعتبر في العموم الوحدة الشخصية كفخر الإسلام منع اتصاف المعنى به حقيقة، فإن الواحد الشخصي لا يتناول الكثير، وإنما يتصور في الذهني وهو ينكره، نعم يجوز مجازاً، ومن منع مجازاً أيضاً زعم أن لا علاقة، ومن لم يشترط الوحدة الشخصية جوّز اتصاف المعاني به وهو الحق، يقال: مطر عام فلا يلتفت إليه لأنه مع أنه لا أثر له في كلام الإمام فخر الإسلام أصلاً، وأن الواحد الشخصي ذهنياً أو عينياً لا يتصور تناوله للكثير لا يلزم منه الاتصاف بما يتصف به اللفظ وعباراتهم تدل عليه وحرر النزاع بعض من تعمق نظره أن المراد بالعموم الاستغراق لإفراد المفهوم الصالح الجريان الأحكام من التخصيص والتأويل هل يتصف به المعنى، فيخص بمخصص كاللفظ أم لا يتصف؟ كما يقال : الثابت اقتضاء هل له عموم أم لا؟ وإلى الثاني ذهب الإمامان الشيخ فخر الإسلام والشيخ شمس الأئمة الحلواني رحمهما الله تعالى حاكمين بأن التصرفات والتجوزات إنما تكون في الألفاظ دون المعاني، فإنها ما لم تعبر بألفاظ لا يتصرف فيها بزيادة أو نقصان، ويطلق عليه العموم مجازاً بأن يراد مطلق الاستغراق والشمول لا ما ذكر، كما يقال المأكول في لا آكل عام والعموم حقيقة هو ما ذكر، وبعضهم ذهبوا إلى الأول، ونسبه بعض مشايخنا إلى الشافعي رحمه الله حتى جوزوا التخصيص في الثابت ومن أنكر الاتصاف به حقيقة ومجازاً، فهو ممن زعماً منهم بعدم العلاقة، وقد وقع ههنا في «التحرير» من الكلام ما يقضى منه العجب، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام .
The third article on linguistic principles. ... Question: Generality (umum) is a reality in the word (lafz), and it is attributed to it as a reality by agreement. But is it attributed to meaning (ma'na)? There is disagreement. It is said: Yes, it is attributed to it like the word, and this is the chosen view according to the author, and it is apparent from the statement of Qadi Imam Abu Zaid in al-Asrar, and upon it is based the statement of Imam Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi (may Allah have mercy on them). And it is said: It is attributed to meaning metaphorically (majazan), and upon this are the majority of usuliyun, including Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him). And it is said: It is not attributed to meaning either as reality or metaphorically, and this is from one whose opinion is not considered among those counted. For us, generality linguistically and customarily is absolute inclusion (mutlaq al-shumul), and this is intelligible in meaning as it is in the word, like the generality of rain for lands, sound for hearers, and the universal for particulars. If it is said: Generality is the inclusion of one single thing, and the inclusion of one single thing is not in rain and sound, rather the individuals are partial... I say: The matter is not as you claimed, rather the nature (tabi'ah) of rain and sound, one by vague unity, is inclusive within the individuals existing in multiple places. And it is said in reply: In language, inclusion of one single thing is not considered necessary; inclusion by way of partiality is sufficient... Then the commentator of al-Mukhtasar said: The linguistic definition is easy because it is known by induction. The dispute is about one thing being related to multiple things: is it valid or not? That is, the relation of one thing to multiple things is not conceivable in external entities by agreement, but is conceivable in mental meanings... and the usuliyun deny their existence, so they prevent meanings from being described with inclusion and generality... The reality of the dispute goes back to the dispute about the existence of natures (taba'i')... Then it is said that istighraq (exhaustive inclusion) in reality is only for meaning, because it is what applies to individuals, and the word is not inclusive except by virtue of indicating individuals... Thus, the basis of the dispute is that whoever considers personal unity (al-wahdah al-shakhsiyyah) in generality, like Fakhr al-Islam, prevents meaning from being described with it as reality... And whoever does not require personal unity allows it, and it is the truth... And some deep-looking scholars have refined the dispute: the intended meaning of generality is the exhaustiveness of the individuals of the concept that is subject to rulings of specification and interpretation, whether it is attributed to meaning so that it is specified by a specifier like the word, or not...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut is a usul dispute on whether 'umum (generality) is a real property (haqiqah) of meanings (ma'ani) or only of words (alfaz). The author begins by stating the agreed point: 'umum is a reality in words. Then he presents three opinions on whether it is also a reality in meanings: (1) Yes, it is a reality—this is the author's chosen view, attributed to Abu Zayd and al-Jassas. (2) It is only metaphorical (majaz)—held by most usuliyun including Fakhr al-Islam. (3) It is neither—an unknown minority. The author argues that the linguistic definition of 'umum as absolute inclusion (mutlaq al-shumul) is equally applicable to meanings, as in 'general rain' or 'universal'. The objection that 'umum requires a single thing's inclusion, which is not found in rain (since rain in different places is different), is countered by saying that the nature (tabi'ah) of rain is one vague unity that includes all individual instances. The text then delves into deeper metaphysical issues: whether a single mental meaning can relate to multiple external entities. Usuliyun (like Fakhr al-Islam) who deny the existence of mental entities (al-wujud al-dhihni) argue that 'umum cannot be real in meanings. The author refutes this by showing that even if mental entities are denied, the logical relation of predication (haml) does not require existence, as seen in negative predicates (ma'dulat). Ultimately, the dispute reduces to the existence of universal natures (kulliyat tabi'iyyah). The author sides with those who allow 'umum in meanings without requiring personal unity. The passage ends by noting that the depth of the dispute lies in whether the meaning is subject to specification like words. This is a classic Hanafi usul position that considers 'umum in meanings as real, in contrast to some Shafi'i views that restrict it to words.

Key Terms
العموم (al-'umum): Absolute inclusion (mutlaq al-shumul) — the property of a thing that encompasses multiple individuals.
اللفظ (al-lafz): The spoken or written word; the verbal expression.
المعنى (al-ma'na): The mental meaning or concept that is understood from the word.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Exhaustive inclusion of all individuals of a concept.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Real, literal, non-metaphorical usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which view does the author (the commentator) prefer regarding whether 'umum (generality) is a reality (haqiqah) in meanings?
A) It is not a reality in meanings, only metaphorical.
B) It is a reality in meanings just as it is in words.
C) It is neither a reality nor a metaphor in meanings.
D) It is a reality only for universals, not for particulars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that 'umum is real in meanings because absolute inclusion applies to natures, and this affects how takhsis operates in legal reasoning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
