6
مع
أنه
Issue: (It is permissible to act upon the general before searching for the specifier) and exhaustive investigation of it according to us (and al-Sayrafi, al-Baydawi, and al-Urmawi agree). And the author of al-Mahsul seems to approve. (And) Imam Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali and al-Amidi reported consensus on the prohibition of acting upon it before searching for the specifier. (But) the establishment of consensus is (denied) and the report is incorrect. (For) al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (narrated the disagreement. And with this is refuted what Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: the report of consensus is based on not counting the statement of al-Sayrafi, for it is obstinacy. Rather, al-Ustadh narrated agreement on clinging to it before searching for the specifier during his lifetime, peace be upon him and his Family and all his Companions and wives, as in al-Taysir. And the strongest proof that the report of consensus is incorrect is that Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, ruled with the blood money for fingers merely by knowing the book of 'Amr ibn Hazm, may Allah be pleased with him, and he left analogy and opinion, and did not search for a specifier nor ask about it. Likewise, the Mistress of Women, Fatima al-Zahra', may Allah be pleased with her, clung to what she thought was general in inheritance without searching or asking about a specifier. Then the specifier appeared like the sun at midday. In summary, it has not been transmitted from any of the Companions ever pausing from the general until searching for a specifier, nor any of them objecting in debates to someone who clung to the general before searching for the specifier. Likewise in the second and third centuries. And the Hanafis obligate acting upon it before searching, and this madhhab has settled until now. So where is the consensus? And there has preceded the transmission from al-Qadi Imam Abu Zayd that pausing is an innovation after the third century. And he also said: The summary of the answer is that the layman is obliged to act upon its generality, while the jurist is obliged to be cautious for himself, so he pauses for a moment to explore this possibility by examining similarities, while it is a proof for acting upon it if he acts. But he pauses out of caution so that he does not need to revoke what he has implemented if the disagreement becomes clear. However, the discussion is about the requirement of the text itself. As for caution, it is a specific mode whereby the original is abandoned, but abandoning it is not necessarily obligatory. And this statement indicates the permissibility of acting before searching. The author of Matla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah said: The best detail is that the Companions are permitted to act upon it before searching for the specifier, for it is not likely to be hidden from them if it existed. As for the layman, who may have it hidden from him, he must pause. As for the mujtahids who have a great share of knowledge, they are in the ruling of the Companions. And this contradicts what was transmitted from al-Qadi Imam. And it has passed that the definitive specifier was hidden from the Mistress of Women, may Allah be pleased with her, when she thought it was general and acted before searching for it. And there is no reason to pause after the establishment of a shar'i proof that necessitates a divine ruling except as a caution for a moment for one who has the rank of ijtihad and reflection. Perhaps for this reason he said after it: 'In it there is what is in it; for us, it is definitive in indication, so the ruling is derived from it definitively. So it is not paused after knowing the divine ruling established definitively due to the possibility of a contrary, which is a negligible possibility, just as there is no pause in other definitive matters due to the possibility of abrogation and interpretation. And this is very apparent. Then this proof is also valid on the view of probability, for it yields a strong probability of a divine ruling, so it must be acted upon without pausing because of a weak possibility, due to consensus on acting upon the preponderant. I admire the statement of those who pause: they made the general like the ambiguous, obligating pausing until the intended meaning appears—rather they made it a puzzle. How could they permit this statement while they rule that the forms are placed for generality individually? Is this not contradiction? So reflect. And those who paused were fair: (They said: The possibility of a specifier opposes its indication.) (We said:) The general is definitive, and there is no possibility of specification except rationally, like the possibility of metaphor in the specific. And rational possibility does not oppose established indication, so it does not negate authority. So understand. And if we grant probability, then the possibility of a specifier is a weak possibility, so it does not oppose the preponderant established generality, and there is no pause without opposition. So understand. Then those who prohibit acting before searching differed regarding the extent of searching. The majority, including Ibn Shurayh, said: Searching until probability of non-existence is required, because induction only yields probability, and searching is by induction. (So requiring certainty closes the door of acting upon the general.) And al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (and a group) said: Searching until certainty of non-existence is required. (They said: When the mujtahid's search increases and he does not find, custom dictates certainty of the specifier's non-existence. (We said:) Custom's dictate of certainty is denied; rather, custom only dictates probability, even if strong, as in al-Mahsul, by considering the probability weak. (I say: If they had said: the mujtahid's probable is certain, because his probability is necessarily binding as mentioned in the introduction, the dispute would be verbal. For the one who suffices with probability meant probability of the specifier's non-existence itself, and this does not contradict what was mentioned; rather, it only yields certainty of the obligation to act upon its implication, and that is not denied by anyone. (Then I say:) In establishing certainty: (When non-existence of the specifier becomes probable for the mujtahid due to not finding after intense induction, then the general becomes definitive like the specific, because of its possibility of being metaphorical—a weak, negligible possibility not arising from evidence—by agreement. And here also, the non-existence of the specifier became weak and negligible due to the absence of evidence for it, rather evidence for its non-existence customarily and linguistically. And the specific is definitive in the broader sense, so this general is also definitive. And certainty with it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi argument on the usul issue of acting upon a general text ('amm) before searching for a specifier (mukhassis). The author opens by stating the Hanafi position: it is permissible to act upon the general without prior search and exhaustive investigation. He names al-Sayrafi, al-Baydawi, and al-Urmawi as agreeing, and notes that the author of al-Mahsul (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) seems to approve. Then he addresses the claim of consensus (ijma') reported by al-Ghazali and al-Amudi that acting before search is prohibited. The author denies this consensus, citing that other scholars like Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi recorded disagreement. He further supports the Hanafi position with examples from the Sahaba: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab applied the general ruling from the book of 'Amr ibn Hazm regarding blood money for fingers without seeking a specifier, and Fatima al-Zahra' acted on her understanding of a general text in inheritance law, not realizing there was a specifier until it became clear. The author argues that no Sahaba is reported to have paused from a general text to search for a specifier, nor did they object to others doing so. This practice continued in the second and third centuries. The Hanafi madhhab requires acting before search, and this has been settled. He quotes al-Qadi Imam Abu Zayd that pausing is an innovation after the third century. The author distinguishes between the layman, who must act on the general as is, and the jurist, who may pause momentarily out of caution to consider potential specifiers, but this caution is not obligatory. He then addresses the nature of the general's indication: it is definitive (qat'i) in the Hanafi view because the forms of generality are placed for that meaning. The possibility of specification is only rational, similar to the possibility of metaphor in a specific text, and such rational possibilities do not negate authority. Even if the general were considered merely probable (zanni), its probability is strong and overrides the weak possibility of a specifier, so acting without pause is required by consensus on following the preponderant. Finally, the passage discusses disagreement among those who prohibit acting before search regarding the extent of search. Some say search until probability of non-specifier, others require certainty. The author leans toward the sufficiency of strong probability, noting that if the mujtahid searches intensely and finds nothing, custom yields probability of non-existence, which becomes practically certain due to the obligation to act upon it. The dispute becomes verbal, as the outcome is the same: the general is treated as definitive in its implication.

Key Terms
عام ('amm): اللفظ المستغرق لجميع أفراد ما يصلح له بحسب الوضع
مخصص (mukhassis): دليل يخرج بعض أفراد العام عن حكمه
قطعي (qat'i): ما لا يحتمل النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited in the passage for the Hanafi position?
A) A hadith from the Prophet
B) The practice of 'Umar and Fatima
C) A verse from the Quran
D) Logical argument

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits and requires acting upon a general text before searching for a specifier, based on Sahaba practice and the absence of reported pause.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Epistemic Strength of the General and the Extent of Search
Debating the epistemic status of general texts and the extent of search required

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that the general is qat'i in indication, and evaluate the different opinions on the extent of search required by those who prohibit acting before search.

Core Idea
The general text is definitive in meaning; the mere rational possibility of a specifier does not suspend its authority.

Having established the permissibility of acting on the general before search, the passage now delves into the epistemological debate. Is the general qat'i or zanni? How much search is required before one can safely act? These questions are crucial for understanding the binding force of general texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafi claim of qat'i indication
The author asserts that the general text is qat'i (definitive) in its indication, based on the linguistic placement of general forms.
The general is like the specific in being definitive.
2
Examine the response to the objection
The author responds to the objection that the possibility of a specifier undermines certainty. He argues that this possibility is merely rational and does not oppose linguistic indication.
Analogous to the possibility of metaphor in a specific text.
3
Compare the layman and the jurist
The layman is obliged to act on the general as is; the jurist may pause for caution (but it is not obligatory) to consider potential specifiers.
This caution does not negate the general's authority.
4
Analyze the disagreement on search extent
Opponents differ: some require search until probability, others until certainty. The author criticizes requiring certainty as closing the door to action.
The majority require search until probability of non-existence.
5
Conclude that the dispute is largely verbal
Both sides end up acting on the preponderant, so the disagreement is primarily about terminology.
The mufta bihi is to act after strong probability.

(يجوز العمل بالعام قبل البحث عن المخصص) واستقصاء تفتيشه عندنا (وعليه الصيرفي والبيضاوي والأرموي) ويلوح آثار رضا صاحب المحصول (ونقل) الإمام حجة الإسلام (الغزالي والآمدي الإجماع على المنع من العمل به قبل البحث عن المخصص (وهو) أي ثبوت الإجماع (ممنوع) والنقل غير مطابق ( فإن الأستاذ أبا إسحاق الإسفراييني وأبا إسحق الشيرازي والإمام فخر الدين الرازي (حكوا (الخلاف و به اندفع ما قال الشيخ ابن الهمام نقل الإجماع مبني على عدم اعتداد قول الصيرفي فإنه مكابرة بل الأستاذ حكى الاتفاق على التمسك به قبل البحث عن المخصص في حياته صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه أجمعين (وسلم كما في التيسير) وأدل الدليل على أن نقل الإجماع غير مطابق أن أمير المؤمنين عمر رضي الله عنه حكم بالدية في الأصابع بمجرد العلم بكتاب عمرو بن حزم رضي الله عنه وترك القياس والرأي، ولم يبحث عن المخصص ولم يسأل عنه، وكذا سيدة النساء فاطمة الزهراء رضي عنها تمسكت بما ظنته عاماً في الميراث مع عدم البحث والسؤال عن المخصص، ثم ظهر المخصص ظهور الشمس على نصف النهار وبالجملة لم ينقل عن واحد من الصحابة قط التوقف في العام إلى البحث عن المخصص ولا إنكار واحد منهم في المناظرات على من تمسك بالعام قبل البحث عن المخصص، وكذا في القرن الثاني والثالث، والحنفية يوجبون العمل به قبل البحث، واستقر هذا المذهب إلى الآن فأين الإجماع، وقد تقدم النقل عن القاضي الإمام أبي زيد من أن التوقف مبتدع بعد القرن الثالث، وقال أن التوقف مبتدع بعد القرن الثالث، وقال هو أيضاً، وجملة الجواب أن العامي يلزمه العمل بعمومه كما وأما الفقيه فيلزمه أن يحتاط لنفسه، فيقف ساعة

(But) the establishment of consensus is (denied) and the report is incorrect. (For) al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (narrated the disagreement. And with this is refuted what Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: the report of consensus is based on not counting the statement of al-Sayrafi, for it is obstinacy. Rather, al-Ustadh narrated agreement on clinging to it before searching for the specifier during his lifetime, peace be upon him and his Family and all his Companions and wives, as in al-Taysir. And the strongest proof that the report of consensus is incorrect is that Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, ruled with the blood money for fingers merely by knowing the book of 'Amr ibn Hazm, may Allah be pleased with him, and he left analogy and opinion, and did not search for a specifier nor ask about it. Likewise, the Mistress of Women, Fatima al-Zahra', may Allah be pleased with her, clung to what she thought was general in inheritance without searching or asking about a specifier. Then the specifier appeared like the sun at midday. In summary, it has not been transmitted from any of the Companions ever pausing from the general until searching for a specifier, nor any of them objecting in debates to someone who clung to the general before searching for the specifier. Likewise in the second and third centuries. And the Hanafis obligate acting upon it before searching, and this madhhab has settled until now. So where is the consensus? And there has preceded the transmission from al-Qadi Imam Abu Zayd that pausing is an innovation after the third century. And he also said: The summary of the answer is that the layman is obliged to act upon its generality, while the jurist is obliged to be cautious for himself, so he pauses for a moment to explore this possibility by examining similarities, while it is a proof for acting upon it if he acts. But he pauses out of caution so that he does not need to revoke what he has implemented if the disagreement becomes clear. However, the discussion is about the requirement of the text itself. As for caution, it is a specific mode whereby the original is abandoned, but abandoning it is not necessarily obligatory. And this statement indicates the permissibility of acting before searching. The author of Matla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah said: The best detail is that the Companions are permitted to act upon it before searching for the specifier, for it is not likely to be hidden from them if it existed. As for the layman, who may have it hidden from him, he must pause. As for the mujtahids who have a great share of knowledge, they are in the ruling of the Companions. And this contradicts what was transmitted from al-Qadi Imam. And it has passed that the definitive specifier was hidden from the Mistress of Women, may Allah be pleased with her, when she thought it was general and acted before searching for it. And there is no reason to pause after the establishment of a shar'i proof that necessitates a divine ruling except as a caution for a moment for one who has the rank of ijtihad and reflection. Perhaps for this reason he said after it: 'In it there is what is in it; for us, it is definitive in indication, so the ruling is derived from it definitively. So it is not paused after knowing the divine ruling established definitively due to the possibility of a contrary, which is a negligible possibility, just as there is no pause in other definitive matters due to the possibility of abrogation and interpretation. And this is very apparent. Then this proof is also valid on the view of probability, for it yields a strong probability of a divine ruling, so it must be acted upon without pausing because of a weak possibility, due to consensus on acting upon the preponderant. I admire the statement of those who pause: they made the general like the ambiguous, obligating pausing until the intended meaning appears—rather they made it a puzzle. How could they permit this statement while they rule that the forms are placed for generality individually? Is this not contradiction? So reflect. And those who paused were fair: (They said: The possibility of a specifier opposes its indication.) (We said:) The general is definitive, and there is no possibility of specification except rationally, like the possibility of metaphor in the specific. And rational possibility does not oppose established indication, so it does not negate authority. So understand. And if we grant probability, then the possibility of a specifier is a weak possibility, so it does not oppose the preponderant established generality, and there is no pause without opposition. So understand. Then those who prohibit acting before searching differed regarding the extent of searching. The majority, including Ibn Shurayh, said: Searching until probability of non-existence is required, because induction only yields probability, and searching is by induction. (So requiring certainty closes the door of acting upon the general.) And al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (and a group) said: Searching until certainty of non-existence is required. (They said: When the mujtahid's search increases and he does not find, custom dictates certainty of the specifier's non-existence. (We said:) Custom's dictate of certainty is denied; rather, custom only dictates probability, even if strong, as in al-Mahsul, by considering the probability weak. (I say: If they had said: the mujtahid's probable is certain, because his probability is necessarily binding as mentioned in the introduction, the dispute would be verbal. For the one who suffices with probability meant probability of the specifier's non-existence itself, and this does not contradict what was mentioned; rather, it only yields certainty of the obligation to act upon its implication, and that is not denied by anyone. (Then I say:) In establishing certainty: (When non-existence of the specifier becomes probable for the mujtahid due to not finding after intense induction, then the general becomes definitive like the specific, because of its possibility of being metaphorical—a weak, negligible possibility not arising from evidence—by agreement. And here also, the non-existence of the specifier became weak and negligible due to the absence of evidence for it, rather evidence for its non-existence customarily and linguistically. And the specific is definitive in the broader sense, so this general is also definitive. And certainty with it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi argument on the usul issue of acting upon a general text ('amm) before searching for a specifier (mukhassis). The author opens by stating the Hanafi position: it is permissible to act upon the general without prior search and exhaustive investigation. He names al-Sayrafi, al-Baydawi, and al-Urmawi as agreeing, and notes that the author of al-Mahsul (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) seems to approve. Then he addresses the claim of consensus (ijma') reported by al-Ghazali and al-Amudi that acting before search is prohibited. The author denies this consensus, citing that other scholars like Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi recorded disagreement. He further supports the Hanafi position with examples from the Sahaba: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab applied the general ruling from the book of 'Amr ibn Hazm regarding blood money for fingers without seeking a specifier, and Fatima al-Zahra' acted on her understanding of a general text in inheritance law, not realizing there was a specifier until it became clear. The author argues that no Sahaba is reported to have paused from a general text to search for a specifier, nor did they object to others doing so. This practice continued in the second and third centuries. The Hanafi madhhab requires acting before search, and this has been settled. He quotes al-Qadi Imam Abu Zayd that pausing is an innovation after the third century. The author distinguishes between the layman, who must act on the general as is, and the jurist, who may pause momentarily out of caution to consider potential specifiers, but this caution is not obligatory. He then addresses the nature of the general's indication: it is definitive (qat'i) in the Hanafi view because the forms of generality are placed for that meaning. The possibility of specification is only rational, similar to the possibility of metaphor in a specific text, and such rational possibilities do not negate authority. Even if the general were considered merely probable (zanni), its probability is strong and overrides the weak possibility of a specifier, so acting without pause is required by consensus on following the preponderant. Finally, the passage discusses disagreement among those who prohibit acting before search regarding the extent of search. Some say search until probability of non-specifier, others require certainty. The author leans toward the sufficiency of strong probability, noting that if the mujtahid searches intensely and finds nothing, custom yields probability of non-existence, which becomes practically certain due to the obligation to act upon it. The dispute becomes verbal, as the outcome is the same: the general is treated as definitive in its implication.

Key Terms
عام ('amm): اللفظ المستغرق لجميع أفراد ما يصلح له بحسب الوضع
مخصص (mukhassis): دليل يخرج بعض أفراد العام عن حكمه
قطعي (qat'i): ما لا يحتمل النقيض
ظني (zanni): ما يحتمل النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in the passage, why does the possibility of a specifier not undermine the definitiveness of a general text?
A) Because specifiers are rare.
B) Because the possibility is rational, not based on evidence.
C) Because the general text is from the Quran.
D) Because all scholars agree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a general text is qat'i in its indication; the mere rational possibility of a specifier does not affect its binding force, so one may act upon it immediately without searching for exceptions.
